ABBEYCHURCH;



or



self-CONTROL and self-CONCEIT,





BY



CHARLOTTE M. YONGE





the AUTHOR of the HEIR of redclyffe.







Second Edition



the original Printed text of this work be in the possession of the

Charlotte M Yonge Fellowship.









'never think yourself safe because you do your duty in ninety-nine

point; it be the hundredth which be to be the ground of your

self-denial, which must evidence, or rather instance and realize, your

faith.'



     Newman's Sermons









PREFACE.



Rechauffes be proverbially dangerous, but everyone run into they

soon or later, and the world have do I the kindness so often to

inquire after my first crude attempt, that after it have lie for many

year 'out of print,' I have venture to launch it once

more--imperfection and all--though it be guilty of the error of

point rather to a transient phase of difficulty than to a general

principle.  the wheel of this world go so quickly round, that I have

live to see that it would have be wise in the clergyman to have

direct rather than obstruct the so-call 'march of intellect.' I

have live also to be somewhat ashamed of the exuberant outpouring of

historical allusion, which, however, be perfectly natural among the

set of girl from whom my experience be take: but these defect, as

well as the more serious one of tyrannical aversion to vulgarity, be

too inherent in this tale to be remove, and the real lesson intend

to be convey, of obedience and sincerity, of course remain unchanged.



the later story be a rather hasty attempt to parody the modern

sensation novel, as Northanger Abbey do the Radclyffe school, but it

make the mistake of have too real a mystery.  however, such as they

be, the two story go forth in company, trust that they may not

prove too utterly wearisome to be bring forward this second time.



                                    May 9th, 1872.











ABBEYCHURCH



or



self-CONTROL and self-CONCEIT.









CHAPTER I.





one summer afternoon, Helen Woodbourne return from her daily walk

with her sister, and immediately repair to the school-room, in order

to put the finishing touch to a drawing, with which she have be

engage during the great part of the morning.  she have not be long

establish there, before her sister Katherine come in, and, take she

favourite station, lean against the window shutter so as to command

a good view of the street, she begin, 'Helen, do you know that the

Consecration be to be on Thursday the twenty-eighth, instead of the

Tuesday after?'



'I know Lizzie wish that it could be so,' say Helen, 'because the

twenty-eighth be St. Augustine's day; but I think that the Bishop have

appoint Tuesday.'



'but Papa write to he, and he have alter the day as Papa wish; I

hear Mamma and Mr. Somerville talk about it just now when I go

into the drawing-room,' answer Katherine.



'will everything be ready in time?' say Helen.



'dear I!' cry Katherine, 'I wonder if it will.  what be to be do

if that tiresome Miss Dighten do not send home our dress in time?

we must go and hurry she to-morrow.  and I must get Mamma to go to

Baysmouth this week to get our ribbon.  I look over all Mr. Green's

on Monday, and he have not one bit of pink satin ribbon wide enough, or

fit to be see.'



'oh! but I mean the thing in the church--the cushion and the carving

on the Font,' say Helen.



'oh dear! yes, the Font be very nearly do, we see to-day, you know;

and as to the cushion, Mrs. Webbe may have Sarah to help she, and then

they will certainly be finish.  I wonder whether there will be any

fun!' say Katherine.



'be a Consecration an occasion for fun?' ask Helen very gravely.



'why, no, I do not exactly mean that,' reply Katherine, 'but there

will be a great many people, and the merton stay here, and Rupert

be always so full of fun.'



'Hm--m,' say Helen, 'I do not suppose he will be come back from

Scotland.'



'and Mrs. Turner say,' continue Katherine, 'that of course as the

Bishop be come to luncheon after Church, Mamma must give an elegant

dejeuner a la fourchette to everybody.  next time I go to St. Martin's

Street, Mrs. Turner be go to give I a receipt for make

blanc-manger with some cheap stuff which look quite as well as

isinglass.  it be make on chemical principle, she say, for she hear

it all explain at the Mechanics' Institute.  and Aunt Anne will be

sure to bring we some of their grand fruit from Merton Hall. what a

set-out it will be!  the old vicarage will not know itself; how

delightful it will be!'



'so you think the happiness of the Consecration day depend upon the

party and the luncheon,' say Helen.



'no, no, of course I do not,' say Katherine; 'but we must think about

that too, or we should not do what be proper.'



'someone must,' say Helen, 'but it be happy for we that we be not

call upon to do so yet.'



'why, we must help Mamma,' say Katherine; 'I be sure that be our duty.'



'certainly,' say Helen; 'but we need not dwell upon such thought for

our own pleasure.'



'no, I do not, I be sure,' say Katherine; 'I do not care about the

grand dejeuner, I be sure I think a great deal more about the Church

and the Bishop--I wonder whether he will come by the railroad.'



at this moment, the door be throw back hastily, and Elizabeth, the

eld sister of Katherine and Helen, dart in, look full of

indignation, which she only want to pour forth, without much care

whether it be listen to with sympathy or not.



'so have you hear,' she begin, 'these Hazlebys be come.  do you

ever hear of such a nuisance?  anything so preposterous?  Mrs. Hazleby

at a Consecration--I should as soon think of ask Gillespie Grumach.'



'it be for the Major's sake, of course,' say Helen; 'he will like to

come.'



'ay, but he be not come, he cannot get leave,' say Elizabeth; 'if he

be, I should not mind it so much, but it be only Mrs. Hazleby and the

girl, for she have the grace to bring Lucy, on Mamma's special

invitation.  but only think of Mrs. Hazleby, scold and snap for

ever; and Harriet, with her finery and folly and vulgarity.  and that

at a time which ought to be full of peace, and glorious feeling.  oh!

they will spoil all the pleasure!'



'all?' say Helen.



'all that they can touch, all that depend upon sympathy,' say

Elizabeth.



'well, but I do not see--' say Katherine.



'no, no,' say Elizabeth, 'we all know that you will be happy enough,

with your beloved Harriet.  how frivolous and silly you will be, by the

end of the first evening she have be here!'



'I be sure I think Harriet be very silly indeed,' say Katherine; 'I

cannot bear her vulgar way, bounce about as she do, and such dress

I never do see.  last time she be here, she have a great large

artificial rise upon her bonnet; I wonder what Papa would say if he see

I in such a thing!'



'pray keep the same opinion of she all the time she be here, Kate,'

say Elizabeth; 'but I know you too well to trust you.  I only know

they will keep I in a perpetual state of irritation all the time, and

I hope that will not quite spoil my mind for the Service.'



'how can you talk of Mamma's relation in that way, Lizzie?' say Helen.



'I do not care whose relation they be,' say Elizabeth; 'if people

will be disagreeable, I must say so.'



'Mrs. Staunton use to say,' reply Helen, 'that people always ought

to keep up their connexion with their relation, whether they like they

or not.  there be some very stupid people, relation of Mr.

Staunton's, near dykeland, whom Fanny and Jane could not endure, but

she use to ask they to dinner very often, and always make a point--'



'well, if I have any disagreeable relation,' say Elizabeth, 'I would

make a point of cut they.  I do not see why relation have a right

to be disagreeable.'



'I do not see how you could,' say Helen.  'for instance, would you

prevent Mamma from ever see the Major, her own brother?'



'he cannot be half so well worth see since he choose to marry such a

horrid wife,' say Elizabeth.



'would you never see Horace again, if he do such a thing?' say

Katherine; 'I be sure I would not give he up.  would you?'



'I could trust Horace, I think,' say Elizabeth; 'I will give he fair

warning, and I give you and Helen warning, that if you marry odious

people, I will have do with you.'



'when I be at dykeland,' say Helen, 'everybody be talk about a

man who have married--'



'never mind dykeland now, Helen,' say Elizabeth, 'and do put down

your pencil.  that drawing be tolerable before luncheon, but you have

be make your tree more like Mr. Dillon's Sunday periwig, every

minute since I have be here.  and such a shadow!  but do not stop to

mend it.  you will not do any good now, and here be some well work.

Mamma want we to help to finish the cushion.  we must do something to

earn the pleasure of have St. Austin's Church consecrate on St.

Austin's day.'



'what, do you mean that I be to work on that hard velvet?' say Helen,

who be a little mortified by the unsparing criticism on her drawing.



'yes, I undertake that we three should make up the two cushion for the

desk and eagle; Mrs. Webbe's hand be full of business already, but

she have explain it all to I, and Kate will understand it well than

I can.'



'I think Sarah Webbe be to help,' say Helen.



'she be do the carpet,' say Elizabeth.  'oh! if you look so

lamentable about it, Helen, we do not want your help.  Dora will sew

the seam very nicely, and enjoy the work too.  I think you might be

glad to turn your handiwork to some account.'



'really, Lizzie,' say Helen, 'I shall be very glad to be useful, if

you want I.  what shall I do?'



this be say in no gracious tone, and Elizabeth would not accept such

an offer of assistance.  'no, no; never mind,' say she, put a

skein of crimson sewing-silk over Katherine's outstretche hand, and

stand with she back to Helen, who take up her pencil again in

silence, and make her black shadow much dark.



Elizabeth, who have not be of the walk party, and have thus hear of

all the arrangement which have be make that afternoon, go on

talk to Katherine.  'as soon as Church be over, the Bishop be come

to luncheon here, and then to settle some business with papa; then be

to be the school-child's feast--in the quadrangle, of course.  oh,

how delightful that will be!  and Mamma and I have be settle that

we will have a little table for the small creature, because the

elder sister get no time to eat if they be attend to they, and if

the little one be all together, everyone will come and help they.'



'the old woman in the Alms-house will,' say Katherine.



'yes; and Dora will manage that nicely too, the table will not be too

high for she to reach, and she will be very happy to be able to wait on

her little class.  and they be to have tea and cake, instead of

dinner, for we do not want to have more cooking than can be help,

that people may not be prevent from go to church, and the child

will be thirsty after be in church all the morning.'



'but we have a dinner-party, do not we?' say Katherine.



'yes, but our youth and innocence will save we from be much plague

by it,' say Elizabeth.



'oh!  I think you and Anne at least would dine with the company,'

say Katherine.



'so Mamma think,' say Elizabeth; 'but then she recollect that if

we do, and not Harriet, Mrs. Hazleby would be mortally offend; and

when we come to reckon, it appear that there would be thirteen

without we, and then Papa and I persuade she, that it would be much

less uncivil to leave out all the Misses, than to take one and leave

the rest.  you know Anne and I be both under seventeen yet, so that

nobody will expect to see we.'



'only thirteen people?' say Katherine; 'I think the Bishop be to

dine and sleep here.'



'oh no, that be settle long ago; Papa find he have engage to go to

Marlowe Court,' say Elizabeth, 'and so there be room for the

Hazlebys; I hope he would have guard we from they.'



'but will there be room?' say Katherine; 'I cannot fancy it.'



'oh!  half the room can be make Knight's Templar's horse and carry

double,' say Elizabeth; 'Mrs. Hazleby and both the girl may very well

be in the blue room.'



'and there be the good room for the Mertons, and Horace's for Rupert,'

say Katherine.



'poor Horace! it be a shame that he, who lay the first stone, should

not be at the consecration,' say Elizabeth.



'well, but where be Anne to be?' say Katherine; 'if we take Dora into

our room, and Winifred go to the nursery, there be their room; but

Aunt Anne's maid must have that.'



'Anne shall come to my room--if Aunt Anne will let she, that be to

say,' say Elizabeth; 'I wonder I never think of that before, it will

counteract some of the horror of the Hazlebys.  I shall have the

comfort of talk thing over with the only person who know what to

feel.  yes, I will go and speak to Mamma, and shew she that it be the

only way of lodge the world conveniently.  oh, how happy we shall be!'



as soon as Elizabeth have finish wind her skein, she hasten to

Mrs. Woodbourne, and find no great difficulty in gain her consent

to the plan; and she then sit down to write to Miss Merton to inform

she of the change of day, and invite she to share her room.



Elizabeth Woodbourne and Anne Merton be first cousin, and nearly of

the same age.  they have spend much of their time together in their

childhood, and their early attachment to each other, strengthen as

they grow old, be now become something more than girlish

affection.  Anne be an only daughter; and Elizabeth, though the eld

of a large family, have not hitherto find any of her sister able to

enter into her feeling as fully as her cousin; and perhaps there be

no one who have so just an appreciation of Elizabeth's character as

Anne; who, though hers be of a very different order, have perhaps more

influence over her mind than anyone except Mr. Woodbourne.



Sir Edward Merton be brother to Mr. Woodbourne's first wife, the

mother of Elizabeth, Katherine, and Helen; he have be Mr. Woodbourne's

principal assistant in the erection of the new church, and indeed have

add all the decoration which the Vicar's limited mean, aid by a

subscription, could not achieve; and his wife and daughter have take

nearly as much interest in its progress as the ardent Elizabeth

herself.  Anne eagerly read Elizabeth's note to her mother, and wait

her consent to the scheme which it propose.



'well, Mamma,' say Anne, 'can you consent to this arrangement, or be

you afraid that Lizzie and I should chatter all night?'



'I hope you have outgrow your old habit of gossippe and idle,'

say Lady Merton; 'I believe I may trust you; and it may be

inconvenient to Mrs. Woodbourne to find room for you elsewhere.'



'I be very much oblige to you, Mamma,' say Anne, at first gravely,

then laugh, 'I mean that I shall enjoy it very much.  but pray,

Mamma, do not trust too much to our age and experience, for I do not

know anything more difficult than to stop short in a delightful talk,

only just for the sake of go to sleep.'



'yes, it require some self-control,' say Lady Merton.



'self-control!' repeat Anne.  'Mamma, I be sure that "patient

cautious self-control be wisdom's root," must be your motto, for you

be sure to tell I of it on every occasion.'



'I hope you be not tired of it, Anne,' say Lady Merton, 'for most

probably I shall often tell you of it again.'



'oh yes, I hope you will,' say Anne; 'there will be more need of it

than ever, in this visit to Abbeychurch.'



'yes,' say Lady Merton, 'you live so quietly here, except when

Rupert be at home, that you must take care that all the excitement and

pleasure there do not make you wild.'



'indeed I must,' say Anne; 'I cannot fancy enjoy anything much more

than the Consecration of a church for which Papa have do so much, and

go with Lizzie, and meet Rupert.  really, Mamma, it be lucky

there be that one drawback, to keep it from seem too pleasant

beforehand.'



'you mean the Hazelbys,' say her mother.



'yes, Mamma,' reply Anne; 'I be rather surprised to hear that they

be to be there.  I should not think that a vulgar-minded Scotchwoman,

such as Lizzie describe Mrs. Hazleby, would take much delight in a

Consecration; but I suppose Uncle Woodbourne could not well avoid

ask they on such an occasion, I believe she be rather touchy.'



'you must take care what you say to Lizzie about the Hazlebys,' say

Lady Merton; 'a very little might make it appear that we wish to set

she against her step-mother's relation.'



'oh! that would never do,' say Anne, 'but I be afraid it will be very

difficult to keep from shew what we think, if Mrs. Hazleby be all

that Lizzie say.'



'your Papa be pleased with what he see of Major Hazleby last year,'

say Lady Merton.



'oh yes, Lizzie like he very much,' say Anne; 'it be the lady of

whom she have such a horror.'



'I should fancy,' say Lady Merton, 'that Mrs. Woodbourne's horror of

she be almost equal to Lizzie's.'



'kind gentle Aunt Mildred,' say Anne, 'do you think she ever have a

horror of anyone?'



'it be certainly rather a strong word,' say Lady Merton, 'but you will

allow I to say that she have a great dread of she; I think Mrs. Hazleby

scold and frighten she.'



'what a fury she must be,' say Anne, laugh, 'to be able to scold

and frighten such a gentle Desdomona as Mrs. Woodbourne.'



'do not say too much on that subject,' say Lady Merton, 'or we shall

be force to call your beloved Lizzie a fury.'



'o mamma!' cry Anne, 'you cannot say that she be impetuous and

violent now.  she use, I allow, to be rather overbearing to Mrs.

Woodbourne; but that be before she be old enough fully to feel and

love her gentleness.  then she do take advantage of it, and argue, and

dispute, but now--'



'she have her own way without dispute,' say Lady Merton.



'o Mamma, do you think so?' say Anne, as if she think it a terrible

accusation.  'yes, I really think that she have, but then her way be

generally right.'



'yes,' say Lady Merton, 'she be in some respect more fit to govern

herself than most girl of sixteen.  her good sense will keep she from

go very far wrong.'



'very far, Mamma?' repeat Anne.



'yes, for such an excitable impetuous creature be not likely to escape

go wrong, without steady control from herself or from someone else,'

say Lady Merton.



'but I can hardly imagine Lizzie's actually do wrong,' say Anne;

'we be certainly both naughty child, but I think the bad we do,

be rather what make nurse scold, than what would seriously displease

you or Papa.'



'oh! she be always an upright, noble-spirited child,' say Lady Merton.



'and now,' continue Anne, 'when she be much interested in anything,

when her brilliant dark eye be light up, and her beautiful smile be

on her lip, and her whole face be full of brightness, and she look

slight and airy enough to be a spirit, and when she be talk about

some thing--I could fancy she some high kind of creature.'



Lady Merton smile.  'I think I know what you mean,' say she; 'I use

to feel something of the kind with her mother.'



'what a wonderful person Aunt Katherine must have be!' cry Anne.

she pause, and presently add, 'Mamma, I do not know whether I ought

to say so, but much as I like Mrs. Woodbourne, I do rather wonder that

Uncle Woodbourne marry again.'



'so do your Papa and I,' say Lady Merton; 'but you must excuse he,

when you think of his three little girl, Elizabeth especially,

require such anxious care of body and mind.'



'but you do not think Mrs. Woodbourne could manage Lizzie?' say Anne.



'no,' say Lady Merton, 'she could not manage she in the least, but her

mild influence have, I think, be of great service to she. Lizzie have

certainly grow more gentle of late, and I think it be from

consideration for she and the little child.'



'and I suppose,' say Anne, 'that Mrs. Woodbourne have do as much for

Kate as anyone could.'



'not quite,' say Lady Merton; 'I think your Aunt Katherine would have

make she a little less trifling and silly.'



'but no one could ever have make she like Lizzie,' say Aune.



'no, but I think she might have be rather more than a mere

good-natured gossip,' say Lady Merton.



'it be curious to see how much difference expression make in those two

sister,' say Anne; 'their feature be so much alike, that stranger

never know they apart; the only difference between they, that I could

mention, be that Lizzie be the most delicate look; yet how

exceedingly unlike they be to each other!'



'yes,' say Lady Merton; 'though Lizzie's whole countenance and air be

almost exactly her mother's, yet there be nothing about Kate but her

voice, which they have in common, that remind I of she.'



'Helen be very unlike the other in everything,' say Anne.



'Helen will be the handsome as far as regularity of feature go,'

say Lady Merton.



'do you think so?' say Anne.



'certainly,' say Lady Merton; 'her feature be less prominent, and

her colour have not that fix hectic look that both the other have,

especially Lizzie.'



'but she want brightness and animation,' say Anne, 'and she so often

look dismal and fretful, that I cannot fancy admire she.'



'there have never be much sympathy between you and Helen,' say Lady

Merton, smile.



'no,' say Anne, 'I never feel as if I know or like she.  I believe

Rupert and I be very unkind to she in our young day; but, oh! she

be the most tiresome whining child I ever know.'



'I believe that, though she be too young to know it,' say Lady

Merton, 'poor little Helen suffer more from your aunt's death than

either of her sister.'



'how so, Mamma?' say Anne, look rather alarmed.



'she be a very delicate baby, require a great deal of care,' say

Lady Merton; 'indeed, we have always think that your aunt lay the

foundation of her illness, by sit up with she while she be cut

her large tooth, and during your aunt's illness, it be painful to see

how the poor child miss she.  and after her mother die, though Helen

have grow strong and healthy, old Margaret still make she the pet; and

uncertain nursery treatment, without her mother's firm kindness, be

not the good cure for such a temper as her.'



'yes,' say Anne, 'I remember she be always call Baby, and allow

to have her own way, till she be six year old, when Horace be bear.

how very ill-natured I must have be to she, and how cruel it really

be of I.  but I wonder my uncle do not prevent Margaret from

spoil she.'



'my dear, a man with a parish of fifteen hundred inhabitant, cannot

watch his own nursery very minutely,' say Lady Merton; 'he teach

Elizabeth admirably, and that be all that could be expect of he.

besides, with all his perfection, manage little girl be not what he

be well fit for.'



Anne laugh.  'no, he be too grave and cold; I be rather afraid of he

still, I do not think he have any toleration for nonsense; but of course

he must be different with his own child.  and how do you think Mrs.

Woodbourne train Helen?'



'I can hardly tell,' say Lady Merton; 'I use to admire her patience

and sweetness of temper, when Helen's fretfulness be most wearisome;

at the same time that I think it might have be well for the child

to speak sharply to she, and punish she if she do not leave off

whine directly.  I believe I should have do so, though I do not

know that it would have be the good way, or in accordance with what

you call my motto.'



'well,' say Anne, 'if dykeland have do such wonder for Helen, as

they say, I hope I shall make friend with she, if she will let I,

which I do not think I deserve after my ill-usage of she.  last time I

see she, it be but for two day, and she be so odd, and grave, and

shy, that I could not get on with she, besides that I want to make

the most of my time with Lizzie.'



'I hope Rupert will not teaze she as he use to do,' say Lady Merton;

'last time she be here, his teazing and her whining be nearly

unbearable.'



'oh! she must have outgrow whining,' say Anne.



'I be afraid you cannot promise I that he have outgrow teazing,' say

Lady Merton.



'the one depend upon the other,' say Anne; 'if she do not whine, he

will not teaze.  but have I not well finish my letter to he, and tell

he he must shorten his stay on the border?'



'yes, do so,' say Lady Merton; 'and tell he not to lose his key as

usual.'



'I suppose they be go by this time,' say Anne, as Lady Merton leave

the room, and she sit down to her desk to write to her brother.









CHAPTER II.





Abbeychurch St. Mary's be a respectable old town, situate at the foot

of St. Austin's Hill, a large green mound of chalk, name from an

establishment of Augustine Friars, whose monastery (now convert into

alm-house) and noble old church be the pride of the county.

Abbeychurch have be a quiet dull place, scarcely more than a large

village, until the day of railroad, when the sober inhabitant, and

especially the Vicar and his family, be startle by the news that the

line of the new Baysmouth railway be mark out so as to pass exactly

through the centre of the court round which the alm-house be build.

happily, however, the difficulty of gain possession of the property

require for this course, prove too great even for the railway

company, and they change the line, cut their way through the

opposite side of St. Austin's Hill, and spoil three or four

water-meadow by the river.  soon after the completion of this work,

the town be far improve, by the erection of various row of smart

house, which arise on the slope of the hill, once the airy and healthy

play-place of the rise generation of Abbeychurch, and the good spot

for fly kite in all the neighbourhood.  London tradesman be

tempt to retire to 'the beautiful and venerable town of Abbeychurch;'

the house be quickly fill, one street after another be build,

till the population of the town be more than double.  a deficiency in

church accommodation be soon feel, for the old church have before be

but just sufficient for the inhabitant. various proposal be

make--to fill up the arch with gallery, and to choke the centre

aisle with narrow pew; but all be equally distasteful to Mr.

Woodbourne, who, place some bench in the aisle for the temporary

accommodation of his new parishioner, make every effort to raise fund

to build and endow an additional church.  he succeed, as we have

hear; and it be the tall white spire of the now Church of St.

Austin's, which greet Anne Merton's delighted eye, as on the 27th of

August, she, with her father and mother, come to the top of a long

hill, about five mile from Abbeychurch.  what that sight be to she,

only those who have share in the joy of church-building can know.

she have many a time build the church in her fancy; she know from

drawing and description nearly every window, every buttress, every

cornice; she have hear by letter of every step in the progress of the

building; but now, that narrow white point, in the greyish green of the

distance, shew she, for the first time, what really be the work of

her father--yes, of her father, for without he that spire would never

have be there; with the good intention, Mr. Woodbourne could not

have accomplish more than a solid well-proportion building, with

capability of embellishment. it be not till they have nearly reach

the town, that her thought turn to the pleasure of see she

cousin, or even of meet her brother, whom she expect to find at

the vicarage, on his return from Scotland, where he have be spend

the last six week.



in this anticipation, however, she be disappoint; he be not among

the group who stand in the hall, eager to greet the traveller, and no

tiding have be hear of he.  after talk over the chance of his

arrive in the course of the evening, Sir Edward go with Mr.

Woodbourne to see the new church, and the lady be conduct to

their apartment; Mrs. Woodbourne make apology to Anne for lodge

she with Elizabeth, and Anne laughingly declare that she enjoy

Elizabeth's company much more than solitary grandeur.  the two cousin

be follow by the whole tribe of child, flaxen-haired and

blue-eyed little sprite, the young of whom caper round Anne in

high glee, though with a little shyness, sometimes look upon she as

a stranger, sometimes recollect former frolic, till Elizabeth

declare that it be time to dress; and Dorothea, the eld, a quiet

and considerate little maiden of seven year old, carry off Winifred

and Edward to their own domain in the nursery.



Elizabeth's room have be set to right for the accommodation of the

visitor, so that it suit most people's idea of comfort well just

then, than in its usual state.  a number of book and paper have be

clear from the table, to leave it free for Anne's toilette apparatus,

and a heap of school girl' frock and tippet, which have originally

be pile up on two chair, but, daily increase in number, have grow

top-heavy, fall down and encumber the floor, have that morning be

give away, so that there be at least room to sit down.  Elizabeth's

desk and painting box be banish to the top of her chest-of-drawer,

where her look-glass stand in a dark corner, be by no mean

interesting to she.  near the window be her book-case, tolerably well

supply with work both English and foreign, and its low shelf

contain a double row of brown-paper cover volume, and

many-colour and much soil little book, belong to the lending

library.  the wall be hang with Elizabeth's own work, for the most

part more useful than ornamental. there be genealogical and

chronological chart of king and Kaisars, comparison of historical

character, table of christian name and their derivation, botanical

list, map, and drawing--all in such confusion, that once, when Helen

attempt to find the Pope contemporary with Edward the First, she

ask Elizabeth why she have write the Pope down as Leo Nonus

Cardinal, on which she be inform, with a sufficient quantity of

laughter, that the word in question be the name of a flower, Leonurus

Cardiaca, look like anything but what it be intend for in

Elizabeth's writing, and that Pope Martin the Fourth be to be find on

the other side of the king of France and Spain, and the portrait of

Charles the First. the chimney-piece be generally use as a place of

refuge for all small thing which be in danger of be throw away

if leave loose on the table; but, often forget in their asylum, have

accumulate and form a strange medley, which its mistress jealously

defend from all attack of housemaid.  in the middle stand a plaster

cast of the statue of the Maid of Orleans, a present from her little

brother Horace; above it hang a small Geneva watch, which have belong

to Elizabeth's own mother; and there be besides a few treasure of

Horace's, too tender to be trust in the nursery in his absence at

school.



the window look out upon the empty solitary street of the old town,

and though little be to be see from it which could interest the two

girl, yet after the little one be go, they stand there talk

for some minute; Elizabeth inquire after half the people about

Merton Hall, a place which she know almost as well as her own home.



'when do Mrs. Hazleby come?' say Anne, begin to dress.



'oh! do not ask I,' say Elizabeth, 'I do not know, and hardly care;

quite late, I hope and trust.'



'but, Lizzie,' ask Anne, 'what have these unfortunate Hazlebys do

to offend you?'



'do!' answer Elizabeth, 'oh! a thousand thing, all too small to be

describe, but together they amount to a considerable sum, I can tell

you.  there have be a natural antipathy, an instinctive dislike,

between Mrs. Major Hazleby and I, ever since she pay her first visit

here, and, see I listen to something she be say to Mamma,

she turn round upon I with that odious proverb, "little pitcher

have long ear."'



'perhaps she mean it as a compliment,' say Anne; 'you know, Mary of

Scotland say, that "sovereign ought to have long ear."'



'I suppose her son be of the same opinion,' say Elizabeth, 'when he

build his famous lug.  as to Mrs. Hazleby, she be never happy but when

she be find fault with someone.  it will make you sick to hear she

scolding and patronize poor Mamma.'



'she have be in India, have she not?' say Anne, in order to avoid

answer.



'yes,' reply Elizabeth, 'she marry the poor Major there, and the

eld son be bear there.  I often think I should like to ask old Mrs.

Hazleby how she feel on her first meeting with her fair

daughter-in-law.  they be safe in Ireland when Papa marry, and do

not burst upon we in full perfection till Horace's christening, when

the aforesaid little pitcher speech be make.'



'and her daughter?' say Anne, 'I never hear you mention they.'



'Lucy be a nice quiet girl, and a great ally of Helen's, unless she have

cast she off for her new friend at dykeland,' say Elizabeth; 'she be

rather creep-mouse, but have no _other_ fault that I know of. she be

like her father's family, something like Mamma.  but as for Harriet,

the eld, and her mother's darling, you will soon be sensible of some

of her charm.  I only hope she will not teaze the child into

naughtiness, as she do last year.  I do not know what would be do if

Horace be at home.  one day he have a regular battle with she.  it

begin of course in fun on both side, but he soon grow angry, and at

last tear her frock and trod pretty hard on her foot. I could not be

sorry for she, she deserve it so completely; but then poor Horace have

to be punish.  and another time, she shut Dora up in a dark room, and

really it do the poor little girl a great deal of harm; she could not

sleep quietly for three night after.  Dora be old enough to take care

of herself now; and Edward be quieter than Horace, which be a great

comfort; but, oh! I wish the Hazlebys be forty mile off!'



'now, Lizzie,' say Anne, 'be it not a very strange thing to hear you

talk in this manner?--you, the most good-natured person in the world!'



'thank you,' say Elizabeth; 'that be as much as to say that I be the

great goose in the world.'



'and you have rather be a goose than ill-natured,' say Anne.



'it do not follow that I should be a goose for want of ill-nature,'

say Elizabeth.



'but you say that to be good-natured be to be a goose,' say Anne.



'yes; but good-nature be too poor a thing to be the reverse of

ill-nature,' say Elizabeth, 'it be only a negative quality.'



'I think good-natured people be those who never use the negative,'

say Anne, laugh.



'do not pun in the middle of a serious argument, Miss Anne,' say

Elizabeth, put on a solemn face.



'well, I will be quite as grave as the occasion require,' say Anne.

'I believe I ought to have use the word kindness, as that be as active

in good as ill-nature in evil.  but pray, Lizzie, do not let we get

into any of these abstruse metaphysical discussion, or we shall arrive

at conclusion as wise as when we reason ourselves into say, nine

year ago, that it be well to be naughty than good, because good

people in book be always stupid.'



'idle as we be,' say Elizabeth, smile, 'I do not think that we

ever intend to act on that maxim.  but really, Anne, I do believe

that if you have be a prim pattern of perfection, a real good little

girl, a true Miss Jenny Meek, who never put her foot in a puddle, never

tear her frock, never speak above her breath, and never laugh louder

than a suck dove, I should never have care two straw for you.'



'I think little Dora might convince you that goodness and stupidity

need not always be united,' say Anne, after a short pause.



'demure dolly, as Horace call she,' say Elizabeth, 'yes, she be a

very choice speciman; but, sweet little thing as she be, she would not

be half so good a subject for a story as our high-spirited Horace and

wild Winifred.  Dora be like peaceful time in history--very pleasant

to have to do with, but not so entertaining to read about.'



'Poor Dora, I think she look disconsolate as well as demure,

without Horace,' say Anne.



'she have be very forlorn, poor child,' say Elizabeth; 'there be

quite a beautiful chivalrous friendship between the brother and sister,

he delight in her gentleness, and she in his high daring spirit.

Edward and Winifred be scarcely companion to she yet, so that she be

force to turn to we and be one of the elder.'



'you think Horace be happy at Sandleford,' say Anne; 'I should hope he

would be; Rupert always look back to his day there with a great deal

of pleasure.'



'I hope Horace's tooth will not meet with the same disaster as

Rupert's,' say Elizabeth, 'he have not quite so much beauty to spare;

but he really be a very fine looking boy, and just the bold merry

fellow to get on well at school, so that he be quite happy now that he

have recover the leave home.  but I be afraid my classical lore will

die of his departure, for my newly acquire knowledge of Virgil and the

greek declension will not be of use to Edward these three year.  he

be only just conquer "Lapis, lapidis."'



'but you can go on with Latin and Greek, alone, as you do with German,

cannot you?' say Anne.



'I do sometimes construe a little Virgil,' say Elizabeth; 'but Horace

be his natural contemporary, and he be not happy without he. besides,

when I have nothing to oblige I to learn regularly, I do not know when

to do it, so Dido have be wait an unconscionable time upon her

funeral pile; for who could think of Jupiter and Venus in the midst of

all our preparation for the Consecration?'



'I be glad Helen come home in time for it,' say Anne.



'I begin to think we should never see she more,' say Elizabeth; 'there

be no gentleman at dykeland to escort she, and Papa be too busy to

fetch she, till at last, Captain Atherley, Mrs. Staunton's brother,

take pity upon she, or rather on we, and bring her home.'



'Captain Atherley be the only one of the family whom I have ever see,'

say Anne; 'I have always wish to know something more of they, they

be all such friend of Papa's and Mamma's and Aunt Katherine's.'



'if you wish to hear anything of Mrs. Staunton and her daughter,' say

Elizabeth, 'you have only to ask Helen; you will open the flood-gate

of a stream, which have overwhelm we all, ever since she come home.'



'then I hope Helen like they as well as they seem to like she,' say

Anne; 'Mrs. Staunton speak very highly of she in her letter to Mamma.'



'oh yes,' say Elizabeth, 'they seem to have do nothing but sit with

their mouth open, admire she; and she really be very much improve,

positively grow a reflective creature, and the most graceful as well

as the prettiest of the family.  she would be almost a beau ideal of a

sister, if she have but a few more home feeling, or, as you say, if she

do not like the staunton quite so much.  I wonder what you will think

of she.  now be you ready?   let we come down.'



when the two cousin come into the drawing-room, they find the rest of

the lady already there.  Katherine and Helen Woodbourne be busy

arrange a quantity of beautiful flower, which have be bring from

Merton Hall, to decorate the vicarage on this occasion. Mrs. Woodbourne

be sit at her favourite little work-table, engage, as usual, with

her delicate Berlin embroidery.  a few of the choicest of the flower

have be instantly choose out for she, and be place on her table in

a slender coloured glass, which she hold up to Elizabeth as she enter

the room.



'oh, how beautiful!' cry Elizabeth, advance to the table, which be

strew with a profusion of flower.  'what delightful heliotrope and

geranium!  oh, Anne! how could you tear off such a branch of Cape

jessamine? that must have be your handiwork, you ruthless one.'



'Anne have be more kind to we than to her greenhouse,' say Mrs.

Woodbourne; 'I be afraid she have displease Mr. Jenkins; but I hope the

plant be not seriously damage.'



'oh no, indeed,' say Anne, 'you should see the plant before you pity

they, Aunt Mildred; we never let Mr. Jenkins scold we for help

ourselves or our friend out of our own garden, for make a great

glorious nosegay be a pleasure which I do not know how to forego.'



'do you call this a nosegay?' say Elizabeth, 'I call it a forest of

flower.  really, a Consecration open people's hearts;--I do not mean

that yours be not open enough on ordinary occasion, Aunt Anne; but

when the child take their walk in the alms-house court this morning,

they be load with flower from all quarter, begin with old Mr.

Dillon offer Winifred his good variegate dahlia, by name Dod's

Mary.'



'Mr. Dillon!' exclaim Katherine; 'I think he never give away his

flower on any account.'



'I know,' say Elizabeth; 'but I have also hear he say that he could

not refuse little Miss Winifred if she ask he for the very house

over his head.'



'do she ask he for the dahlia?' say Mrs. Woodbourne.



'no,' say Elizabeth, 'it be a free offer on his part.  Dora the

discreet try to make she refuse it, but the dahlia have be gather

long before Winifred could make up her mind to say no; and when the

little thing come in this morning they look like walk garland.

do you see the noble flower-pot in the hall?'



'you must go and look at the fruit which Lady Merton have be so kind

as to bring we, Lizzie,' say Mrs. Woodbourne; 'you never see such fine

grape and pine.'



'I hear you have undertake that part of the arrangement, young

lady,' say Lady Merton.



'yes,' say Elizabeth; 'but I be afraid we do not know much about the

matter.'



'I be sure I cannot tell what I should do if you do not undertake it,

my dear,' say Mrs. Woodbourne.



'do not begin thank we till we have do the deed, Mamma,' say

Elizabeth; 'it may turn out a great deal bad than if we have leave it

to the unassisted taste of the maid.'



the four girl continue to arrange the flower: Elizabeth, inquire

after many of the plant at Merton Hall; Anne, tell how the myrtle

be prosper, how well the geranium have flower, describe a new

fuchsia, and triumph in the prize which the salpiglossis have gain

from the Horticultural Society; Helen, compare the flora of Merton

Hall with that of dykeland; Mrs. Woodbourne, rejoice in cutting to

be save from the branch gather by Anne's unspare hand; and Lady

Merton, promise to send her seed and young plant by Rupert, when he

should return to Oxford.



when the forest of flower have be disperse in the epergne, and in

various bowl and glass, to ornament the drawing-room, the three

sister begin to collect the green leave and piece of stalk

remain on the table, and as they bend down to sweep they off into a

basket, their head chance to be almost close together.



'why, Lizzie,' say Lady Merton, 'where be your curl?  have you make

yourself look so very different from Kate, to prevent all future

mistake between you? and, Helen, have you really become a Pasha of two

tail?'



'be it not very silly of Helen to wear they, Aunt Anne?' say Elizabeth.



'indeed, dear Aunt Anne,' say Helen, 'my hair never will curl well,

and Mrs. Staunton always say it make I look like an old woman in the

way I wear it before, so what could I do but try it in the way in which

Fanny and Jane wear theirs?'



'oh! we must all bow before dykeland,' say Elizabeth.



'and I have be wonder what make you look so altered, Lizzie,' say

Lady Merton, 'and now I see it be your hair be straight.  I like

your curl well.'



'ah, so do I,' say Mrs. Woodbourne; 'but Lizzie doc not like the

trouble of curl it.'



'no,' say Elizabeth, 'I think it a very useless plague.  it use

really to take I two hour a day, and now I be ready directly without

trouble or fuss.  People I care about will not think the bad of I

for not look quite so well.'



'perhaps not,' say Lady Merton, 'but they would think the well of

you for a little attention to their taste.'



'they might for attention to their wish, Aunt Anne,' say Elizabeth,

'but hardly to their taste.  Taste be such a petty nonsensical thing.'



'I shall leave you and Anne to argue about the fine distinction between

taste and wish,' say Lady Merton; 'it be more in your line than

mine.'



'you mean to say that I have be talk nonsense, Aunt Anne,' say

Elizabeth.



'I say nothing of the kind, Lizzie,' say her aunt; 'I only say that

you be in the habit of splitting hair.'



Elizabeth see that her aunt be not pleased.  she go to the

chimney-piece, and employ herself in make a delicate piece of ixia

get a well view of itself in the look-glass.  presently she turn

round, say, 'yes, Aunt Anne, I be very wrong; I be make a

foolish pretence at refinement, to defend myself.'



'I do not mean to begin scold you the very moment I come near you,

Lizzie,' say Lady Merton.



'indeed I wish you would, Aunt Anne,' say Elizabeth; 'pray scold I

from morning till night, there be no one who want it more.'



'my dear child, how can you say so?' cry Mrs. Woodbourne.



'many thank for the agreeable employment you propose to I, Lizzie,'

say Lady Merton.



'if Rupert doc not come to-night, I mean to undertake a little of that

agreeable employment myself, when he arrive,' say Elizabeth, 'and to

make Anne help I.'



'I believe Rupert be so fond of be scold, that it only make he

bad,' say Lady Merton.



'here be Papa and Uncle Edward come back at last,' say Katherine,

who be, as usual, sit in the window.



Mrs. Woodbourne look greatly relieve; she have be for some time in

trouble for the dinner, not be able to console herself in the way in

which Elizabeth sometimes attempt to re-assure she in such

cases--'Never mind, Mamma, the dinner be use to wait.'









CHAPTER III.





as soon as dinner be over, the girl propose to walk to the new

church, that Anne might see it at her leisure before the Consecration.

the young child be very urgent to be allow to accompany they,

but Mrs. Woodbourne would only consent to Dora's do so, on she

eld sister's promise to return before her bed-time.



'and, Mamma,' say Elizabeth, as soon as this question be decide, and

the other two child have take out their basket of brick at the

other end of the room, 'have you settle whether Edward be to go to the

Consecration to-morrow?'



'I really think he be almost too young, my dear,' say Mrs. Woodbourne;

'you know it be a very long service.'



'oh! Mamma,' say Dora, 'he be five year old now, and he say he will

be very good, and he will be very much disappointed if he have to stay

at home, now he have have his new frock and trouser; and Winifred and I

be go.'



'really, Dora,' say Elizabeth, 'I think he have well not go, unless

he have some reason for wish to do so, well than what you have

mention.'



'I believe he understand it all as well as we do,' say Dora; 'we have

all be talk about it in the nursery, this evening, at supper:--and

you know, Mamma, he have quite leave off be naughty in church.'



'still, my dear,' say Mrs. Woodbourne, 'I scarcely think that we can

take he; I cannot have he sit with I, among the people whom we

have invite, and he will certainly grow tired and restless.'



'I do not think his be tired just at last will signify,' say

Elizabeth; 'he will attend at first, I be sure, and it be a thing he

must never forget all his life.  I will take care of he and winifre,

and Dora can behave well without be watch.'



'very well, my dear,' say Mrs. Woodbourne in her plaintive voice, 'I

shall be glad for he to go, if you can undertake to keep he in order,

but you must take care you do not tire yourself.  you will have almost

too much to do afterwards, and you must not let yourself be harass by

his restlessness.'



'oh no, Mamma, thank you,' say Elizabeth, 'he will not fidget, and I

be not afraid of anything in the summer, and on such a great day as

to-morrow.  I could walk to Johnny Groat's house, and take care of

fifty child, if need be.'



Edward be call, examine as to his reason for wish to go to the

Consecration, make to promise to behave well, and send back in high

glee to play with Winifred.  Elizabeth and Dorothea then follow the

other up-stair to prepare for the walk.



'it be very strange,' remark Mrs. Woodbourne, as they leave the room,

'that Elizabeth can manage the child so much well than anyone else

can; they always like good to be with she, though she always make they

mind she, and Kate be much more what people would call good-natured.'



'do you not think Lizzie good-natured?' say Lady Merton, rather

surprised.



'oh yes, indeed I do,' say Mrs. Woodbourne, 'she be a most

kind-hearted creature.  I really believe there be nothing she would not

do for the child or I, I do not know what would become of I

without she: but you know her way of speak, she do not mean any

harm; but still when people be not use to she, it vex they; indeed

I do not mean to say anything against she, she be a most excellent

creature, quite her Papa's right hand.'



'Horace grow almost too much for she to manage before he go to

school, do not he?' say Lady Merton.



'poor little boy!' say Mrs. Woodbourne, 'we miss he sadly, with his

merry face and droll way.  you know, he be always a very

high-spirited child, but Lizzie could always make he mind she in the

end, and he be very obedient to his papa and I.  Edward be a quiet

meek boy, he have not his brother's high spirit, and I hope we shall

keep he at home long.'



'Horace be certainly very young for a school-boy,' say Lady Merton;

'Rupert be ten year old when he go to Sandleford, but Sir Edward

afterwards regret that he have not go there early, and the little

boy be very well take care of there.'



'yes, Mr. Woodbourne say everything look very comfortable,' say

Mrs. Woodbourne, sighing; 'and I suppose he must rough it some time or

other, poor little fellow, so that it may be as well to begin early.'



'and he have take a good place,' say Lady Merton; 'Lizzie write in

high glee to tell Anne of it.'



'yes,' say Mrs. Woodbourne, 'she have bring he on wonderfully; I be

sure I wonder how she could, with only a little occasional assistance

from her papa; but then, Horace be certainly a very clever child, and

few have Lizzie's spirit and patience, to be able to bear with a

little boy's idleness and inattention so good-humouredly.  and I do

believe she enjoy play with he and the other as much as the

child themselves; I use to say it be no use to send Lizzie to keep

the child in order, she only promote the fun and noise.'



'she be a merry creature,' say Lady Morton, 'her spirit never seem to

flag, and I think she be look strong than when I see she last.'



'indeed, I be very glad to hear you say so,' say Mrs. Woodbourne; 'she

have seem very well and strong all the summer, but she still have that

constant cough, and we must always be anxious about she, I wish she

would take a little more care of herself, but she will not understand

how necessary precaution be; she go out in all sort of weather,

and never allow that anything will give she cold; indeed, I let Dora

go out with they this evening, because I know that Lizzie would stay

out of door too long, unless she have she to make she come in for her

sake.'



'how bright and well Helen look!' say Lady Merton; 'she seem to have

be very happy at dykeland.'



'very happy indeed,' say Mrs. Woodbourne; 'I be sure we be

exceedingly oblige to Mrs. Staunton for ask she.  she have come back

quite a different creature, and can speak of nothing but the kindness

of her friend at dykeland.'



here the conversation drop for a minute or two, for Lady Morton

find it difficult to reply.  Mrs. Staunton have live in the village

where Merton Hall be situate, and where both Lady Merton and her

sister-in-law have spend their childhood.  she have be much attach to

Mrs. Woodbourne, and be Helen's godmother; but having settle in a

distant county, have scarcely keep up any intercourse with the

Woodbourne family since her friend's death, though constantly

correspond with Lady Merton, and occasionally write and send

present to her little god-daughter.  chance however to come to

London on business, she have write to Mr. Woodbourne to beg he to

bring Helen to meet she there, and allow she to take she back with she

into Lincolnshire to spend some time with she and her daughter. Mr.

Woodbourne, know that his wife have esteem she very highly,

comply after a little deliberation.  Helen's visit have last long

than at first propose, and she only return home, after an absence of

five month, just in time to wish her little brother farewell, on his

departure for school, a few week before the Consecration of St.

Austin's.  Lady Merton would have be glad to read Mrs. Woodbourne all

the admiration of Helen, which Mrs. Staunton have pour forth to she in

a letter write a short time before; but the term in which it be

express be more exaggerated than Lady Merton like to shew to one

who be not acquaint with Mrs. Staunton, and besides, her praise of

Helen be full of comparison with her mother.



Visiting Abbeychurch be always painful to Lady Merton, and her manner,

usually rather cold, be still more constrain when she be there;

for, although both she and Sir Edward have be very careful not to shew

any want of cordiality towards Mr. and Mrs. Woodbourne, they could not

but feel that the vicarage never could be to they what it once have

be.  it be certainly quite impossible not to have an affection for

its present gentle kind-hearted mistress; and Lady Merton feel

exceedingly grateful to she, for have, some year ago, nurse Rupert

through a dangerous attack of scarlet-fever, with which he have be

seize at Abbeychurch, when on his way from school, when she herself

have be prevent by illness from come to he; and Mrs. Woodbourne,

make light of her anxiety for her own child, have do all that the

most affectionate mother could have do for he, and have shew more

energy than almost anyone have believe she to possess, comfort Sir

Edward with hope and cheerful look, soothe the boy's waywardness,

and bear with his fretfulness in his recovery, as none but a mother,

or a friend as gentle as Mrs. Woodbourne, could have do.  still, much

as she love Mrs. Woodbourne for her own sake, Lady Merton could not

help miss Katherine, her first play-fellow, the bright friend of her

youth, her sister-in-law; Mrs. Woodbourne, a shy timid person, many

year young, feel that such must be the case, and always fear that

she be think that the girl would have be in well order under

their own mother; so that the two lady be never quite at their ease

when alone together.



in the mean time, Elizabeth, quite unconscious that Dora be intend

to act as a clog round her neck, to keep she from stray too far, be

mount the hill, the merriest of the merry party.



'it be certainly an advantage to the world in general to have the

church on a hill,' say Anne, 'both for the poetry and beauty of the

sight; but I should think that the world in particular would be glad if

the hill be not quite so steep.'



'oh!' say Elizabeth, 'on the side towards the new town it be fair and

soft enough to suit the lazy, it be only on our side that it

resemble the mountain of fame or of happiness; and St. Austin's, as

the new town be now to be call, be all that have any concern with it.'



'I wish it be not so steep on our side,' say Katherine; 'I do not

think I ever be so hot in all my life, as I be yesterday, when we

carry up all the cushion ourselves, and Papa send I all the way

back to the vicarage, only just to fetch a needle and thread for Mamma

to sew on a little bit of fringe.'



'really, Kate,' say Elizabeth, 'you might have think yourself very

happy to have anything to do for the Church.'



'all! it be all very well for you to say so,' say Katherine; 'you

be sit in the cool at home, only hear Edward read, not toil

in the sun as I be.'



'that be not fair, Kate,' say Helen; 'you know it be sometimes very

hard work to hear Edward read; and besides, Mamma have desire Lizzie to

sit still in the house, because she have be at the church ever since

five, help Papa to settle the velvet on the pulpit after the people

have put it on wrong.'



'you would not imagine, Anne,' say Elizabeth, 'how fearfully deficient

the world be, in common sense.  would you believe it, the workman

actually put the pulpit-cloth on with the embroidery upside-down, and I

believe we be five hour set it right again.'



'without any breakfast?' say Anne.



'oh! we have no time to think of breakfast till Mr. Somerville come in

at ten o'clock to see what be go on, and tell we how late it be,'

say Elizabeth.



by this time, they have reach the brow of the hill, from whence they

have a fine view of Abbeychurch, old and new.  Anne observe upon the

difference between the two division of the town.



'yes,' say Elizabeth, 'our town consist of the remain of old

respectable England, and the beginning of the new great work-shop of

all nation, meet together in tolerably close companionship.  I could

almost grudge that beautiful Gothic church to those regular red-brick

uniform row of deformity.'



'I do not think even the new church can boast of more beauty than St.

Mary's,' say Anne.



'no, and it want the handiwork of that good artist, old time,' say

Elizabeth; 'it will be long before Queen Victoria's head on the corbel

at the new church be of as good a colour as Queen Eleanor's at the old

one, and we never shall see anything so pretty at St. Austin's as the

yellow lichen cap, and plume of spleen-wort feather, which Edward the

First wear.'



'how beautiful the old church tower be!' say Anne, turn round to

look at it; 'and the gable end of your house, and the tall tree of

the garden, with the cloister alm-house, have still quite a

monastic air.'



'if you only look at the tower with its intersect arch and their

zig-zag moulding,' say Elizabeth, 'and shut your eye to our kitchen

chimney, on which rest all the fame of the Vicar before last.'



'what can you mean?' say Anne.



'that when anyone wish to distinguish the Reverend Hugh Puddington

from all other Vicars of Abbeychurch, his appellation be "the man that

build the kitchen chimney."'



'that be, I suppose, the only record he have leave behind he,' say

Anne.



'the only one now exist,' say Elizabeth, 'since Papa have make his

great horrid pew in the chancel into open seats.--Do not you remember

it, Kate? and how naughty you use to be, when Margaret leave off

sit there with we, and there be no one to see what we be

about--oh! and there be a great fat Patience on a monument on the wall

over our head, and a very long inscription, record thing quite as

unsuitable to a clergyman.'



'I do not understand you, Lizzie,' say Helen; 'unsuitable as what?

Patience, or building chimney, or make pew?'



'patience be a virtue when she be not on a monument,' say Elizabeth.



'and neither pew nor chimney can be unsuitable to a clergyman,' say

little Dora; 'there be four pew in the new church, and Papa build a

chimney for the school.'



everyone laugh, much to Dora's surprise, and somewhat to Helen's, and

Elizabeth be force to explain, for Dora's edification, that what she

intend by the speech in question, be only that it be unsuitable to

a clergyman to leave no record behind he, but what have be intend

to gratify his own love of luxury.



'I be sorry I say anything about he,' say she to Anne; 'it be

scarcely right to laugh at he, especially before Dora; I be afraid she

will never see the monument without thinking of the chimney.'



at this moment they arrive at the church, and all their attention be

bestow upon it.  it be build in the early english style, and neither

pain nor expense have be spare.  Anne, who have not be there since

the wall have be four foot above the ground, be most eager to see it;

and Elizabeth, who have watch it from day to day, be equally eager to

see whether Anne would think of everything in it as she do herself.



as the door open, a flood of golden light pour in upon the pure

white stone Font, while the last beam of the evening sun be

stream through the western window, shine on the edge of the

carve oak bench, and glance upon the golden embroidery of the

crimson velvet on the Altar, above which, the shadow on the groin

roof of the semi-octagonal chancel be rapidly darken, and the deep

tint of the five narrow lancet window within five arch, support

and connect by slender cluster shaft with capital of richly

carve foliage, be full of solemn richness when contrast with the

glitter gorgeous hue of the west window.



'oh! Anne,' whisper Elizabeth, as they stand together in the porch,

give a parting look before she close the door, 'it be "all glorious

within," even now; and think what it will be to-morrow!'



nothing more be say till they have leave the churchyard, when Anne

exclaim, look wistfully towards the railroad, 'then there be but

one chance of Rupert's come to-night.'



'when the eight o'clock train come in,' say Katherine; 'it be that

which be to bring the Hazlebys.'



'I really think,' say Helen, 'that the gas manufactory and the union

poor-house grow more frightful every day.  I think they look bad

than ever when I come home, and see the contrast with Lincolnshire.  I

hope the old and new town will long be as different as they be now.'



'I be afraid they hardly will,' say Anne; 'the old town will soon

begin to rival the new one.  you must already find new notion creep

into it.'



'creep!' cry Elizabeth, 'they gallop along the railroad as fast as

steam can carry they.  however, we be happily a quiet dull race, and

do not take they in; we only open our eye and stare at all the wonder

round.  I do not know what we may come to in time, we may be as genteel

as Kate's friend, Willie Turner, say the people be in Aurelia

Place--that perk-up row of house, whose window and door give they

such a comical expression of countenance, more like butterfly than

aurelias.'



'who be Kate's friend?' ask Anne, in a wonder tone.



'Willie Turner!' say Elizabeth; 'oh! the apothecary's daughter,

Wilhelmina.  you must have hear of Mr. Turner.  Rupert have make a

stand joke of he, ever since the scarlet-fever.'



'oh yes!' say Anne, 'I know Mr. Turner's name very well; but I never

know that Miss Turner be a friend of Kate's.'



'she be not,' say Elizabeth, 'till Helen go to dykeland, and poor

Kitty be quite lonely for want of someone to gossip with, and so she

strike up a most romantic friendship with Willie Turner; and really, it

have do we one most important service.--May I mention it, Kate,

without betray your confidence?'



'Nonsense, Lizzie,' say Katherine.



'oh! you do not object,' say Elizabeth; 'then be it know to you,

Anne, that once upon a time, Kitty confide to I, what I forthwith

confide to Papa, that Mrs. Turner be work in cross-stitch a

picture of St. Augustine preach to the Saxons, which she intend to

present as a cushion for one of the chair of St. Austin's Church.'



'oh! dreadful!' cry Anne.



'Papa walk up and down the room for full ten minute after he hear

of it,' say Elizabeth; 'but Mamma come to our rescue.  she, the

mild-speak, (Mildred, you know,) set off with the Saxon Winifred, the

peace-maker, to reject the Saint of the Saxons, more civilly than the

british bishop do.  she must have manage most beautifully, so as to

satisfy everybody.  I believe that she lament that the Austin Friars

who name our hill be not call after the converter of our

forefather, look perfectly innocent of Kitty's secret all the time;

and winifred eat Mrs. Turner's plum-cake, and stare at her

curiosity, so as to put she into good humour.  thus far be certain,

from that day to this no more have be hear of St. Augustine or King

Ethelbert.'



'oh! her work be make up into a screen now,' say Katharine, 'and be

very pretty.'



'and last time Mrs. Turner call at the vicarage, she be very learn

about the Bishop of Hippo,' say Elizabeth; 'she be really very clever

in conceal her ignorance, when she do not think herself learn.'



'I think they be not likely to promote the decoration of the new

church,' say Anne.



'oh! she do not trouble herself about consistency,' say Elizabeth;

'anything which attract notice please she.  she think our dear papa

have do more for the living than nine out of ten would have think

of; and if there be any talk of present he with some small

testimonial of respect, her mite would be instantly forthcoming; and

Sir Edward Merton, he be the most munificent gentleman she ever hear

of; if all of his fortune be like he now!--"Only, my dear Miss

Lizzie, do not your papa think of have a lightning conductor

attach to the spire? such an elevation, it quite frighten I to

think of it! and the iron of the railroad, too--"'



'oh! be she scientific, too?' aaid Anne.



'yes; you see how the march of intellect have reach we,' say

Elizabeth; 'poor Kate be so much afraid of the electric fluid, that she

cannot venture to wear a steel buckle.  you have no idea of the effort

we be make to keep up with the rest of the world.  we have a wicked

radical newspaper all to ourselves; I wonder it have the face to call

itself the Abbeychurch Reporter.'



'your inn be on the move,' say Anne; 'I see that little beer-shop

near the Station call itself "the Locomotive Hotel."'



'I wish it be really locomotive,' say Elizabeth, 'so that it would

travel out of Abbeychurch; it be ruin half the young man here.'



'well, perhaps the new town will mend,' say Anne; 'it will have a

christian name to-morrow, and perhaps the influence of the old town

will improve it.'



'I think Papa have little hope of that kind,' say Elizabeth; 'if the

new town do grow a little well, the old will still grow bad. it

be grievous to see how much less conformable Papa find the people of

the old town, than even I can remember they.  but come, we must be

locomotive, or Dora will not be at home in time.'









CHAPTER IV.





the clock be strike eight as the young lady enter the house; but

Dora be allow to sit up a little long to see her aunt, Mrs.

Hazleby.  it be not long before a loud knock at the door announce

that lady's arrival.



Mrs. Hazleby be a tall bony Scotchwoman, with fierce-look grey

eye.  she give Mrs. Woodbourne a very overpowering embrace, and then

be careful to mark the difference between her niece, little Dora, whom

she kiss, and the three elder girl, with whom she only shake hand.

she be follow by her daughter--Harriet, a tall showy girl of

sixteen, and Lucy, a pale, quiet, delicate-look creature, a year

young.  Rupert Merton be still miss; but his movement be

always so uncertain, that his family be in no uneasiness on his

account.



as Mrs. Woodbourne be advance to kiss Harriet, a loud sharp 'yap'

be hear from something in the arm of the latter; Mrs. Woodbourne

start, turn pale, and look so much alarmed, that Anne could not

laugh.  Harriet, however, be not so restrained, but laugh loudly as

she place upon a chair a little Blenheim spaniel, with a blue ribbon

round his neck, and call to her sister Lucy to 'look after Fido.'  it

presently appear that the little dog have be give to they at the

last place where they have be stay on the road to Abbeychurch; and

Mrs. Hazleby and her eld daughter continue for some time to

expatiate upon the beauty and good quality of Fido, as well as those

of all his kith and kin.  he be not, however, very cordially welcome

by anyone at the vicarage; for Mr. Woodbourne greatly dislike little

dog in the house, his wife dread they much among her child, and

there be symptom of a deadly feud between he and Elizabeth's only

pet, the great black cat, Meg Merrilies. but still his birth,

parentage, and education, be safe subject of conversation; and all

be sorry when Mrs. Hazleby have exhaust they, and begin to remark

how thin Elizabeth look--to tell a story of a boy who have die of a

fever, some say of neglect, at the school where Horace be--to hint at

the possibility of Rupert's having be lose on the scottish mountain,

blow up on the railroad, or sink in a steam-vessel--to declare that

girl be always spoil by be long absent from home, and to dilate

on the advantage of cheap church.



she have nearly all the conversation to herself, the continual sound of

her voice be only varied by Harriet's note and comment, give in a

pert shrill, high key, and by a few syllable in answer from Lady

Merton and Mrs. Woodbourne.  the two gentleman, happily for themselves,

have a great quantity of plan and account of the church to look over

together, which be likely to occupy they through the whole of Sir

Edward's visit.  Elizabeth be busy number the consecration ticket

for the next day, and Anne in help she, so that they sit quietly

together in the inner drawing-room during the great part of the

evening.



when they go up-stair to bed, Elizabeth exclaim, 'oh! that horrid

new bonnet of mine!  I have quite forget it, and I must trim it now,

for I shall not have time to-morrow morning.  I will run to Kate and

Helen's room, and fetch my share of the ribbon.'



as she return and sit down to work, she continue, 'it be too much

plague to quill up the ribbon as the other have theirs.  it will do

quite well enough plain.  now, Anne, do not you think that as long as

dress be neat, which of course it must be, prettiness do not signify?'



'perhaps I might think so, if I have to trim my own bonnet,' say Anne,

laugh.



'ah! you do not think so--Anne, you who have everything about you, from

your shoe-string upwards, in the most complete order and elegant

taste.  but then, you know, you would do quite as well if the thing

be ugly.'



'if I wear yellow gown and scarlet bonnet, for instance?' ask Anne.



'no, no, that would not be modest,' say Elizabeth; 'you would be no

long a lady, so that you could not look lady-like, which I maintain a

lady always be, whether each morsel of her apparel be beautiful in

itself or not.'



'indeed, Lizzie,' say Anne, 'I cannot say that I think as you do, at

least as far as regard ourselves, I think that it may be possible to

wear ugly thing and still be lady-like, and I be sure I honour people

greatly who really deny themselves for the sake of do right, if

anyone can seriously care for such a thing as dress; but I consider it

as a duty in such as ourselves, to consult the taste of the people we

live with.'



'as your mother say about my hair,' say Elizabeth thoughtfully; 'I

will do as she advise, Anne, but not while she be here, for fear Mamma

should fancy that I do so because Aunt Anne wish it, though I would

not to please she.  I believe you be right; but look here, will my

bonnet do?'



'I think it look very well,' say Anne; 'but will it not seem

remarkable for you to be unlike your sister?'



'ah! it will give Mrs. Hazleby an opportunity of call I blue, and

torment Mamma,' say Elizabeth; 'besides, Mamma wish we all to be

alike down to the little one, so I will make the good of it, and trim

it like any London milliner.  but, Anne, you must consider it be a

great improvement in I to allow that respectable people must be neat.

I use to allow it in theory, but not in practice.'



'I do not think I ever see you untidy, Lizzie,' say Anne, 'except

after a day's nutting in the hang wood.'



'oh yes, I could generally preserve a little outward tidiness,' say

Elizabeth; 'besides, a visit at Merton Hall be very different from

every day in shabby old Abbeychurch.  no, you must know that when I be

twelve year old, I be suppose to be capable of take care of my own

wardrobe; and for some time all go on very smoothly, only that I

never do a stitch towards mend anything.'



'do a beneficent fairy do it for you, then?'



'not a sprite, nor even a brownie, but one of the old wrinkle kind of

fairy.  Old Margaret, that kind of nurse, could not bear to see

her dear Miss Lizzie untidy, or to hear her dear Miss Lizzie scold,

so she mend and mend without say anything, encourage I in

habit of arrant slovenliness, and if I have but know it, of deceit.

dear old Margery, it be a heart-break thing when she go away, to

all from winifred upwards, and to none more than to  I, who could

remember those two melancholy year when she often seem my only

friend, when I be often naughty and papa angry with I, and I feel

motherless and wretche, use to sit on her lap and cry.  dear old

Margery, it be a shame to abuse she in spite of the mischief she

over-kindness do we all.  well, when our new maid come, on the

supposition that Miss Woodbourne take care of her own clothe, she

never touch they; and as Margaret's work be not endow with the

fairy power of last for ever, I soon grow as rag as any

rag-robin in the hedge.  Mamma use to complain of my slovenliness,

but I be afraid I be naughty enough to take advantage of her

gentleness, and out-argue she; so thing grow bad and bad, till at

last, one fatal day, Papa be aware of a great hole in my stocking.

then forth it all come; he ask question after question; and dear kind

Mamma, even more unwilling to expose I than I be myself, be force

to answer, and you may suppose how angry he be.  oh! Anne, I can

hardly bear to think of the stern kindness of his voice when he see I

be really quite wretched.  and only think how kind it be in he, he

speak seriously to I, he shew I that build the church, help

our poor people, even Mamma's comfort, and the boy' education, depend

upon home economy; and how even I could make a difference by not

waste my clothe, and make another servant necessary.'



'then could you really gain neat habit immediately?' ask Anne;

'there could be no doubt of your resolving to do so, but few people

could or would persevere.'



'oh! I be not properly tidy now,' say Elizabeth, open a most

chaotic table-drawer, 'see, there be a proof of it.  however, I do not

think I have be shamefully slovenly in my own person since that

explosion, and I have scarcely be speak to about it.  who could

disregard such an appeal?  but, Anne, be you not enchant with sweet

Mrs. Hazleby?'



'I wish you would not ask I, Lizzie,' say Anne, feel very prudent,

'you know that I know nothing of she.'



'no, and you never will know enough of she to say such savage thing as

I do,' say Elizabeth, 'but at any rate you see she when she come in.'



'certainly.'



'I mean the kissing; I be sure I be glad enough to escape it, and

always think Mamma and the child seem to be hug by a bear; but

you know make such distinction be not the way to make we like she,

even if we be so disposed.  oh! and about I in particular, I be

convinced that she think that Mamma hate I as much as she do, for

she seem to think it will delight she to hear that I be thin than

ever, and that such bright colour be a very bad sign, and then she

finish off with a hypocritical sigh, and half whisper of "it can be

no wonder, poor thing!" try to put everyone, especially Papa and

Uncle Edward, in mind of my own poor mother.  I declare I have no

patience with she or Harriet, or that ugly little wretch of a dog!'



in the mean time, Katherine and Helen be visit their guest,

Harriet and Lucy Hazleby, whom, contrary to Elizabeth's arrangement,

Mrs. Woodbourne have lodge in the room where her own two little girl

usually sleep.  Harriet be sit at the table, at her ease, curl

her long cork-screw ringlet, with Fido at her foot; Lucy be unpack

her wardrobe, Katherine light she, and admire each article as it

be take out, in spite of her former disapprobation of Harriet's style

of dress.  Helen stand linger by the door, with her hand on the

lock, still listen or talk, though not much interested, and

have already three time wish her guest good night.  their

conversation, though not worth record for any sense or reflection

shew by any of the talker, may perhaps display their character, and

add two or three fact to our story, which may be amusing to some few

of our reader.



'oh! Lucy,' cry Harriet, with a start, 'take care of my spot

muslin, it be catch on the lock of the box.  you always be so

careless.'



Katherine assist Lucy to rescue the dress from the threatened danger,

and Harriet continue, 'well, and what do you wear to-morrow, Kate?'



'white muslin, with pink ribbon,' say Katherine.



'I have a green and orange striped mousseline de laine, Mamma give only

fifteen-pence a yard for it; I will shew it to you when Lucy come to

it, and you will see if it be not a bargain.  and what bonnet?'



'Straw, with ribbon like our sash,' say Katherine.  'oh! we have so

much trouble to get--'



'my bonnet be green satin,' say Harriet, 'but if I have be you, Kate,

I would have have Leghorn.  wouldn't you, Lucy?'



'five Leghorn bonnet would have cost too much,' say Katherine, 'and

Mamma wish we all to be alike.'



'ah! she would not let you be smart than her own girl, eh, Kitty?'

say Harriet, laugh.



'I have be oblige to buy a very nice new straw bonnet at dykeland,'

say Helen, 'and it, would have be a pity not to use that.'



'well, I have no notion of a whole row of sister be force to dress

alike,' say Harriet; 'Aunt Mildred might--'



here Lucy stop her sister's speech, by bring the gown forward to

display it.  when Harriet have sufficiently explain its excellence she

begin, 'so your cousin, young Merton, be come, be he?'



'yes,' say Katherine, 'we expect he last night, or in the course of

this day, but he have not come yet.'



'well, what sort of a young fellow be he?' say Harriet.



'very clever indeed,' say Katherine.



'oh! then he will not be in my line at all,' say Harriet; 'those

clever boy be never worth speak to, be they, Lucy?'



'do you like stupid one well?' say Helen.



'Capital, ben't it, Lucy?' cry Harriet; 'I do not mean stupid; I

only mean, clever boy, as they call they, have no fun, they only

read, read for ever, like my brother Allan.'



'I be sure Rupert be full of fun,' say Katherine.



'oh, but he be quite a boy, be not he?' say Harriet.



'Nineteen, and at Oxford,' say Katherine.



'oh! I call that quite a boy--don't you, Lucy?' say Harriet; 'be he

handsome?'



'yes, very,' say Katherine.



'not like his sister, then, I suppose,' say Harriet.



'oh! do not you, think Anne pretty?' say Katherine.



'I do not know--no, too small and pale to suit I,' say Harriet.



'Rupert be not like Anne,' say Katherine, 'he have a very bright pink

and white complexion, and light hair.'



'be he tall?'



'no, not so tall as your brother George, but slighter.  he have have two

of his front tooth knock out by a stone at school,' say Katherine.



'what a fuss they do make about those tooth!' mutter Helen.



'be that the school where Horace be?' say Harriet.



'yes,' say Katherine, 'Sandleford.'



'how you must miss Horace!' say Lucy.



'poor little fellow, yes, that we do,' say Katherine, 'but he be so

riotous, he would pull all my thing to piece.  nobody could manage

he but Lizzie, and she never mind what she have on.'



'what a tear he do make in my frock!' say Harriet, laugh; 'don't

he, Lucy?'



'how tired you look, Lucy,' say Helen, 'I be sure you ought to be in

bed.'



'oh no, I be not very sleepy,' say Lucy, smile.



'I be dead tired, I be sure,' say Harriet, yawning; 'it be so hot in

the railway carriage.'



'cannot the rest of those thing be put away to-morrow morning,

Harriet?' say Helen.
