'oh!' say Harriet, yawning, 'there will not be time; Lucy may as well

do they all now she have begin.  how sleepy I be! we walk about London

all the morning.'



'Come, Helen,' say Katherine, 'it be quite time for we to be go; we

must be up early to-morrow.'









CHAPTER V.





the morning of the twenty-eighth of August be as fine as heart could

wish, and the three sister rise almost as soon as it be light, to

fulfil their promise of attend to all the small nondescript matter

of arrangement, needful when a large party be expect by a family not

much in the habit of receive company.  Katherine, who have quite give

up all thought of equal her eld sister in talent, and who pride

herself on be the useful member of the family, make herself very

busy in the store-room; Helen, arrange the fruit with much taste; and

Elizabeth be up-stair and down, here, there, and everywhere, till it

be difficult to find anything which she have not rectify by labour of

head or hand.



'well,' say she, as she bring Helen a fresh supply of vine leave

from the garden, 'I wonder whether Rupert will come in time.  I shall

be very sorry if he do not, for he have do a great deal for the

church.'



'has he indeed?' say Helen, with an air that express, 'I should not

have think it.'



'o Helen, how can you take so little interest in the church?' say

Elizabeth; 'do not you remember how much trouble Rupert take to find a

pattern for the kneeling-stool, and what a beautiful drawing he send

of those at Magdalen Collegia Chapel?  I be sure he would be very much

vex to miss the Consecration.'



'I suppose he might come if he please,' say Helen; 'but perhaps he

do not choose to get up early enough.'



'that be the first time I ever hear Rupert accuse of indolence,' say

Elizabeth.



'I do not mean that he do not generally get up in good time,' say

Helen; 'he be not lazy; but I do not think he choose to put himself

out of the way; and besides, he rather like to make people anxious

about he.'



'I know you have never like Rupert,' say Elizabeth drily.



'Papa think as I do,' say Helen; 'I have hear he say that he be a

spoil child, and think too much of himself.'



'oh! that be only because Aunt Anne work that beautiful waistcoat

for he,' say Elizabeth; 'that be not Rupert's fault.'



'and Papa say that he be quite fond enough already of smart

waistcoat,' say Helen; 'and he laugh at his wear a ring.'



'that be only a blood-stone with his crest,' say Elizabeth, 'and I be

sure no one can accuse Rupert of vulgar smartness.'



'not of _vulgar_ smartness,' say Helen, 'but you must allow that

everything about he have a--kind of--what shall I say?--recherche air,

that seem as if he think a great deal of himself; I be sure you must

have hear Papa say something of the kind.'



'really, Helen,' say Elizabeth, 'I cannot think why you should be

determined to say all that you can against that poor Rupert.'



Helen make no answer.



'I do believe,' say Elizabeth, 'that you have have a grudge against he

ever since he make you an April fool.  oh! how capital it be,' cry

she, sit down to laugh at the remembrance.  'to make you believe

that the beautiful work-box Uncle Edward send you, be a case of

surgical instrument for Mr. Turner, to shew his gratitude for his

attendance upon Rupert when he have the fever, and for set his mouth

to right when his tooth be knock out at school.  oh! there never

be such fun as to see how frightened you look, and how curious Kate

and Horace be, and how Mamma beg he not to open the box and shew

her the horrid thing.'



'I wish Rupert would keep to the truth with his joke,' say Helen.



'Helen,' say Elizabeth, 'you cannot mean to say that he ever say what

be untrue.  you be let yourself be carry much too far by your

dislike.'



'if he do not positively assert what be not true, he often make

people believe it,' say Helen.



'only stupid people, who have no perception of a joke,' say Elizabeth;

'he never deceive I with any joke; it be only that you do not

understand.'



'I wonder how such a candid person as you be, can defend the slight

departure from truth for any purpose,' say Helen.



'I would not defend anyone whom I do not believe to be upright and

open,' say Elizabeth; 'but it be only your slowness, and old spite

against Rupert because he use to joke you, that put these fancy

into your head.  now I must go to the child; I hope, Helen, you will

really enter into the spirit of the day, little as you seem to care

about the church.'



Helen give a deep sigh as her sister leave the room; she be vex at

having be laugh at, at the disregard of her argument, at the

reproach, and perhaps a little at Elizabeth's having take no notice of

the beautiful pyramid of cherry which have cost her half an hour's

labour.



there be some truth in what Helen say of her cousin, though few would

have give his fault so much prominence.  Rupert Merton be an only

son, and very handsome, and this be the history of nearly all his

foible.  no one could say that his career at school, and so far at

college, have not be everything that could be wish, and most people

have nearly as high an opinion of he as he have of himself; but Helen,

who have almost always be make a laughing-stock when he be with she,

have not quite so agreeable a recollection of his lively, graceful,

pleasant manner as her sister have, and be glad to find that his

torment way be not entirely cause by her own querulous temper,

as Elizabeth sometimes tell she they be.



when Mrs. Woodbourne come down, Helen's handiwork receive its full

share of admiration, and Mrs. Woodbourne be much pleased by the girl'

forethought and activity, which have save she from a great deal of

fatigue.



the breakfast be quickly finish, and immediately afterwards the four

eld Miss Woodbournes, together with Anne, go to the school to see

if the child be ready to go to church.  it be pleasant to see the

smile courtesying row of girl, each with her Prayer-book in her

hand, reply to Elizabeth's nod, greeting, and question, with

bright affectionate look, or a few word, which shew that they be

conscious of the solemnity of the service in which they be about to

bear a part.



Elizabeth leave her sister and Anne to assist the school-mistress in

marshal they on their way to church, and return home to fetch

Edward and Winifred, whom she have engage to take with she.  she find

that nearly all the party be go, and report say that the Bishop

have arrive at the house of Mr. Somerville, who be to be curate of St.

Austin's.  Winifred and Edward be watch for she at the door, in

great dread of be forget, for they say, 'Papa have come for

Mamma, and fetch she away in a great hurry, and then Harriet and Lucy

set off after they, and Uncle Edward have take Aunt Anne long before to

look at the church.'  Elizabeth be rejoice in the prospect of a

quiet walk with the child, and be only delay in a vain attempt

to reduce the long finger of Winifred's glove to something more like

the length of the short finger of its owner, when a sharp voice at the

top of the stair cry out, 'wait for I!' and Mrs. Hazleby appear,

look very splendid in a short black silk cloak trim with scarlet.



'where have you be all this time?' say she to Elizabeth, while she

catch hold of Winifred's hand, or, more properly speak, of her

wrist; 'we shall all be too late.'



'I have be at the school,' say Elizabeth.



'what! do you keep school to-day?' ask Mrs. Hazleby.



'no,' say Elizabeth, 'but the child be go to the Consecration.'



'poor little thing!' exclaim Mrs. Hazleby; 'how will they sit out

such a service?'



'none under seven year old be to be there,' say Elizabeth, 'and of

the old one only those who be tolerably good; and I should think

they could join in the service sufficiently to prevent they from

find it tedious.'



'well, I hope so,' say Mrs. Hazleby, in a voice which mean, 'what

nonsense!'  'how steep the hill be!' add she presently; 'what a

fatigue for old people!'



'it be not nearly so steep on the other side,' reply Elizabeth, 'and

the people on this side have the old church.'



'why do they choose such an exposed situation?' continue Mrs.

Hazleby; 'so hot in summer, and so cold in winter.'



'there be no other open piece of ground to be have near enough to the

new town,' answer Elizabeth, keep to herself an additional reason,

which be, that tradition say that there have once be a little chapel

dedicate in the name of St. Augustine, on the site of the new church.

Mrs. Hazleby be silent for a few moment, when, as they come in sight

of what be pass at the top of the hill, she see a gentleman hasten

across the church-yard, and ask who he be.



'Mr. Somerville, the new curate,' be the answer.



'what! another curate?  I think Mr. Walker might have be enough!'

exclaim Mrs. Hazleby.



'Papa do not think so,' say Elizabeth drily.



'well, I suppose that be another hundred a year out of Mr. Woodbourne's

pocket,' say Mrs. Hazleby; 'enough to ruin his family.'



'I be sure,' say Elizabeth, begin to grow angry, 'Papa have rather

do his duty as a clergyman, than lay up thousand for we.'



'fine talk for young thing,' say Mrs. Hazleby; 'besides, it be

nothing to you, you three eld one will be well enough off with your

mother's fortune.'



Elizabeth be more annoyed and provoke by this speech than by anything

Mrs. Hazleby have ever say to she before; her cheek burn with

indignation, and something which feel very like shame, but her bonnet

conceal they, and she attempt no reply.  Mrs. Hazleby begin talk

to Winifred about her new sash, and criticize Elizabeth's dress; and

though Elizabeth could have wish Winifred's mind to have be

occupy with other thing at such a time, yet she be glad of the

opportunity this diversion give she to compose herself before enter

the church.



almost everyone who have ever join in our beautiful Consecration

Service, can imagine the feeling of some of the party from the

Vicarage--can figure to themselves Mrs. Woodbourne's quiet tear;

Dora's happy yet awe-strike face; Anne sympathize with everyone,

rather than feel on her own account; can think of the choke

overwhelming joy with which Elizabeth look into little Edward's

wonder eye, as the name of their father be read, the first among

those who petition the Bishop to set that building apart from all

ordinary and common use; can feel, or perhaps have know, the

exultation with which she join in the Psalms, and the swelling of

heart as she follow the prayer for a blessing on the family of

those who have be the mean of the building of that House.  but we

must go no far; for, such thought and scene be too high to be

more than touch upon in a story of this kind; therefore we will only

add, that Winifred and Edward behave quite as well as Elizabeth have

engage that they should do, only begin to yawn just before the end

of the service.



after they have return from the church, the luncheon at the vicarage

give ample employment to Elizabeth's hand, and nearly enough to her

thought, in carve cold chicken, and do the honour of Merton Hall

peach, at the side-table; and she be very glad, when at three

o'clock the company adjourn to the quadrangle, to see the

school-child's feast.



the quadrangle be enclose on the north side by the old church, on the

south and west by the alm-house, and on the east by the low wall of

the Vicarage garden; there be a wide gravel path all round the court,

and here table be spread, around which be to be see the merry

face of all the child of the two school--the boy, a uniform rank

array in King Edward's blue coat and yellow stocking, with but a

small proportion of modern-look youth in brown or blue, and deep

white collar--the girl, a long party-colour line, only resemble

each other in the white tippet, which have lately encumber

Elizabeth's room.



much activity be call for, from all who choose to take part in

supply the child; the young lady' basket of bun be rapidly

empty, and Mr. Somerville's great pitcher of tea frequently drain,

although he pretend to be very exclusive, and offer his service to

none but the child of St. Austin's, to whom Winifred introduce he.

the rest of the company walk round the cloister, which be cover

with dark red rose and honeysuckle, talk to the old people,

admire their flower, especially Mr. Dillon's dahlia, and watch

the troop of child, who look like a live flower-bed.



Mrs. Hazleby chance to be stand near Mrs. Bouverie, a lady who

live at some distance from Abbeychurch, and who be go to stay and

be one of Mr. Woodbourne's daughter.



'oh yes,' say Mrs. Hazleby, 'the eld of they.'



'she have a remarkably fine countenance,' say Mrs. Bouverie.



'do you admire she?' say Mrs. Hazleby; 'well, I never could see

anything so remarkably handsome in Lizzie Woodbourne.  too thin, too

sharp, too high-colour; Kate be twenty time pretty, to say nothing

of the little one.'



'I should not call Miss Woodbourne pretty,' say Mrs. Bouverie, 'but I

think her brow and eye exceedingly beautiful and full of expression.'



'oh yes,' cry Mrs. Hazleby, 'she be think vastly clever, I assure

you, though for my part I never could see anything in she but pertness.'



'she have not the air of be pert,' say Mrs. Bouverie.



'oh! she can give herself air enough,' say Mrs. Hazleby; 'my poor

they say.'



'so I be think,' say Mrs. Bouverie, look at Elizabeth, who be

request.



Mrs. Bouverie speak in a tone so different from that which Mrs. Hazleby

expect, that even she find that she have go too far, and

mother, and quite as delicate, poor girl.'



'be she indeed?' say Mrs. Bouverie, in a tone of great interest.



'yes, that she be, scarcely ever without a cough.  full of spirit, you

see--rather too, much of it; but I should not be surprised any day--'



at this moment Winifred come run up, to cry, 'look, Aunt Hazleby,

at the basket of ball; I have be to the house to fetch they, and now

the boy be go away to the cricket-ground, and the girl be to

have a famous game at play.'



Mrs. Hazleby only say, 'hm,' but the other lady pay more attention to

the little girl, who be very little troubled with shyness, and soon

be very happy--throw the ball to the girl, and--at the same

that she should not be puzzle at Church to-day; Lizzie say she have

that she and Anne be the last to come in-door to dress.



they be surprised on come in to find Helen sit on the last step

Elizabeth look up rather fiercely; but, kiss her little sister,

'and be you, not my sister?' ask Dora, hold firmly by the hand

'oh yes, yes, Dora!' cry Helen, 'we be your sister, only not quite,

the same as Winifred.'



'and have you no mamma, really no mamma?' continue Dora looking

'not really, Dora; but your mamma be quite the same to we as if she

'she seem to enjoy it, and enter into it as much as any of we,' say

'ah! well, some people's child be vastly clever,' say Mrs.

Helen reply very courteously, by an offer to go and look for he. he

'yes,' say Helen, 'because Dora come to I in her distress, and would

'how come Mrs. Hazleby to begin upon it?' say Elizabeth; 'be it from

'I can hardly tell,' say Helen, 'I be not there at first; I rather

'why, I believe it arise from her see Uncle Edward play with

'Horrid woman!' say Elizabeth, blush deeply.



'my dear Lizzie,' say Anne, laugh, 'do you hope he will not?'



'Nonsense, Anne,' say Elizabeth, laugh too; 'but I hope you quite

give up the hazleby after this speciman.'



'now, Lizzie,' say Helen, 'that be quite in your unjust sweeping style

of censuring.  you do not mean to say that Lucy, or the Major, or the

boy, be disagreeable.'



'root and branch, they be all infect,' say Elizabeth; 'who could

help it, live with Mrs. Hazleby?'



'pray do not be so unfair, Lizzie,' continue Helen; 'I be sure that

'by way of foil, I suppose,' say Elizabeth; 'still, save your

'really, Lizzie,' say Helen, 'it be not like you to blame poor Lucy

'well,' say Elizabeth impatiently, 'I do allow that she be a redeem

'I never call she any such thing!' exclaim Helen; 'but you will

'I be sorry I have vex you, Helen,' say Elizabeth, more kindly; and

'indeed, Lizzie,' say Anne, 'I cannot think why you argue against

'because I cannot bear Helen's sententious decide manner,' say

'but,' say Anne, 'do you not exaggerate the exaggeration, and so put

'you mean by turn her string of superlative into a paragon of

Anne do not see that her cousin might not have help it, but she

think she have say enough on the subject, and let it pass.



'now, Anne,' say Elizabeth, presently after, 'what strange people we

'indeed it be wonderful happiness,' say Anne, her eye fill with

look back to it more when we be far from it.'

a misty day, from a great distance.  it be about the middle of the

scaffold pole and unfinished roughness lose sight of in the distance.

I never see a more beautiful sight.'



'and do you expect that distance of time will conceal all blemish as

well as distance of place?' say Anne.



'yes, unless I take a telescope to look at they with,' answer

Elizabeth; 'perhaps, Anne, in thirty year time, if we both live so

could have be vex by anything at such a time.'



'you like look forward,' say Anne; 'I suppose I be too happy, for I

be afraid to look forward; any change of any sort must bring sorrow

'I suppose you be right,' say Elizabeth; 'that be, I believe the

safe frame of mind to be that which resign itself to anything that

may be appoint for it, rather than that which make scheme and

project for itself.'



'oh! but, Lizzie,' say Anne, 'I do not mean that.  mine be rather an

indolent frame, which do not scheme, because my present condition be,

I do believe, happy than any I could imagine upon earth.  I do not

think that be resignation--there be some thing under which I do not

think I could be resign, at least not with my present feeling.'



'yes, you would, Anne,' say Elizabeth; 'you be just the calm temper

person who would rise up to meet the trial in peace.--But I do not know

before--that bright vision be my great delight.  I like to fancy what

Horace and Edward may be, I like to imagine my own mind grow old, I

like to consider what I shall think of the thing that occupy we now.

but then I be not likely to be disappoint, even if my castle in the

'oh! do not say so, my dear Lizzie,' cry Anne; 'I cannot bear it.'



'indeed, Anne,' say Elizabeth, 'I do not mean to say anything which

could shock you.  I only touch upon what you must have know half

your life, and what Mrs. Hazleby have take good care that I should not

forget.  I be perfectly well now, and have nothing the matter with I;

but then I know that a little illness have a great effect upon I, and

my cold be much soon catch than cure.'



before Anne could answer, there be a knock at the door, and Lady

Merton's maid appear, ready to dress her young lady for the evening;

and thus the conversation end.



the girl be to drink tea in the inner drawing-room, as soon as the

company be go into the dining-room; and Anne and Elizabeth wait

to come down-stair till dinner have begin.



as soon as they enter the room, Harriet begin to admire the lace

dark hair too! it want a little colour to relieve it; why do you not

wear a red band in it, like mine?'



'I think this way of wear it save time,' say Elizabeth; 'but I

believe I shall curl it again.'



'indeed I hope you will; you have no notion how thin it make you

look,' say Harriet.



'of course I must look thin if I be thin,' say Elizabeth, a good deal

annoy by Harriet's pertinacity.



'thin you be, indeed,' continue Harriet, take hold of her wrist.

perhaps, that however thin the arm might look, her own broad ruddy hand

would hardly bear a comparison with Elizabeth's long slender white

finger, and return to the subject of the hair, shake her profusion

of ringlet.



'and straight hair be all the fashion now, but I think it give a

terrible dowdy look.  only that do not signify when you be not

out.--By-the-bye, Miss Merton, be you out?'



'I shall not be seventeen these three month,' say Anne.



'well, I be not seventeen yet, nor near it,' pursue Harriet; 'but I

always dine out, and at home too.  don't I, Lucy?'



Elizabeth do not think it necessary to make any apology for Harriet's

'oh, law! Kate,' say Harriet, 'never mind; I do not mind it a bit, I

'if you consider that dining constitute be out, I generally be,'

'I do not know,' reply Elizabeth.



'oh, then, you be not,' say Harriet; 'everyone know who be out: I

one for the blue, one for the bran.'



'I be glad you put the blue after the bread,' say Anne; 'there be

Elizabeth be too far advanced in her career of nonsense to be easily

'I should be content, if the foolish people would be raise by the

'if people be really wise, they will not let themselves be debase,'

Helen glance towards Lucy, Elizabeth catch her eye, and smile in a

Harriet and Katherine, who have not be much interested by this

as soon as the tea-thing be remove, Elizabeth and Anne go to

'do you ever hear anything so vulgar?' say she.

'still the reverse of wrong be not always right,' say Anne.



they now find themselves at the nursery door, and summon the

Mrs. Woodbourne call Elizabeth, and introduce she as 'my eld

altogether as different a style of person as could well be imagine.



not much prepossess in favour of the party in general by this

one of the window, and there begin to meditate, as be her usual

know by heart, into English, far more literal than Sir Walter Scott's,

and with no fault, except that not above half the couplet profess to

oppress by a Norman baron, and go to the Crusades; and at another

young Miss Maynard, a shy girl, yet pleased with notice, by a

conversation, which, if not very interesting, save she from belong

to any of the four agreeable tribe mention at tea-time.



now, Anne, though she do not posse the tall figure or strike

countenance of her cousin, the Woodbournes, or the brilliant

complexion of her brother, be one of those people who always look

well.  she be small and slightly make, and very graceful; and

everything she wear be appropriate and become, so that, without

bestow much thought on the matter, she never look otherwise than

perfectly well dressed.  she be rather pale; her eye be grey, with

long dark lash; and her hair brown; her feature be well form and

animate; and though by no mean remarkable, everyone call she

nice-look; some say she be pretty, and a few thought and feel that

her countenance be lovely.  so much have lately be say about

dress--about Elizabeth's curl, and Helen's tail, and Anne's

lace--that, wonderful to say, it be the readiest subject Elizabeth

could find to meditate upon.  as she look at her cousin's white

muslin frock, with its border of handsome moravian work, and its

delicate blue satin ribbon, at her well arranged hair, and pretty

mosaic brooch, she enter upon a calculation respect the portion of

a woman's mind which ought to be occupy with her dress--a mental

process, the result of which might perhaps have prove of great benefit

to herself, and ultimately to Dora and Winifred, have it not be

suddenly cut short in the midst by a pierce scream from the latter

young lady, who have be play on the floor with Edward and Fido.



Mrs. Woodbourne instantly catch up the little girl in her arm, and

sit down on the sofa with she on her lap, while Winifred bury she

hand in her pocket-handkerchief, scream and sob violently. Fido

slunk away under the sofa; and Elizabeth hastily make her way through

the circle of lady who surround Mrs. Woodbourne.



'that be what come of teaze he,' say Mrs. Hazleby reproachfully to

Edward; who answer in a loud voice, 'I be sure I do not make he do

it.'



Elizabeth kneel down by Mrs. Woodbourne, and begin to unroll the

handkerchief in which Winifred have wind up her hand; but she be

prevent by a fresh scream from the patient.



'oh! my dear, never mind, do not cry; come, be a brave woman,' say

poor Mrs. Woodbourne, her voice quiver with alarm.



'poor little dear!' exclaim Mrs. Dale, 'she bear it like a little

angel; but it be quite a severe bite.'



'Mamma,' say Elizabeth, rise, 'I think she have well come up-stair

with I.  do not you come, Mamma; I will send for you, if--if it be

more than a scratch.'



she take Winifred in her arm and carry she off, follow by Mrs.

Dale, Miss Maynard, Harriet, Katherine, and Dora, the last-mention

look quite pale with fright.



'if you please,' say Elizabeth, turn round at the foot of the

stair, 'I can manage she well alone.'



she gain her point, though at the expense of politeness.  Mrs. Dale

and Miss Maynard retreat, and Harriet and Katherine follow in their

train.  Dora look inquiringly at her eld sister.



'yes, Dora, you may come,' say she, run up-stair to her own room,

where she shut the door, and set Winifred on her foot again. 'well,

winifre, let we see,' say she cheerfully, 'be you much hurt?'



'it bleed,' say Winifred, withhold her hand.



'not very much,' say Elizabeth, remove the handkerchief, and washing

off the blood, which have be more the cause of the scream than the

pain.  she soon satisfy herself and her sister that the bite be

scarcely more than a scratch; and a piece of sticking-plaster, fetch

by Dora, whose ready eye and clear thoughtful head have already make she

the good finder in the family, have cover the wound before Mrs.

Woodbourne come up to satisfy herself as to the extent of the injury.

Winifred have by this time be divert from the contemplation of she

misfortune by the fitting on of the sticking-plaster, and by

admiration of Anne's bright rose-wood dressing-box, and be full of the

delight of discover that A. K. M., engraven in silver upon the lid,

stand for Anne Katherine Merton, when her mamma come in.  it appear

that the little girl and her brother have be play rather too

roughly with Fido, and that he have revenge himself after the usual

fashion of little dog, especially of those not come to year of

discretion.  Winifred be quite ready to assure her mamma that he have

scarcely hurt she, and that she be very sorry she have cry so much.

Mrs. Woodbourne and Elizabeth, however, agree that it would be well

for she to appear no more that evening, and Dora undertake to keep she

company in the nursery--glad, as Elizabeth could see, to escape from

the presence of Aunt Hazleby, who have sink much in Dora's good grace

since her conversation with she in the afternoon.



'if people would but let child alone,' say Elizabeth, as the two

little girl depart hand in hand; 'it put I out of all patience to

see she first make silly by be pity, and then tell she be an

angel.  too bad and too silly, I declare.'



'you should consider a little, my dear, and not speak so hastily,' say

gentle Mrs. Woodbourne; 'they mean it kindly.'



'mistaken kindness,' say Elizabeth, as she open the drawing-room

door.



in a moment they be overwhelm with inquiry for 'the sweet little

sufferer,' as Mrs. Dale call she.



'I only hope there be no fear of the dog's be mad,' observe that

lady.



'oh! there be no danger of that,' say Elizabeth, know how such a

terror would dwell on Mrs. Woodbourne's spirit.  'see, he can drink.'



Mrs. Hazleby have take possession of the cream-jug, which have

accompany the coffee, and be console the offender by pour some

of its content into a saucer for he.



'but I think it be water that mad dog refuse,' say Mrs. Dale.



'mad dog!' cry Mrs. Hazleby, 'he be as mad as I be, I fancy; it be

quite enough to make he bite when Edward there be pull his ear.'



'I do not pull his ear, Aunt Hazleby; I do not make he bite

winifre,' vociferate Edward; 'I tell you so before, Aunt Hazleby, and

you will say so.'



'fine little fellow,' whisper Mrs. Dale, quite loud enough for Edward

to hear she; 'I quite admire his spirit.'



'do not be rude, Edward my dear,' say his mother.



'but Aunt Hazleby will say that I make Fido bite Winifred, Mamma,' say

Edward; 'and I do not, he do it of himself.'



'never mind now, my love, pray be quiet, my dear boy,' say Mrs.

Woodbourne imploringly; and Edward, who be really a very tractable

boy, walk off to his sister Katherine.



Mrs. Dale then seize upon Mrs. Woodbourne, to tell she some horrible

story of hydrophobia; and Elizabeth, in hope of lessen the

impression such story be likely to make on Mrs. Woodbourne's mind,

listen also, sometimes not very courteously correct evident

exaggeration, and at other contradict certain statement.  at

last, just as the subject, fertile as it be, be exhaust, Anne's

go to the piano, and carry off a train of listener, bring Mrs.

Bouverie next to Elizabeth, and she take the opportunity of enter

into conversation with she.



'do you play, Miss Woodbourne?'



'no, I do not,' reply Elizabeth, who particularly dislike this mode

of begin a conversation.



'do not you like music?' continue Mrs. Bouverie.



'I seldom have hear any I like,' say Elizabeth shortly.



'indeed you have be unfortunate,' say Mrs. Bouverie; 'but perhaps

you be not fond of the piano?'



'no,' say Elizabeth, with rather less of the manner of a suspect

criminal examine in sight of the rack; 'I be sick of all the

Abbeychurch piano; I know they all perfectly, and hear nothing else.'



Mrs. Bouverie laugh, and be glad to obtain something like an answer.

'your cousin play very well,' say she.



'yes,' say Elizabeth, 'I like her music well than most people's, and

she do not make a great fuss about it, she play when she think

people like it, and not when they ask only out of politeness, without

care about it.'



'do you think many people ask in that manner?' say Mrs. Bouverie.



'oh yes, everyone,' say Elizabeth; 'what can they do when they see a

disconsolate damsel sit in a corner with nothing to say, and only

longing to be at the piano by way of do something?  it would be too

cruel not to ask she.'



'do you ever do so?' say Mrs. Bouverie, smile.



'no,' say Elizabeth, 'luckily it be no affair of mine yet; but if ever

it be, there would be a hard struggle between my politeness and

sincerity.'



'sincerity would be most likely to gain the day,' think Mrs.

Bouverie.  'perhaps,' say she, 'you be not a fair judge of other

people's sincerity, since you do not like music yourself.'



'I think,' say Elizabeth, 'that even if I do play, I could see in

people's face whether they mean what they say; that be, if vanity

and love of applause do not blind I.'



Mrs. Bouverie be silent for a moment, and then say, 'well, I must

say, I be disappointed to find that you do not play.'



Elizabeth remember how well her mother have, play, and it be plain

to she that Mrs. Bouverie be notice she for her mother's sake.  she

look down and colour as she reply, 'both my sister be musical,

and Helen be say to be likely to sing very well.  I believe the

history of my want of music to be,' add she, with a bright smile,

'that I be too naughty to learn; and now, I be afraid--I be not sorry

for it, as it would have take up a great deal of time, and two singing

sister be surely enough for one family.'



'I be in hope of hearing,' say Mrs. Bouverie, 'that you have train

your school-child to sing the sixty-fifth Psalm as nicely as they

do to-day.  I be sure their teacher must have come from the vicarage.'



'no,' say Elizabeth, 'it be the school-master who teach they.

perhaps, if Helen have not be from home so long, she might have help

the girl, but when she come home three week ago, it be hardly worth

while for she to begin.  that be the only reason I ever wish to

understand music.'



Mrs. Bouverie now begin talk to she about the church and its

architecture, and of the child, in exactly the way that Elizabeth

like, and in half an hour she see more of Elizabeth's true self than

Miss Maynard have ever see, though she have know she all her life. Miss

Maynard have see only her roughness.  Mrs. Bouverie have find her way

below it.   Elizabeth be as sincere and open as the day, although from

seldom meet with anyone who could comprehend or sympathize with her

idea, her manner have acquire a degree of roughness and reserve,

difficult to penetrate, and anything but attractive, suit ill with

her sweet smile and beam eye.  she be talk quite happily and

confidentially to Mrs. Bouverie, when she catch Mrs. Woodbourne's eye,

and see she look anxious, she remember Winifred's disaster, and

take the first opportunity of hasten up-stair to see whether the

little girl's hand be still in as favourable a state as when she leave

she.



a few moment after she have quit the room, Sir Edward Merton

approach Mrs. Bouverie, and take the place beside she, which

Elizabeth have lately occupy.



'I hope Elizabeth have be gracious to you, as I see you have be so

kind as to talk to she,' say he, smile.



'oh, I hope we be become good friend,' say Mrs. Bouverie; 'I have

seldom see so young a girl shew as much mind as your niece.'



'I be very glad to hear you say so,' say Sir Edward, 'for she be apt

to be rather more reserved with stranger than could be wish.'



'perhaps she do not consider I as an entire stranger; I remember

see she once when a most engaging little child of four or five year

old,' say Mrs. Bouverie; 'and now I hope our acquaintance will

continue.  shall we see she at Marlowe Court to-morrow, as I believe we

meet you there?  of course we shall see Miss Merton?'



'no, I believe not,' say Sir Edward; 'we be rather too large a number

without the girl, who really form quite a troop by themselves.'



'I like to see your daughter and Miss Woodbourne together,' say Mrs.

Bouverie; 'I be sure they must be great ally.'



'yes,' say Sir Edward, 'there be a tolerably strong cousinly

friendship between they: Anne have a wholesome feeling of inferiority,

which make she rather proud of her cousin's preference.'



'do you not think Miss Woodbourne very like her mother?' say Mrs.

Bouverie.  'I know she immediately by the resemblance.'



'very--very like she, a little darker certainly,' say Sir Edward, 'but

she remind I of she constantly--there--that smile be my sister's

exactly.'



Elizabeth have just then re-enter the room, and be assure her mamma

that Winifred have be as playful as ever all the remainder of the

evening, and be now fast asleep in bed.



'I be only afraid she be too fragile and delicate a creature,' say

Mrs. Bouverie; 'be her health strong?'



'strong? no, not very,' say Sir Edward, 'she require care, but there

be nothing much amiss with she; I know most people about here be in

the habit of lament over she as in a most dangerous state; but I

believe the fact be, that Mrs. Woodbourne be a nervous anxious person,

and frighten herself more than there be any occasion for.'



'then I hope she generally look less delicate than she do to-night,'

say Mrs. Bouverie.



'oh! she may well look over-work to-night,' say Sir Edward; 'she have

a spirit in she which would not let she rest on such a day as

this.--Come here, Miss Lizzie,' say he, beckon to she, 'I want you

to account for those two red spot upon your cheek.  do you think they

ought to be there?'



'yes, if they come in a good cause, Uncle,' say Elizabeth.



'do you mean, then, to wear they any long than necessary?' say Sir

Edward; 'pray have you sit still for five minute together to-day?'



'yes, while I be at tea,' say Elizabeth.



'and why be not you in bed and asleep at this moment?' ask her uncle.



'that be the very question Mamma have be ask,' say Elizabeth; 'and

I have be promise to depart, as soon as I can make my escape; so

good night, Uncle Edward--good night,' say she, give her hand to her

uncle and to Mrs. Bouverie with almost equal cordiality.



'good night, Lizzie, get you go,' say Sir Edward; 'and if you can

carry off my girl with you, I shall be all the well pleased.'



Elizabeth succeed in touch Anne's arm; and the two cousin flit

away together, and soon forget the various delight and annoyance of

the day in sleep.









CHAPTER VI.





the next morning be gloomy and rainy, as Elizabeth inform Anne at

about seven o'clock; 'and I be not sorry for it,' say she, 'for I want

to have you all to myself at home, so we will turn the incubi over to

Kate and Helen, and be comfortable together.'



'will they submit to such treatment?' say Anne.



'oh yes, my dear,' say Elizabeth; 'they want we as little as we want

they; they only want a little civility, and I will not be so sparing of

that useful commodity as I be yesterday evening.  and now, Anne, I be

go to beg your pardon for be so excessively rude to Harriet, as I

be last night.  she do not mind it, but you do, and much more than

if it have be to yourself.'



'I believe I do,' say Anne; 'other people do not know what you mean

when you set up your bristle, and I do.  besides, I be sorry for

Lucy, who look as if she have sensitiveness enough for the whole

family.'



'Poor Lucy!' say Elizabeth;





          "a weary lot be thine, fair maid,

             a weary lot be thine."





yes, Lucy have very deep feeling; you may see it in the painful flushing

of her cheek, and the downcast look of her eye, when her mother and

sister expose themselves.  I really believe that that poor girl have

more to endure than most people.'



'o lizzie,' say Anne, 'how differently you speak of she yesterday!'



'yes,' say Elizabeth, 'but then I be furious with Mrs. Hazleby; and

besides, I believe the truth be, that I be very tired and very cross,

not exactly the way in which I intend to conclude the Consecration

day; and now I be in my sense, I be very sorry I behave as I do.

but, Anne, though I hereby retract all I say in dispraise of Lucy, and

confess that I be rude to Harriet, do not imagine that I disavow all I

say about society last night, for I assure you that I express my

deliberate opinion.'



'your deliberate opinion, my dear?' say Anne, laugh.



'yes, my deliberate opinion, my dear,' repeat Elizabeth.  'pray why

should not I have a deliberate opinion, as well as Hannah More, or

Locke on the Human Understanding, or anyone else?'



'because,' rejoin Anne, 'I think that if the rest of the world be

of your deliberate opinion, there would soon be a lock on the human

understanding.'



'I be sure I think there be at present,' return Elizabeth; 'do you

see Aunt Anne last night waste upon Mrs. Dale, oblige to listen to

the dull stuff that ever be invent, and poor Mamma frighten out

of her wit?  I should not wonder if she have dream of mad dog all

night.'



'I do not defend Mrs. Dale's power of intellect,' say Anne, 'but I

should have think that you at least have little reason to complain.

you be very well off next to Mrs. Bouverie.'



'oh! Mrs. Bouverie be a rara avis, an exception to the general rule,'

say Elizabeth; 'but you know, she or my uncle, or aunt, or Papa, be

generally force to put a lock on their understanding.  why, Anne, what

be you laugh at?'



'Lizzie, I beg your pardon,' say Anne, try to check herself, 'but I

could not help it.  your speech put I in mind of the print from

Albano's four element.  do not you remember Juno's visit to AEolus,

where he be open the door of a little corner cupboard where he keep

the puff-cheek wind lock up?  do you mean to say that Mamma keep

her mighty power of mind lock up in the same way, for fear they

should burst out and overwhelm everybody?'



Elizabeth heartily join in her cousin's merriment.  'I will tell you

what I do mean, Anne, what the great law of society be.  now, do not

put on that absurd face of mock gravity, or I shall only laugh, instead

of argue properly.'



'well, let we hear,' say Anne.



'it be almost more important than the law that you must eat with a

knife and fork,' say Elizabeth.  'there be one level of conversation,

fit for the mean capacity; and whoever venture to transgress it, be

instantly call blue, or a horrid bore, &c., &c.'



'Nonsense, Lizzie,' say Anne, laugh; 'I be sure I have hear plenty

of clever people talk, about sensible thing too, and never do I hear

they call bore, or blue, or any of your awful et cetera either.'



'because people do not dare to do so,' say Elizabeth, 'but they

think it all the same.'



'what do you mean by people?' say Anne.



'the dull, respectable, common-place gentry, who make up the mass of

mankind,' say Elizabeth.



'do they?' say Anne.



'do not they?' say Elizabeth.



'I do not know what the mass of mankind may be at Abbeychurch,' say

Anne, 'but I be sure the people whom we see oftenest at home, be such

as I think it a privilege to know.'  and she begin to enumerate these

friend.



'oh! Anne,' interrupt Elizabeth, 'do not, for pity's sake, make I

discontent; here be I in Abbeychurch, and must make the good of it. I

must be as polite and hypocritical as I can make myself.  I must waste

my time and endure dullness.'



'as to waste of time,' say Anne, 'perhaps it be most usefully employ

in what be so irksome as you find be in company.  Mamma have always

wish I to remember, that acquire knowledge may after all be but a

selfish gratification, and many thing ought to be attend to first.'



'that doctrine would not do for everybody,' say Elizabeth.



'no,' say Anne, 'but it do for we; and you will see it plainer, if

you remember on what authority it be say that all knowledge be

profitable for nothing without charity.'



'Charity, yes,' say Elizabeth; 'but christian love be a very different

thing from drawing-room civility.'



'not very different from bear and forbearing, as Helen say,'

answer Anne.



'politeness be not great enough,' say Elizabeth, 'to belong to

charity.'



'you be not the person to say so,' say Anne.



'because I dislike it so much,' say Elizabeth, 'but that be because I

despise it.  it be such folly to sit a whole evening with your hand

before you do nothing.'



'but do you not think,' say Anne, 'that endure restraint, and

listen to what be not amusing, for the sake of please other, be

do something?'



'passively, not actively,' say Elizabeth; 'but it be not to please

other, it be only that they may think you well breed, or rather that

they may not think about you at all.'



'it be to please our father and mother,' say Anne.



'yes, and that be the reason it must be do,' say Elizabeth; 'it be

the way of the world, and cannot be help.'



'rather say it be the trial which have be ordain for we,' say Anne.



'well,' say Elizabeth, smile, 'I know all the time that you have the

good of the argument.  it would not be so if it be not good for we.'



'and as it be,' say Anne, 'I believe that there be more enjoyment in

the present order of thing, than there would be in any arrangement we

could devise.'



'oh! doubtless,' say Elizabeth, 'just as the corn ripen well with

all the disaster that seem to befall it, than it would if we have the

command of the cloud.'



'of course,' say Anne, 'you really be a much more reasonable creature

than you pretend to be, Lizzie.'



'be I?' say Elizabeth.  'well, I will just tell you my great horror,

and I suppose you will laugh at I.  I can endure gossip for old people

who cannot employ themselves, and must talk, and have nothing to talk

of but their neighbour; but only think of those wretched _fainéant_

who go chatter on, waste their own time and other people's, do

no good on the face of the earth, and a great deal of harm.'



'but these unfortunate be probably quite as unable to talk on any

very wise subject, as your beloved old people, to whom you give a

license to gossip,' say Anne; 'and you do not wish to condemn they to

perpetual silence.  they be most likely to be estimable people, who

ought to be amuse.'



'estimable--yes, perhaps,' say Elizabeth, 'but then I cannot esteem a

silly gossip.'



'why, Lizzie,' cry Anne, 'you be still at the old story that it be

well to be wicked than stupid; at least, you reason upon that

foundation, though you do not really think so.'



'I believe,' say Elizabeth, 'that there must be some great crook in my

mind; for though I know and believe as firmly as I do any other

important thing, that mere intellect be utterly worthless, I cannot

feel it; it bewitch I as beauty do some people, and I suppose

always will, till I grow old and stupid, or get my mind into well

order.'



'really,' say Anne, 'I think the strong proof of your beginning to

grow old and stupid, be your do such a very common-place thing, as

to abuse honest gossip.'



there be service at St. Mary's Church on Wednesday and Friday

morning; but on this day the rain be so violent, that of all the

party at the Vicarage, the Mertons, and Elizabeth, Katherine, and

Helen, alone venture to go to church.



when they return, Anne follow her mother to her room, to talk over

the event of the previous day.  after much have be say of the

Consecration, and also of their wonder and regret at Rupert's absence,

Anne say, 'how strange it seem to lose sight of you and Papa as I

have do ever since I have be here!  Mamma, I have scarcely be

with you at all, and never see Papa but when he be talk to Uncle

Woodbourne, and everyone else be in the room.'



'but I hope you be enjoy yourself, my dear?' say Lady Merton.



'oh yes, Mamma,' cry Anne; 'Lizzie be more delightful than ever, when

we be alone.'



'be you take a sudden romantic turn?' say Lady Merton, smile; 'do

you mean in future to keep one friend all to yourself?'



'oh no, Mamma,' say Anne, laugh; 'I only mean that Lizzie be more

like herself when we be alone together.  sometimes when the other be

there, she get vex, and say thing which I do not like to hear,

only for the sake of differ from they.'



'I have see something of the kind about she before,' say Lady Merton,

'but not enough to be unpleasant.'



'no, Mamma, because you do not talk as Miss Hazleby do yesterday,'

say Anne, smile.  'she certainly do make a very ridiculous oration

about officer and flirtation; but Lizzie, instead of put a stop

to it quietly and gently, only go into the other extreme, and talk

about dislike all society.'



'I be very sorry to hear this,' say Lady Merton; 'I be afraid she will

make herself absurd and disagreeable by this spirit of contradiction,

even if nothing bad come of it.'



'it be not all out of a spirit of contradiction,' say Anne, 'though

she say this morning, that she be very tired and very cross yesterday

evening.  but, Mamma, she also say that she think the time she spend

in company waste, and she really believe that no one dare to talk

sense, or that if he do, everyone dislike he.'



'that be only a little unconscious affectation of be wise than

other people, assist by live in a place where there be the usual

complement of dull people, and where her father's situation prevent

he from associate only with those whom he would prefer,' say Lady

Merton; 'her good sense will get the well of it.  I be much more

anxious about this spirit of contradiction.'



'yes, it certainly lead she to be very unjust, as she acknowledge this

morning,' say Anne, 'and rather unkind to Helen.  but then it be no

wonder that she be mad with the Hazlebys.'



Anne then tell the history of poor Dora's trouble, and be quite

satisfied with her mother's displeasure at Mrs. Hazleby, and her

admiration of little Dora.



'and what do you think of Helen?' ask she presently.



'I can hardly tell,' say Anne, 'she be still very demure, with very

little of Lizzie's sparkling merriment; indeed, she do not seem in

the least able to enter into a joke.  but then she say some very

sensible thing.  Lizzie say she wonder what we should think of she.

she think she very much improve, but complain that she have lose she

home feeling, and care only for dykeland; I scarcely know what she

mean.'



'I think that I can guess,' say Lady Merton, 'from know a little

more of Mrs. Staunton's character.  she be a very amiable person, and

have in reality, I believe, plenty of good sense; but she have allow

herself to fall into an exaggerated style of feeling and expression,

which, I dare say, bewitch a girl like Helen, and now make she find

home cold and desolate.'



'like the letter which Mrs. Staunton write to you about Rupert, and

which Papa call ecstatic,' say Anne.



'that be an instance of Mrs. Staunton's way of express herself,'

say Lady Merton; 'now I will give you one of her acuteness of feeling,

as she call it.  your Aunt Katherine be her great friend when she

be a girl, though I believe the kind epithet she lavish upon I

would have be enough to stock two or three moderate friendship.  we

all use to walk together, and spend at least one evening in the week

together.  one evening, your aunt, who have a good deal of the same high

careless spirit which you observe in Lizzie, chance to make some

observation upon the rudeness of sailor in general, forget that

Helen Atherley's brother be a sailor.'



'or if she have remember it,' say Anne, 'judge by Lizzie, she would

have say the same thing; she would have take it for grant that the

present company be always except.'



'Captain Atherley be not of the present company,' say Lady Merton,

'he be in the Mediterranean; and it happen that he have not have time

to call at Merton Hall in due form, the last time he have be at home,

so that poor Helen think that this speech be aim at he. she say

nothing at the time; but next morning arrive a note to I, to entreat

I to find out what her darle Henry could possibly have do to

offend dear Katherine Merton, for she should be wretche till she

understand it, and Katherine have forgive she and he.  she assure I

that she have lain awake all night, think it over, and have at last

come to the conclusion that it must be this unfortunate omission, and

she promise to write to dear Henry immediately, to make he send all

possible apology.'



'Poor Captain Atherley!' exclaim Anne; 'and what could my aunt say?'



'unfortunately,' say Lady Merton, 'both she and I have entirely

forget the speech, and could not guess what could have give rise to

Helen's imagination.  after a consultation, I be depute to Helen

with many assurance that Katherine be very sorry, she could not

exactly tell why, but for whatever have grieve Helen; and after a good

deal of kiss and lament on both side, which, I believe,

Katherine consider as a punishment for her inconsiderate speech,

thing be set right again.'



'Inconsiderate, Mamma?' say Anne; 'that seem as if you blame my

aunt, when it seem to I that Mrs. Staunton deserve all the blame for

her excessive folly, and what I should think want of confidence in her

friend's affection.'



'it be certainly very silly,' say Lady Merton; 'but you know, Anne,

that when people have once accustom themselves to get into a habit of

make mountain of mole-hill, they cannot see anything as it really

be.  I think Katherine quite in the right, as you do now, but I

believe she consider that, know as she do the over-sensitiveness

of her friend, she should have be more cautious in what she say.'



'that be the right way for she to take it,' say Anne; 'but I still

think Mrs. Staunton must be an excessively silly person.  of course one

would wish to keep from hurt people's feeling, but it really be

hardly possible to help it, if they will ride out to meet offence in

such a way.'



'yet, Anne,' say her mother, 'you may comfort yourself with know

that as long as you do what be command, set a watch before your lip,

you be not likely to wound the feeling of other, however sensitive.'



'I know, Mamma,' say Anne, 'that would correct every fault of that

kind; but then I hardly know how to do so thoroughly.  and I think

sensitiveness be a good thing--at least, it make people know well

what will hurt other.'



'be sensitive for other, without be ready to take offence for

yourself, then, Anne,' say Lady Merton.  'and now that you have fit

the moral to my story, I must go down and help Mrs. Woodbourne to

entertain Mrs. Hazleby.'



'I pity you,' say Anne.  'if everyone, or indeed if half the world

be like she, I should be more violent in my opinion than Lizzie be.'



'and what be you go to do?' ask Lady Merton.



'I be go to sit in the school-room,' say Anne; 'I have a special

invitation from Dora this morning.'



on go down-stair, Anne find that Katherine and Harriet have go to

spend the morning with the Mrs. Turner mention during the walk to St.

Austin's, as her daughter, Miss Wilhelmina, have engage to teach

Harriet to make wax flower.  Lucy be up-stair, write to Major

Hazleby; and Helen be sit in the school-room, where Elizabeth be

teach the child.  Little Winifred have just finish her lesson,

and be skip off in high glee with her medal round her neck, to

tell her mamma that she have gain four good mark.  Dora be perch

on a high stool, at Elizabeth's desk, with a broadly rule paper before

she, on the top of which the word, 'my dear Horace, St. Austin's

Church be consecrate yesterday,' be to be see in fair round hand.

no more be visible, for the little girl laughingly lay down her rosy

cheek, and all her light wavy curl, flat upon the letter, as  Anne

advance and make a stealthy attempt to profit by the intelligence she

be send to her brother.  Edward be stand by Elizabeth, read

Mrs. Trimmer's Fabulous Histories, for, though five year old, he make

very slow progress in english literature, be more backward in

learn to read than any of the other have be, except Helen.  he

do not like the trouble of spelling, and be in the habit of guess

at every word he do not know; and on his very composedly call old

Joe the gardener, 'the old gander,' Anne burst into an irrepressible

giggle, and Helen, sedate as she be, could not help follow she

example.  they have just compose themselves, when Edward make another

blunder, which set they off again, and Elizabeth, who when alone with

the child, could bear anything with become gravity, also give way.



Edward, find that he be divert they, begin to make absurd

mistake on purpose, so that Elizabeth be force to call he to order.

Anne think it well to leave the room, and Helen follow she, say,

'we have well leave Lizzie to manage he by herself; she always do

well without I.'



'you have never shew I your drawing, Helen,' say Anne; 'I should

like very much to see they, if you will let I.'



'if you please,' say Helen.  'will you come up to my room?  I keep all

my own thing there, out of the way of the critic.'



'what critic?' inquire Anne.



'Lizzie, to be sure, and Papa,' say Helen; 'I think they the severe

people I know.'



'do you indeed!' say Anne.



'do not you?' say Helen; 'do not lizzie say the sharp thing

possible?  I be sure she do to I, and she never like anything I do.

if there be any little fault in it, she and Papa always look at that,

rather than anything else.'



'well,' say Anne, 'it be a comfort that if they like anything you do,

you be sure it be really very good.  their praise be worth more than

that of other people.'



Helen sigh, but make no reply, as by this time they have arrive at

the door of the room which she share with Katherine.  it be a

complete contrast to Elizabeth's; it be large and light, and look

out upon the bright garden, the alm-house, and the church tower.  the

upper part of the window be occupy by Katherine's large cage of

canary bird, and below be a stand of flower-pot, a cactus which

never dream of blossoming, an ice-plant, and a columnia belong to

Katherine, a nourishing daphne of Helen's, and a verbena, and a few

geranium cutting which she have bring from dykeland, look very

miserable under crack tumbler and stemless wine-glass.  on a small

round table be, very prettily arrange, various little knicknack and

curiosity, which Elizabeth always laugh at, such as a glass ship,

which be surround with miniature watering-pot, humming-top, knife

and fork, a tonbridge-ware box, a gold-stud horn bonbonniere, a

Breakwater-marble ruler, several variety of pincushion, a pen-wiper

with a doll in the middle of it, a little dish of money-cowrie, and

another of indian shot, the seed of the mahogany tree, some sea-egg, a

false book make of the wreck of the Royal George, and some piece of

spar and petrifaction which Helen have acquire on an expedition to

Matlock with the staunton.  the book-shelf, however, be to Anne the

most attractive object in the room; and whilst Helen be untie the

string of her portfolio, she go up to it.



'what a beautiful little Bishop Wilson!' exclaim she, take out one

of the book.



'yes,' say Helen with a sigh, 'that be dear Mrs. Staunton's last

present to I before I leave dykeland.  she say that perhaps she

should not see I again before I be confirm, and it be the fit

Godmother's gift she could find.'



'and be this pretty Lady of the Lake yours too?' say Anne; 'what a

pretty binding, with the Douglas arm on it!'



'yes,' say Helen, 'that be Fanny's present; and Jane give I the

pretty forget-I-not brooch I wear yesterday.  you see I have plenty of

keep-sake from the dear people.'



Anne then turn to the portfolio on the table.  Helen shew she, in

the first place, a rather stiff and formal look forget-I-not,

paint by Fanny Staunton, and a carelessly sketch but neatly shade

head draw by Jane, both which specimen of art Anne try hard to

admire for Helen's sake, but could not find it in her heart to do so.

Helen's own drawing, which be landscape, give more promise of

improvement, and display a good deal of taste and freedom of hand,

though some be by no mean correct in the outline. Helen point out

several fault which she candidly acknowledge to be wrong, and some

other which she say 'Lizzie call blunder.'



'there,' say she, 'be the house at dear dykeland; there be my window

with the Banksia rose cluster round it, so that I could gather they

as I stand in my room.  that room be still to be call Helen's.  but

now, Anne, do you think that line ought to be straight? Lizzie say it

should, but I think the perspective alter it; I be sure I see it so.'



'indeed, Helen,' say Anne, 'I think the shadow must have deceive

you.'  and with a little trouble she prove that Elizabeth be right.



'ah!' say Helen, 'if Lizzie would but have shew I patiently, instead

of say, 'why, Helen, cannot you draw a straight line?' I should have

understand she.'  then she continue, while take out India-rubber and

pencil to rectify the mistake, 'I use to draw a great deal at dear

dykeland; we have a sketching master, and use to go out with he twice

a week, but it be very delightful when we three go alone, when one

of we use to read while the other draw. I be sure these sketch will

for ever remind I of those happy day.'



'why, Helen,' say Anne, smile, 'you speak as if you never mean to

be happy again.'



'do I?' say poor Helen; 'I be afraid I do seem rather silly about dear

dykeland.  the other day I be singe



     "my heart's in the Highlands, my heart be not here,

      my heart's in the Highlands, gone chase the deer,"



when in come Lizzie, and say, "no, Helen,



      your heart be at dykeland, your heart's in the bog,

      your heart be at dykeland, gone chase the frog,"



for she be always laugh at it for be so damp, dear place.  and it

be before Horace go to school, and he would do nothing but sing it

at I all day, and make winifred do so too.'



Anne could not help laugh.



'then you too think I absurd,' say Helen; 'but if you only know how

happy I be at dykeland, and how desolate I sometimes feel here, you

would not wonder at I.'



'then you do not like Abbeychurch?' say Anne incredulously; she could

not say 'you be not happy at home.'



'who could prefer a little dismal town to a pleasant house in the

country?' say Helen; 'you like Merton Hall well than this place, do

not you, Anne?'



'of course,' reply Anne; 'but then Merton Hall be my home.'



'and Abbeychurch be mine,' sigh poor Helen.  'I believe it be very

wrong to be discontent with home, but I cannot help it.'



'my dear Helen, what do you mean?' exclaim Anne, quite aghast.



'indeed, Anne,' say Helen, 'I do not wonder that you be shocked, but

you do not know how I feel here.  at Dykelands I feel that people like

I and be pleased with I, but at home nobody want I, nobody care

for I, I be in the way wherever I go.'



'my dear Helen,' cry Anne, 'that must be fancy!'



'I wish it be,' say poor Helen, shake her head.



'but only think,' proceed Anne, 'what you be accuse they of. not

love you, and wish you away.'



'no, I do not say it be as bad as that,' say Helen; 'but I be sure I

be of no use here, and might as well be away.'



'I suppose,' say Anne, 'that you have be so long away as to have

lose all your old home occupation, and you have not yet have time to

make new one.'



'perhaps it be so,' say Helen; 'but I do not think I have any

occupation before I go to dykeland, at least none worth have, and

now I cannot make myself new one.  Lizzie do everything, and will

not let I help she, for fear I should do mischief.'



'now, Helen,' say Anne, who have by this time collect her idea,

which have be completely startle by her cousin's avowal of dislike of

home, 'I will tell you what I think Mamma would say to you.  I think

you use to be indolent and waste your sense, but now dykeland have

give you a spur, and you be very much improve.'



'do you really think so?' interrupt Helen, who have lately feel quite

starve for want of praise.



'yes,' say Anne, 'and so do everyone, and so Lizzie tell I.'



'lizzie?' say Helen; 'I think she consider I as great a baby as

ever.'



'no, no, my dear,' say Anne; 'I will tell you what she say of you.

she say you be almost all she could wish in a sister, and that you

be quite a reflective creature; and that be high praise from she.'



'well, if she think so,' say Helen, 'she do not shew it; she be

always make game of my opinion and feeling.'



'so she do of almost everyone's,' say Anne; 'but that be no proof

that she do not love they.'



'and she will never listen to anything that I say, or take interest in

anything I care for,' continue Helen.



'indeed, Helen, you only think so because you do not understand she

way,' say Anne; 'all last month she could think of nothing but the

Consecration, and Horace's go to school.  now all that be over and

you be quiet again, after we be go you will get on capitally

together.'



'I be sure she contradict every word I say,' say Helen.



'that be not out of unkindness, I assure you,' say Anne, who

unfortunately could not deny that such be the fact.  'she only like

an argument, which sharpen your wit, and do no harm, if both side

be but good-humour and cheerful.  she will find you out in time, and

you will understand she well.'



'oh! Lizzie be delightful when she do not contradict,' say Helen;

'she be clever than anyone I ever see, even than Fanny Staunton, and

Papa say her patience and diligence with Horace be beyond all

praise; but I can never be clever enough for she to make I her friend.'



'but you do not think people choose their friend only for their

cleverness?' say Anne.



'why, no,' say Helen, 'I do not think they ought, but Lizzie do. you

would not be her friend if you be not clever.'



'well,' say Anne, 'but try and convince she that you can be her friend

without be clever, if you will not allow that you be.'



'oh!' say Helen, brighten up, 'if Lizzie would but make a friend of

I, how happy we should be! if she would but talk to I of her own

concern, and listen to mine!  but she never choose to hear I speak

of dykeland.'



'then,' say Anne, 'you must remember that she have never be there,

and do not know the people.'



'yes,' say Helen; 'but I think that if she have be there, and I at

home, I should have listen for her sake, besides that Mrs. Staunton

be our own mamma's dear friend.'



Anne have always think that her own mother have be Aunt Katherine's

dearest friend; but she have forbearance enough to leave the honour to

Mrs. Staunton in Helen's imagination, and answer, 'and for that very

reason, and for your sake too, Helen, she will delight to hear about

Mrs. Staunton when you be quiet together, if you do not give she too

much at a time, or talk of Dykelands when she be think of something

else.  oh yes, Helen, you and Lizzie will be excellent friend, unless

you be much more silly than I think either of you.'



Anne smile so cheerfully, that Helen could not help smile too; but

she would probably have find another sorrow to lament over, if at this

moment Dora have not come up to summon they to their early dinner.



Helen feel exceedingly grateful to Anne for having listen so kindly

and patiently to her list of grievance.  it be the first sympathy, as

she consider, that she have meet with since she have leave dykeland, and

it atone in her mind for various little thoughtless way of Anne's,

which have wound she in former year, and which she have not perhaps

strive sufficiently to banish from her memory; and this be a great

advantage from this conversation, even if she derive no far

benefit from it.



on her side, Anne have some thought of tell Elizabeth what Helen's

feeling really be, in hope that she might shew a little regard for

they; but, sisterless herself, she think the bond of sisterhood too

sacred to be rashly interfere with by a stranger's hand; besides, she

consider Helen's complaint as really confidential, if not expressly

so, and resolve to mention they to no one but Lady Merton, and to

limit her attempt at be useful to bring the two sister before

each other in their most amiable light, and at any rate to avoid say

anything that could possibly occasion a discussion between they, though

she could hardly imagine that it be possible to dislike one of the

merry argument that she delight in. however, remember her

mother's story of Mrs. Staunton, she decide that though it be a great

misfortune for people to have such strange fancy, yet their friend

ought to respect they.









CHAPTER VII.





as soon as dinner be over, Elizabeth go up to her own room, and be

follow in a few moment by Anne, who find she put on her bonnet

and cloak.  'can you be go out in such weather as this?' exclaim

she.



'yes,' say Elizabeth; 'I must



          "let content with my fortune fit,

           though the rain it raineth every day."'





'but what be the fortune which oblige you to go out?' say Anne.



'the fortune of an old woman to whom Kate or I read every Friday,'

say Elizabeth, 'and the fortune of various young school-child, who

must be prepare for Papa or Mr. Walker to catechize in Church on

Sunday.'



'why do not you send Kate or Helen, instead of murder yourself in

the wet?' say Anne.



'Miss Kitty be three inch deep in the mystery of a spencer, (I do

not mean Edmund,)' say Elizabeth, 'and it will not be out of her head

these three day, at least not till she have make Mamma's old black

satin gown into one after Harriet's pattern; I hear she ask for it

as I come up-stair.'



'and would not Helen go?' say Anne; 'she do not catch cold as easily

as you do.'



'Helen have contrive, somehow or other,' say Elizabeth, 'to know no

more about the school-child than if they be so many Esquimaux;

besides, anyone with any experience of Helen's way, have rather walk

ninety mile in the rain, than be at the pain of route she out of

the corner of the sofa to do anything useful.'



'indeed,' say Anne, 'I think Helen do wish to make herself useful.'



'I dare say she sit still and wish it in the abstract, for I think

it must be a very disagreeable thing to reflect that she might as well

be that plaster statue for any good that she do,' say Elizabeth;

'but she grumble at every individual thing you propose for she to do,

just as she say she wish to be a companion to Dora and Winifred, yet

whenever they wish she to play with they or tell they a story, which be

all the companionship child of their age understand, she be always

too much at her ease to be disturb.  and now, as she be the only

person in the house with whom poor Lucy be tolerably at her ease, it

would be cruel to take she away.'



'that be more of a reason,' say Anne; 'what a pity it be that Lucy be

so shy!'



'excessive shyness and reserve be what prevent her mother from be

able to spoil she,' say Elizabeth; 'so do not regret it.'



'still I do not like to see you go out in this way,' say Anne.



'I may truly say that rain never hurt I,' say Elizabeth; 'and if I

once let one trifle stop I in these parish matter, I shall be stop

for ever, and never do anything.  perhaps I shall not come back this

hour and a half, for old Mrs. Clayton must be die to hear all about

our Consecration, luncheon, dinner, &c., and as she be the widow of the

last Vicar, we be in duty bind to be civil to she, and I must go and

call upon she.  oh! you poor thing, I forget how deserted you will be,

and really the drawing-room be almost uninhabitable with that bengal

tiger in it.  here be that delightful Norman Conquest for you to read;

pray look at the part about Hereward the Saxon.'



Elizabeth would not trust herself to stay with Anne any long, and run

down-stair, and might soon be hear put up her umbrella and

shut the front door after she.



Anne find the afternoon pass rather heavily, in spite of the

companionship of William the Conqueror and Hereward the Saxon, of

assist the child in a wet day game of romp, and of shew Dora

and winifre the content of the box they have admire the day before.

Helen and Lucy be sit at work very comfortably in the corner of

the sofa in the inner drawing-room; Harriet and Katherine very busy

contrive the spencer in the front drawing-room, keep up a

whispering accompaniment to the conversation of the elder lady--if

conversation it could be call, when Mrs. Hazleby have it all to

herself, while give Lady Merton and Mrs. Woodbourne an account of the

discomfort she have experience in country quarter in Ireland.



Sir Edward and Mr. Woodbourne be engage in look over the account

of the church in the study, and Fido be try to settle his dispute

with Meg Merrilies, who, with arch back, tail erect, and eye like

flame green glass, wage a continual war with he over her basket in

the hall.



Anne be very glad to hear her cousin's footstep in the hall as she

return.  come straight to the drawing-room, Elizabeth exclaim,

'Mamma, do you tell Mrs. Clarke that she might have a frock for Susan?'



'yes, my dear,' say Mrs. Woodbourne; 'she ask I yesterday when you

be not near, and I tell she you would give her one.  I think the

child look very ragged.'



'I suppose she must have it,' say Elizabeth, look much vex; 'I

tell she she should not, a month ago, unless she send the child to

school regularly, and they have scarcely be there five day in the

last fortnight.'



'I wish I have know it, my dear,' say Mrs. Woodbourne; 'you know I be

always very sorry to interfere with any of your plan.'



'o Mamma, there be no great harm do,' say Elizabeth.  she then go

to fetch the frock, and give it to the woman with a more gentle and

sensible rebuke than could have be expect from the vehemence of her

manner towards Mrs. Woodbourne a minute before.  when this be do,

and she have take off her bonnet, she come to beckon Anne up-stair.



'so you have finish your labour,' say Anne, take up her work,

while Elizabeth sit down to rule a copy-book for Winifred.



'yes,' say Elizabeth, '"we be free to sport and play;" I have read to

the old woman, and cram the child, and give old Mrs. Clayton a

catalogue raisonnee of all the company and all their dress, and a

bill of fare of our luncheon and dinner, and where everything come

from.'



'and yet you profess to hold gossip in abomination,' say Anne.



'oh! but this be old gossip, regular legitimate amusement for the poor

old lady,' say Elizabeth.  'she really be a lady, but very badly off,

and most of the Abbeychurch gentility be too fine to visit she, so

that a little quiet chat with she be by no mean of the common-place

kind.  besides, she know and love we all like her own child.  it

be one of the first pleasure I can remember, to gather rose for she,

and carry they to she from her own old garden here.'



'well, in consideration of all that you say,' say Anne, 'I suppose I

must forgive she for keep you away all this afternoon.'



'and what do you do all that time?' say Elizabeth.  'have you read

Hereward, and do not you delight in he?'



'yes,' say Anne, 'and I want to know whether he be not the father of

Cedric of Rotherwood.'



'he must have be his grandfather,' say Elizabeth; 'Cedric live a

hundred year after.'



'but Cedric remember Torquilstone before the Normans come,' say Anne.



'no, no, he could not, though he have be tell what it have be before

Front-de-Boeuf alter it,' say Elizabeth.



'and old Ulrica be there when Front-de-Boeuf's father take it,' say

Anne.



'I cannot tell how long a hag may live,' say Elizabeth, 'but she could

not have be less than a hundred and thirty year old in the time of

Richard Coeur-de-Lion.'



'Coeur-de-Lion come to the throne in 1189,' say Anne.  'no, I suppose

Torquil Wolfganger could not have be dispossess immediately after

the Conquest.  but then you know Ulrica call Cedric the son of the

great Hereward.'



'her wit be a little out of order,' say Elizabeth; 'either she

mean his grandson, or Sir Walter Scott make as great an anachronism as

when he make that same Ulrica compare Rebecca's skin to paper.  if she

have say parchment, it would not have be such a compliment.'



'how much interest Ivanhoe make we take in the Saxons and Normans!'

say Anne.



'and what nonsense it be to say that work of fiction give a distaste

for history,' say Elizabeth.



'you be an instance to the contrary,' say Anne; 'no one love story

so well, and no one love history well.'



'I believe such story as Ivanhoe be what teach I to like

history,' say Elizabeth.



'in order to find out the anachronism in they?' say Anne; 'I think it

be very ungrateful of you.'



'no indeed,' say Elizabeth; 'why, they use to be the only history I

know, and almost the only geography.  do not you remember Aunt Anne's

laugh at I for argue that Bohemia be on the Baltic, because

Perdita be leave on its coast?  and now, I believe that Coeur de Lion

feast with Robin Hood and his merry man, although history tell I

that he dislike and despise the English, and the only sentence of

their language history record of his uttering be, "he speak like a

fool Briton."  I believe that Queen Margaret of Anjou haunt the

scene of grandeur that once be her, and that she live to see the

fall of Charles of Burgundy, and die when her last hope fail she,

though I know that it be not so.'



'then I do not quite see how such story have teach you to like

history,' say Anne.



'they teach we to realize and understand the people whom we find in

history,' say Elizabeth.



'oh yes,' say Anne; 'who would care for Louis the eleventh if it be

not for Quentin Durward? and Shakespeare make we feel as if we have

be at the battle of Shrewsbury.'



'yes,' say Elizabeth; 'and they have do even more for history. they

have teach we to imagine other hero whom they have not mention.

cannot you see the Black Prince, his slight graceful figure, his fair

delicate face full of gentleness and kindness--fierce warrior as he

be--his black steel helmet, and tippet of chain-mail, his clustering

white plume, his surcoat with England's leopard and France's lily?

cannot you make a story of his long constant attachment to his

beautiful cousin, the Fair Maid of Kent?  cannot you imagine his

courteous conference with Bertrand du Guesclin, the brave ugly

Breton?--Edward lie almost helpless on his couch, break down with

suffering and disappointment, and the noble affectionate Captal de

Buch, who die of grief for he, think whether he will ever be able

to wear his black armour again, and carry terror and dismay to the

stout heart of France.'



'give Froissart some of the credit of your picture,' say Anne.



'Froissart be in some place like Sir Walter himself,' say Elizabeth;

'but now I will tell you of a person who live in no day of romance,

and have not have the advantage of a poetical historian to light he up

in our imagination.  I mean the great Prince of Conde. now, though he

be very unlike Shakespeare's Coriolanus, yet there be resemblance

enough between they to make the comparison very amusing. there be much

of Coriolanus' indomitable pride and horror of mob popularity when he

offend Beaufort and his kingdom in the halle, when, though as 'Louis

de Bourbon' he refuse to do anything to shake the power of the throne,

he would not submit to be patronize by the mean fawn Mazarin.  not

that the hard-hearted Conde would have listen to his wife and mother,

even if he have love they as Coriolanus do, or that his arrogance do

not degenerate into wonderful meanness at last, such as Coriolanus

would have scorn; but the parallel be very amusing, and give I a

great interest in Conde.  and do you ever observe what a great

likeness there be in the character of the two apostate, Julian and

Frederick the Great?'



'then you like history for the sake of compare the character

mention in it?' say Anne.



'I think so,' say Elizabeth; 'and that be the reason I hate

abridgement, the mere bare bone of history.  I cannot bear dry fact,

such as that Charles the Fifth beat Francis the First, at Pavia, in a

war for the duchy of Milan, and nothing more tell about they.  I be

always ready to say, as the Grand Seignior do about some such great

battle among the Christians, that I do not care whether the dog bite

the hog, or the hog bite the dog.'



'what a kind interest in your fellow-creature you display!' say Anne.

'I think one reason why I like history be because I be search out

all the character who come up to my notion of perfect chivalry, or

rather of christian perfection.  I be make a book of true knight.  I

copy their portrait when I can find they, and write the name of those

whose likeness I cannot get.  I paint their armorial bearing over

they when I can find out what they be, and I have a great red cross in

the first page.'



'and I will tell you of something else to put at the beginning,' say

Elizabeth, 'a branch of laurel entwine with the beautiful white

bind-weed.  one of our laurel be cover with wreath of it last

year, and I think it be a beautiful emblem of a pure-hearted hero.

the glare sun, which wither the fair white spotless flower, be like

worldly prosperity spoil the pure simple mind; and you know how

often it be despise and tear away from the laurel to which it be so

bright an ornament.'



'yes,' say Anne, 'it cling more safely and fearlessly round the

simple and most despise of plant.  and would you call the little

pink bindweed childish innocence?'



'no, I do not think I should,' say Elizabeth, 'it be not sufficiently

stainless.  but then innocence, from not see or know what be

wrong, be not like the guilelessness which can use the world as not

abuse it.'



'yet Adam and Eve fall when they gain the knowledge of good and

evil,' say Anne.



'yes, because they gain their knowledge by do evil,' say
