Elizabeth, 'but you must allow that what be try and not find wanting

be superior to what have fail only because it have have no trial.  St.

John's Day be place near Christmas than that of the Holy Innocents.'



'and St. John know what evil be,' say Anne; 'yes you be right there.'



'you speak as if you still have some fault to find with I, Anne,' say

Elizabeth.



'no, indeed I have not,' say Anne, 'I quite agree with you; it be

only your speaking of knowledge of evil we a kind of advantage, that

startle I.'



'because you think knowledge and discernment my idol,' say Elizabeth;

'but we have wander far away from my white convolvulus, and I have

not do with it yet.  when autumn come, and the leave turn bright

yellow, it be a golden crown.'



'but there your comparison end,' say Anne; 'the laurel ought to

vanish away, and leave the golden wreath behind.'



'no,' say Elizabeth; 'call the golden wreath the crown of glory on the

brow of the old saint-like hero, and remember that when he die, the

immortality the world prize be that of the coarse evergreen laurel,

and no one dream of his white wreath.'



'I wish you would make a poem of your comparison, for the beginning of

my book of chivalry,' say Anne.



'it will not do,' say Elizabeth, 'I be no poet; besides, if I wish

to try, just consider what a name the flower have--con-vol-vu-lus, a

prosaic, dragging, botanical term, a mile long.  then bindweed only

remind I of smother and fetter raspberry bush, and a great hoe.

Lily, as the country people call it, be not distinguish enough,

besides that no one ever hear of a climbing lily.  but, Anne, do tell

I whom you have in your book of knight.  I know of a good many in the

real heroic age, but tell I some of the later one.'



'Lord Exmouth,' say Anne; 'I be sure he be a true knight.'



'and the Vendeen leader, I suppose,' say Elizabeth.



'yes, I have write the name of M. de Lescure and of Henri de la

Rochejaquelein; I wish I know where to find their picture, and I want

a prussian patriot.  I think the Baron de la Motte Fouque, who be a

Knight of St. John, and who think so much of true chivalry, would

come in very well.'



'I do not know anything about himself,' say Elizabeth, 'though,

certainly, no one but a true knight could have write Sintram.  I be

afraid there be no leader good enough for you among the spanish

patriot in the Peninsular war.'



'I do not know,' say Anne; 'I admire Don Jose Palafox for his defence

of Zaragoza, but I know nothing more of he, and there be no chance of

my get his portrait.  I be in great want of Cameron of Lochiel, or

Lord Nithsdale, or derwentwater; for Claverhouse be the only Jacobite

leader I can find a portrait of, and I be afraid the blood of the

Covenanters be a blot on his escutcheon, a stain on his white wreath.'



'I be sorry you have nothing to say to bonnie Dundee,' say Elizabeth,

'for really, between the Whiggery and stupidity of England, and the

wickedness of France, good people be scarce from Charles the Martyr to

George the Third.  how I hate that part of history!  oh! but there be

Prince Eugene and the Vicomte de Turenne.'



'Prince Eugene behave very well to Marlborough in his adversity,' say

Anne: 'but I do not like people to take affront and abandon their

native country.'



'oh! but Savoy be more his country than France,' say Elizabeth,

'however, I do not know enough about he to make it worth while to

fight for he.'



'and as to Turenne,' say Anne, 'I do not like the little I know of

he; he be horribly cruel, be he not?'



'oh! every soldier be cruel in those day,' say Elizabeth; 'it be

the custom of their time, and they could not help it.'



Anne shake her head.



'then you will be force to give up my beloved Black Prince,' continue

Elizabeth piteously; 'you know he massacre the people at Limoges.'



'I cannot do without he,' say Anne; 'he be ill and very much

exasperate at the time, and I choose to believe that the massacre be

command by John of Gaunt.'



'and I choose to believe that all the cruelty of the French be by

the express order of Louis Quatorze,' say Elizabeth; 'you cannot be

hard on a man who give all his money and offer to pawn his plate to

bring Charles the Second back to England.'



'I must search and consider,' say Anne; 'I will hunt he out when I go

home, and if we have a print of he, and if he be tolerably

good-look, I will see what I can do with he.'



'you have Lodge's portrait,' say Elizabeth, 'so you be well off for

Cavaliers; do you mean to take Prince Rupert in compliment to your

brother?'



'no, he be not good enough, I be afraid,' say Anne, 'though besides

our own Vandyke there be a most tempting print of he, in Lodge, with a

buff coat and work ruffle; but though I use to think he the

great of hero, I have give he up, and mean to content myself

with Charles himself, the two Lindsays, Ormond and Strafford, Derby and

Capel, and Sir Ralph Hopton.'



'and Montrose, and the Marquis of Winchester,' say Elizabeth; 'you

must not forget the noblest of all.'



'I only forget to mention they,' say Anne, 'I could not leave they

out.  the only difficulty be whom to choose among the Cavaliers.'



'and who come next?' say Elizabeth.



'Gustavus Adolphus and Sir Philip Sydney.'



'do not mention they together, they be no pair,' say Elizabeth. 'what

a pity it be that Sir Philip be a euphuist.'



'forgive he for that failing, in consideration of his speech at

Zutphen,' say Anne.



'only that speech be so hackneyed and commonplace,' say Elizabeth, 'I

be tired of it.'



'the deed be not common-place,' say Anne.



'no, and dandyism be as entirely the fault of his time as cruelty be

of Turenne's,' say Elizabeth; 'Sir Walter Raleigh be bad than

Sydney, and Surrey quite as bad, to judge by his picture.'



'it be not quite as bad a fault as cruelty,' say Anne, 'little as you

seem to think of the last.'



'now come the chivalric age,' say Elizabeth; 'never mind tell I

all the name, only say who be the first of your hero--neither

Orlando nor Sir Galahad, I suppose.'



'no, nor Huon de Bordeaux,' say Anne.



'the Cid, then, I suppose,' say Elizabeth, 'unless he be too fierce

for your tender heart.'



'Ruy, mi Cid Campeador?' say Anne, 'I must have he in consideration

of his noble conduct to the King who banish he, and the speech the

ballad give he:



          "for vassal' vengeance on their lord,

             though just, be treason still;

           the noble blood be his, who well

             bear undeserve ill."





and the loyalty he shew in make the King clear himself of have

any share in his brother's death, even though Alphonso be silly enough

to be affront.'



'like Montrose's feeling towards his lady-love,' say Elizabeth; 'not

bear the least stain on what he love or honour.'



'but he be not our early knight,' say Anne; 'I begin with our own

Alfred, with his blue shield and golden cross.'



'King Alfred!' exclaim Elizabeth, 'do you consider he a knight?'



'certainly,' say Anne; 'besides that I care more for the spirit of

chivalry than for the etiquette of the accolade and golden spur; we

know that Alfred knight his grandson Athelstane, so that he must have

be a knight himself.'



'by-the-bye,' say Elizabeth, 'I think I have find out the origin of

the golden spur be part of a knight's equipment.  do you remember

when the Cid's beloved king Don Sancho be kill, that Rodrigo could

not overtake the traitor Bellido Dolfos, because he have no spur on,

whereupon he curse every knight who should for the future ride without

they.  now that be at the time when the law of chivalry be

attain their perfection, but--'



'not so fast,' say Anne; 'I have a much early pair of golden spur

for you.  do not you remember Edmund, the last king of East Anglia,

be betray to the Danish wedding-party at Hoxne, by the glitter of

his golden spur, and curse every new married pair who should ever

pass over the bridge where he be find.  I think that make for my

side of the question.  here be edmund, a knight in golden spur when

Alfred be a child.  ah ha, Miss Lizzie!'



before Elizabeth could answer, Winifred come to tell she that her mamma

want she, and she be force to leave the question of King Alfred's

and King Edmund's chivalry undecided; for, to her praise be it speak,

she be much too useful a person ever to be able to pursue her own

peculiar diversion for many minute together.  she have to listen to

some direction, and undertake some message, so that she could not

return to her own room till after Anne have go down-stair.  she

herself be not ready till just as the elder be set off to the

dinner-party at Marlowe Court, and rejoice in the cessation of the

rain and the fineness of the evening.



about half an hour afterwards, the young lady assemble in the inner

drawing-room to drink tea.  Helen, however, remain in the outer

drawing-room, practise her music, regardless of the sound of mirth

that proceed from the other room, until Elizabeth open the door,

call out,



     '"Sweet bird, that shunnest the noise of folly,

       most musical, most melancholy,"



come in to tea, so please your highness.'



'what can you mean?' say Helen; 'I be sure I be not melancholy.'



'I be sure you shun the noise of folly,' say Elizabeth.



'I be sorry you consider all our merriment as folly,' say Anne, hope

to save Helen.



'indeed I do not,' say Elizabeth; 'it be no more folly than a

kitten's play, and quite as much in the natural course of thing.'



'Helen's occupation be out of the natural course of thing,' say

Anne, 'I should think she be well employ than we be.'



'in make a noise,' say Elizabeth; 'so be we, I do not see much

difference.'



'O Lizzie, it be not the same thing!' say Helen, exceedingly

mortified at be laugh at for what she consider as a heroic piece

of self-denial, and so it be, though perhaps not so great in she as it

would have be in one who be less musical, and more addicted to the

noise of folly.



'how touchy Helen be this evening!' think Elizabeth; 'I have well

let she alone, both for her sake and my own.'



'how foolish I be to interfere!' think Anne; 'it be the most

awkward thing I ever do; I only rouse the spirit of contradiction,

and do Helen more harm than good; I never will meddle between sister

again.'



presently after, Elizabeth ask Harriet Hazleby whether she have ever

be at Winchester.



'yes,' be the answer, 'and a dull place I would not wish to see.'



'it be a handsome old town, be it not?' inquire Anne, turn to Lucy;

but Harriet catch up the word, and exclaim, 'handsome, indeed!  I do

not think there be one tolerable new look street in the whole place,

except one or two house just up by the railroad station.'



Anne still look towards Lucy, as if await her answer; Lucy

reply, 'the Cathedral and College and the old gateway be very

beautiful, but there be not so many old look house as you would

expect.'



'it must be badly off indeed,' say Elizabeth, 'if it have neither old

house nor new; but I want to know whether William Rufus' monument be

in a tolerable state of preservation.'



'oh! the monument be very grand indeed,' say Harriet; 'everyone

admire they.  there be the head of some of the old king most

beautifully paint, put away in a dark corner.  they be very curious

thing indeed; I wonder they do not bring they out.'



'those be the head of the Stuart king,' whisper Lucy.



'why, Harriet,' exclaim Dora, 'William Rufus be not a Stuart, he be

the second of the Normans.'



'very likely, very likely, Dora, my dear,' answer Harriet; 'I have

do with all those thing now, thank goodness; I only know that see

the Cathedral be good fun; I do not like go into the crypt, I

say I would not go, when I see how dark it be; and Frank Hollis say

I should, and it be such fun!'



Dora open her eye very wide, and Elizabeth say, 'there could

certainly never be a well time or place.'



look up, she see poor Lucy's burn cheek, and be sorry she have

not be silent.  no one speak for a few moment, but presently Anne

say, 'Alfred the Great be not bury in the Cathedral, be he?'



no one could tell; at last Helen say, 'I remember read that he be

bury in Hyde Abbey, which be now pull down.'



'there be a street at Winchester, call Hyde Street,' say Lucy.



'yes, I know,' say Harriet, 'where the Bridewell be, I remember--'



'by-the-bye, Anne,' say Elizabeth, anxious to cut short Harriet's

reminiscence, 'I never answer what you say about Alfred and

Athelstane.  I do not think that Alfred do more than present he with

his sword, which be always solemnly do, even to squire, before they

be allow to fight, and might be do by a priest.'



'but when Athelstane be call a knight, and the ceremony of present

he with his weapon be mention,' say Anne, 'I cannot see why we

should not consider he to have be really knight.'



'because,' say Elizabeth, 'I do not think that the old Saxon word,

knight, mean the sworn champion, the devoted warrior of noble birth,

which it now express.  you know Canute's old rhyme say, "row  to the

shore, knight," as if they be boatman, and not gentleman.'



'I do not think it could have be beneath the dignity of a knight to

row Canute,' say Anne, 'consider that eight king row Edgar the

Peaceable.'



'other thing prove that Knight mean a servant, in Saxon,' say

Elizabeth.



'I know it do sometimes, as in German now,' say Anne; 'but the

question be, when it acquire a meaning equivalent in dignity to the

French Chevalier.'



'though it properly mean anything but a horseman,' say Elizabeth; 'we

ought to have a word answer to the German Ritter.'



'yes, our language be spoil by be mix with French before it have

come to its perfection,' say Anne; 'but still you have not prove that

King Alfred be not a knight in the high sense of the word, a preux

chevalier.'



'I never hear of Alfred on horseback, nor do I ever know he call

Sir Alfred of Wessex.'



'Sir be french, and short for seigneur or senior,' say Anne; 'besides,

I suppose, you never hear Coeur-de-Lion call Sir Richard

Plantagenet.'



'I will tell you how you may find out all about it,' interrupted

Katherine; 'Mrs. Turner's nephew, Mr. Augustus Mills, be go to give

a lecture this evening, at seven o'clock, upon chivalry, and all that.

Mrs. Turner have be tell we all day how much she wish we to go.'



'Mr. Augustus Mills!' say Elizabeth; 'be he the little red-haired

wretch who use to pester I about dance all last year?'



'no, no,' say Katherine, 'that be Mr. Adolphus Mills, his brother,

who be go to be clerk to an attorney somewhere.  this be Mr.

Augustus, a very fine young man, and so clever, Willie say, and he have

most beautiful curl black hair.'



'it want a quarter to seven now,' say Elizabeth, 'and the sky be most

beautifully clear, at last.  do you like the thought of this lecture,

Anne?'



'I should like to go very much indeed,' say Anne; 'but first I must go

and seal and send some letter for Mamma, so I must depart while you

finish your tea.'  so say, she leave the room.



'pray, Kate,' say Helen, as Anne close the door, 'where be this

lecture to be give?'



'at the Mechanics' Institute, of course,' say Katherine.



'so we cannot go,' say Helen.



'and pray why not, my sapient sister?' say Elizabeth; 'what objection

have your high mightiness?'



'my dear Lizzie,' say Helen, 'I wish you have hear all that I have

hear, at dykeland, about Mechanics' Institutes.'



'my dear Helen,' say Elizabeth, 'I wish you would learn that dykeland

be no Delphos to I.'



'Nay, but my dear sister,' exclaim Helen, clasp her hand, 'do

but listen to I; I be sure that harm will come of your go.'



'well, ope your lip, Sir Oracle,' say Elizabeth impatiently, 'no dog

shall bark, only make haste about it, or we shall be too late.'



'do you not know, Lizzie,' say Helen, 'that Socialists often hold

forth in Mechanics' Institutes?'



'the abuse of a thing do not cancel its use,' say Elizabeth, 'and I

do not suppose that Mr. Mills preach Socialism.'



'Captain Atherley say,' persist Helen, 'that all sort of people

ought not to mix themselves up together on equal term.'



'oh! then he never go to church,' retort Elizabeth.



'no, no, that be only my foolish way of express myself,' say

Helen; 'I mean that he say that it be wrong for Church people to put

themselves on a level with dissenter, or infidel, or Socialists, for

aught they know to the contrary.'



'since you have be in the north, Helen,' say Elizabeth, 'you have

think every third man you meet a Chartist or a Socialist; but as I do

not believe there be specimen of either kind in Abbeychurch, I see no

harm in take our chance of the very few dissenter there be here,

and sit to hear a lecture in company with our own townspeople.'



'really, I think we have well not go without ask leave first,' say

Katherine.



'in the first place,' say Elizabeth, 'there be no one to ask; and

next, I know that Mrs. Turner have offer hundred of time to take we

there, and I suppose Papa would have refuse once for all, if he have

be so very much afraid of our turn Chartists as Helen seem to be.

I can see no reason why we should not go.'



'then you consider my opinion as utterly worthless,' cry Helen,

lose all command of temper, which indeed she have preserve long

than could have be expect.  'I might have know it; you never care

for one word I say.  you will repent it at last, I know you will.'



'it be not that I never care for what you say, Helen,' say Elizabeth,

'it be only when you give I dykeland opinion instead of your own,

and talk of what you do not understand.  I suppose no one have any

objection to a walk, at least.  shall we get ready?'



everyone consent, and they go to prepare.  it should be say, in

excuse for Elizabeth, that both she and Helen have be absent from home

at the time of the establishment of the Mechanics' Institute at

Abbeychurch, so that they have not know of their father's opposition to

it.  Helen, who, when at dykeland, have be near the manufacturing

district, have hear more of the folly and mischief commit by

some of the favourer of these institution. unfortunately, however,

her temper have prevent she from reason calmly, and Elizabeth have

wilfully blind herself, and shut her ear to conviction, be

determined to follow her own course.  Anne, who have always live at

Merton Hall, except two month of each year, which she spend in

London, know nothing of country town cabal, and think the lecture

be of the same nature as those she have hear in London, ask no

question, as she have not hear the debate between Elizabeth and Helen.

Katherine, however, hesitate to go without the permission of her

father and mother; or, in other word, she be afraid they would

reprove she, and she be not unwilling to listen to Helen's

representation on the subject, while they be put on their

bonnet.



'it be not only,' say Helen, 'that we be sure that it be not right to

go anywhere without leave from Papa or Mamma, but that I know that

these Mechanics' institute be part of a system of--'



'oh yes, I know,' say Katherine, 'of Chartism, and Socialism, and all

that be horrible.  I cannot imagine how Lizzie can think of go.'



'then you will not go,' say Helen.



'oh, I do not know,' say Katherine; 'it will seem so odd and so

particular if Anne and Lizzie and the Hazlebys go, and we do not. it

would be like set ourselves up against our elder.'



'you do not always think much of that, Kate,' say Helen; 'besides, if

our eld sister think proper to do wrong, I do not see that we be

force to do so too.'



'well, but Lizzie say it be not wrong, and she be the eld,' argue

Katherine.



'Lizzie say it be not wrong, that she might have her own way, and

contradict I,' say Helen.



'we shall see what Anne say,' say Katherine; 'but if they go, I must,

you know.  it be to I that Mrs. Turner give the invitation, and she

and Willie would think it so odd to see the other without I; and Mr.

Mills too, he say so very politely that he hope that he should be

honour with my presence and Harriet's, it would be an additional

stimulus to his exertion, he say.'



'my dear kate,' exclaim Helen, 'how could you listen to such affected

nonsense?'



'why, Lizzie say everybody talk nonsense,' say Katherine, 'but we

must listen and be civil, you know; I be sure I wish people would not

be so silly, it be very disagreeable to hear it; but I cannot help it,

and after this I really think I ought to go, it would be very odd if I

do not.'



'well do what be odd than what be wrong,' say Helen.



in her secret soul, Katherine have be of the same opinion the whole

time, and now that she think she have make a sufficient merit of

give up the expedition, she be about to promise to follow Helen's

advice, when she be interrupt by the entrance of Harriet, with she

shawl and bonnet in her hand, come to gossip with Katherine, and thus

escape from Lucy, who have be quietly suggest that in a doubtful

case, such as the present seem to be, it be always good to keep to

the safe side.  Harriet have laugh at Lucy for not be able to give

any reason, tell she that it be plain that Helen know nothing about

the matter, and declare that she thank goodness that if Mr.

Woodbourne be ever so angry, he be not her master, and her own mamma

never minded what she do.  Lucy could make no answer in word, but her

silent protest against her sister's conduct make Harriet so uneasy that

she quit she as soon as she could.



Helen still hope that Anne would see the folly of the scheme, and

persuade Elizabeth to give it up, and content herself with take a

walk, or that her sister's well sense would prevail; but she be

disappointed, when, as they leave the house, Anne ask where the

lecture be to be give, Elizabeth reply, 'at the Mechanics'

Institute;' and no further observation be make, Anne's silence

confirm Elizabeth in her idea that Helen have be talk nonsense.

still, as St. Martin's Street, where Mr. Turner live, be their way

out of the town, Helen remain in doubt respect her sister's

intention until they reach Mr. Turner's house, and Elizabeth walk

up the step, and knock at the door.



Helen immediately wheel round, and walk indignantly homeward, too

full of her own feeling to make any attempt to persuade Katherine to

follow her example, and every step shew how grieve and affront

she be.



Lucy lay her hand on her sister's arm, and look up imploringly in

her face.



'Pooh!' say Harriet pettishly, jerk the ribbon by which she be

lead Fido: 'give I one reason, Lucy, and I will come.'



'what Helen say,' answer Lucy.



'stuff and nonsense!' say Harriet; 'that be no reason at all.'



'what do Helen say?' ask Anne, who have be rather startle by her

departure.



'only some Dykelands fancy about Socialists,' say Elizabeth; 'that

be the reason she have go off like a tragedy queen.  I do not think

all Abbeychurch be ready for the French Revolution--that be all.'



'there, Lucy, you see,' say Harriet; 'come along, there's a good girl.'



here Mrs. Turner's page open the door, and answer that his mistress

be at home.



'Dora, my dear,' say Elizabeth, 'this be too late an affair for you;

we shall not be at home till after you be go to bed.

good-night--run after Helen.'



Dora obey, and Lucy also turn away; Katherine linger.  'come,

Kate,' say Harriet, mount the step. --'Lucy, you nonsensical girl,

come back; everyone can see you out of the window; it be very rude,

now; if Mrs. Turner see you, what will she think?  Mamma would be very

angry to see you so silly.  come back, I tell you!'



Lucy only look back, and shake her head, and then hasten away; but

Katherine, fear that her friend would be irrecoverably offend if

she turn away from their house, think that she have go too far to

recede, and trust to Elizabeth to shield she from blame, follow

the other up-stair.



Helen turn back, much surprised, as Lucy and Dora overtake she; and

they hasten to give explanation.



'Lizzie say I have well come home,' say Dora.



'and I think it would be the safe thing to do,' say Lucy.



'I be very glad of it,' say Helen; 'I be sure it be not right to go,

but when Lizzie have once set her mind on anything, she will listen to

no one.'



'then do you think Papa and Mamma will be displease?' say Dora; 'I do

not think Lizzie think so.'



'I cannot be quite sure,' say Helen; 'but I do not think Lizzie

choose to believe that they will.'



'but let I understand you, Helen,' say Lucy; 'I only know that you

think that Uncle Woodbourne would not approve of your going.  what be

your reason for thinking so?  I do not clearly understand you.

church-people and dissenter put themselves on a level in almost every

public place.'



'they do not meet in every public place on what they agree to call

neutral ground,' say Helen, 'or profess to lay aside all such

distinction, and to banish religion in order to avoid raise

dispute.  you know that no subject can be safely treat of, except

with reference to the christian religion.'



'how do you mean?' say Lucy.



'why,' say Helen, hesitate a little, 'how many people run wild, and

adopt foolish and wicked view of politic, for want of read history

religiously!  and the astronomer and geologist, without faith,

question the possibility of the first chapter of Genesis; and some

people fancy that the world be people with a great tribe of wild

savage, instead of believe all about Adam and Eve and the

Patriarchs.  now if you turn religion out, you see, you be sure to

fall into false notion; and that be what these Mechanics' Institute

people do.'



'yes,' say Lucy, 'I have hear what you say about those thing before,

but I never see they in connection with each other.'



'nor should I have see they in this light, if it have not be for a

conversation between Captain Atherly and another gentleman, one day at

dykeland,' say Helen.  'but, Lucy, do you leave this party, then,

only because I say it be wrong, or because you think so yourself?'



'indeed, I can hardly tell,' answer Lucy; 'I scarcely know what to

think right and what wrong, but I think I might be certain that it

be safe to go home.'



'I do not see,' say Helen, draw herself up, and feeling as if she

have do a very wise thing, and know her reason for do it, too, 'I

do not see that it be so very hard to know what be right from what be

wrong.  it be the easy way to think what Papa and Mamma would

approve, and then try to recollect what reason they would give.'



'but then you be not always sure of what they would say,' reply

Lucy; 'at least I be not, and it be not always possible to ask they.

what do you do all the time you be at dykeland?'



'oh! dear Mrs. Staunton be quite a mother to I,' say Helen; 'and

besides, it be as easy to think what would please papa there as it be

here.  you be from home for some time last year, be you not, Lucy?'



'yes,' reply Lucy, 'I spend several month at Hastings, with

Grandmamma; and I be almost ashamed to say that I feel more comfortable

there than anywhere else.  I like be by the sea, and have a

garden, and be out of the way of the officer.  Papa and Grandmamma

talk of my always live there, and I hope I should; but then I

should not have like to leave Papa and the rest, and not to be at home

in my brother' holiday, so I believe thing be good as they be.'



'how you must wish to have a home!' say Helen.



'do not you think that home be wherever your father and mother and

brother and sister be, Helen?' say Lucy.



'oh yes, certainly,' say Helen, quickly; 'but I mean a settle home.'



'I do sometimes wish we be settle,' say Lucy; 'but I have be use

to wander all my life, and do not mind it as much as you would,

perhaps.  we scarcely stay long enough in one place to get attach to

it; and some place be so disagreeable, that it be a pleasure to leave

they.'



'such as those in Ireland, that Mrs. Hazleby be talk of yesterday?'

say Helen.



'I do not mind those half so much as I do some other,' say Lucy; 'we

could easily get into the country, and I use to walk with Papa every

day, or ride when Harriet do not want the horse.  it be rather

uncomfortable, for we be very much crowded when George and Allan be

at home; but then they have leave to shoot and fish, and enjoy

themselves very much.'



'really, Lucy,' say Helen, 'I cannot think how you can be so very

content.'



'I do not know there be anything to be discontent with,' say Lucy,

smile; 'I be sure I be very happy.'



'but what do you say just now you dislike?' say Helen.



'do I say I dislike anything?' say Lucy.  'oh! I know what it be. I

do not like go to a large town, where we can only walk in the

street, and go out shopping every day, and the boy have nothing to

amuse they.  and it be bad of all to go to a place where Papa and

Mamma have be before, and know all the people; we go out to tea half

the day we be there, or to dinner, or have company at home, and I

never get a quiet evening's reading with Papa, and Allan have a very

great dislike to company.'



as Lucy finish her speech they come to the vicarage; and as they

open the door, Meg Merrilies came purr out to meet Dora.  they

look round for Fido, in order to keep the peace between the two

enemy, but he be nowhere to be see, and Dora remember to have

see he with Harriet, just as they leave the rest of the party at Mr.

Turner's door; so dismiss he from their mind, they go to finish

their walk in the garden, where Helen give Lucy a full description of

all the beauty of dykeland, and the perfection of its inhabitant;

and find she an attentive and obliging listener, talk herself into

a state of most uncommon good humour and amiability for the rest of the

evening.  on her side, Lucy, though she have no particular interest in

the staunton, and indeed have never hear their name before Helen's

visit to they, be really pleased and amuse, for she have learn to

seek her pleasure in the happiness of other people.









CHAPTER VIII.





if Helen have not be too much offend by Elizabeth's disregard of her

counsel to think of anything but her own dignity, and have wait to

remind Katherine of her argument with she, the latter might perhaps

have take the safe course, for it be not without many qualm of

conscience that she ascend the stair to Mrs. Turner's drawing-room.



there be no one in the room; and as soon as the page have close the

door, Elizabeth exclaim, 'I declare, Anne, there be the bone of

be Elizabeth lower in her sister's eye by humble herself?

while Katherine and Helen be undressing, the former begin:

CHAPTER IX.





although she have sit up so much later than usual the night before, Anne

'I tell Mamma this morning,' say Anne.



'tell she!' exclaim Harriet; 'and what do she say--?'



'she say she wonder that my cousin be allow to go to such a

place,' say Anne; 'and she seem very sorry we have go.'



'but be she angry with you?' persist Harriet.



Anne hesitate; and Elizabeth reply, 'no, of course she could not be

angry with Anne, when it be all my doing.  she must be displease

enough with I, though.'



'but will she tell Mamma and Aunt Mildred?' say Harriet.



'I do not think she will,' answer Anne.



'no, because she trust to I to tell,' say Elizabeth; 'so that you

see I must, Harriet.'



'must you?' say Harriet; 'I cannot see why; it will only get we all a

scolding.'



'which we richly deserve,' say Elizabeth.



'I be sure, if you like to be scold,' say Harriet, 'you be very

welcome; only do not make Mamma scold I too.'



'I be sure, if you like to be insincere and cowardly,' say Elizabeth,

'you shall not make I so too.'



'I do not want you to tell a fib,' say Harriet; 'I only want you to

say nothing.'



'L'un vaut bien l'autre,' say Elizabeth.



'what?' say Harriet; 'do only wait till we be go, if you be

determined to tell--there's a dear girl.'



'Deceive Papa and Mamma for three whole day!' cry Elizabeth; 'I

wonder you be not ashamed of yourself.  besides, Harriet, I do not see

what you have to fear.  it be Kate and I who do wrong; we know

well, and cast away Helen's good advice; we shut our eye and go

headlong into mischief, but you have no reason to suppose that you might

not do as we do.'



'no,' say Harriet, 'I should not care if it be not for Fido.'



'but will my silence find Fido?' say Elizabeth.



'no,' say Harriet; 'but if Mamma know we go there she will scold we

for go, because she will be angry about Fido; and if she once think

that it be I who lose he--oh, Lizzie, you do not know how angry she

will be!'



'but, Harriet,' say Katherine, 'I think you use to say that you

could do anything with your Mamma, and that she never mind where you

go.'



'oh! that be when she be in good humour,' say Harriet; 'she be not

often cross with I, but when she be, you may hear she from one end of

the house to the other.  cannot you, Lucy?  and now she will be

dreadfully cross about Fido, and the other thing come upon it, I do

not know what she may say.  o Lizzie, you will save I!'



'I will only tell of Kate and myself,' say Elizabeth; 'or I will ask

Papa not to mention it to Mrs. Hazleby; though, Harriet, there be some

people who prefer any suffering, just or unjust, to deceit.'



'then you mean to tell directly,' say Katherine, in a piteous tone.



'of course I do,' say Elizabeth; 'there be the dining-room door shut.

come with I, Kate.'



Katherine rather unwillingly follow her sister into the passage; but

when there, fear make her ingenious, a sudden thought strike she.

'Lizzie,' whisper she, 'if you tell Papa that you and I go, Mrs.

Hazleby will be sure to hear, and if she ask Harriet about it, perhaps

she--you know--may tell a story about it.'



'fine confidence you shew in your choose friend!' say Elizabeth.



'why, one must be civil; and Harriet be a sort of cousin,' say

Katherine; 'but I be sure she be not half so much my friend as Willie.'



'well, never mind defend your taste in friend,' say Elizabeth;

'for as I do think your scruple worth answer, I will tell you that I

have think of the same thing; but I do not choose to do evil that good

may come, or that evil may not come.  I shall tell Papa what an

excellent opinion you have of Harriet, and leave he to do as he

please.'



Elizabeth's hand be on the lock of the door of her father's study,

when Katherine exclaim, 'there be someone there--I hear voice!'



'Uncle Edward,' say Elizabeth.  'I do not mind his be there; we

ought to beg his pardon for lead Anne astray.'



'oh! but do not you see,' say Katherine, 'here be a hat and a roll of

paper on the table!  Mr. Roberts must be come.'



'tiresome man!' cry Elizabeth; 'he will be there all day, and I shall

not see Papa I do not know when.  it really be a very convenient thing

when the architect of the old german cathedral use to take a

desperate leap from the top of the tower as soon as it be finish.

well, I must find Mamma now.'



'cannot you wait till the evening, when you may see Papa?' say

Katherine, hope to put off the evil day.



'I cannot have this upon my mind all day unconfessed,' say Elizabeth;

'besides, Harriet will pester I with entreaty as long as it be

untold.  Come, Kitty, do not be such a coward.'



'I be sure I do not want you not to tell,' say Katherine, look

rather miserable; 'only I be not in such a hurry about it as you be.

you do not know where Mamma be.'



'no, but I will find she,' say Elizabeth.



the sister set off on the chase; they look into the drawing-room,

the dining-room, Mrs. Woodbourne's room, without success; they run up

to the nursery, but she be not there; and they be go down again,

when Katherine, see Elizabeth go towards the kitchen stair,

exclaim, 'well, I will go no far; it be so ridiculous, as if it

be a matter of life and death!  you may call if you want I.'



Katherine retreat into her own room, and Elizabeth run down to the

kitchen, where she find Mrs. Woodbourne ordering dinner.



Elizabeth stand by the fire, bite her lip and pinch her finger,

and tremble all over with impatience, while Mrs. Woodbourne and the

cook be busily consult over some grouse which Rupert have bring

from Scotland.



'Lizzie, my dear,' say Mrs. Woodbourne presently, 'would you just run

to my room and fetch down the green receipt-book?'



Elizabeth obey: running be rather a relief to she, and she be

down-stair again in another instant.



'why, Lizzie,' say Mrs. Woodbourne, with a smile, 'you must be wild

to-day; you have bring I the account-book instead of--but, my dear

child, what be the matter?' say she, perceive that Elizabeth's face

be scarlet, and her eye full of tear.



'I will tell you presently,' whisper Elizabeth, breathlessly, 'when

you have do.'  she dart away again, and return with the right

book; but Mrs. Woodbourne be too much alarmed by her manner to spend

another moment in give direction to the cook, and instantly follow

she to her own room.  Elizabeth hastily shut the door, and sit down to

recover her breath.



'my dear Lizzie, there be nothing amiss with any of the--' exclaim

Mrs. Woodbourne, almost gasp for breath.



'oh no, Mamma,' say Elizabeth, a smile pass over her face in spite

of her distress, 'it be not winifred who be mad.  it be I who have be

more mad and foolish and self-willed than you would ever believe.

Mamma, I have be with Mrs. Turner to the Mechanics' Institute.'



'my dear Lizzie, you do not mean it!' say Mrs. Woodbourne.



'yes, Mamma, indeed it be so,' say Elizabeth mournfully; 'I do not

know what have happen there certainly, but I would not listen to

Helen's good advice, and so I have make Papa seem to consent to what he

abhor; I have lead Kate and Anne and Harriet all wrong.  oh! Mamma, be

not it terrible?'



'indeed, I wish I have tell you what your Papa say to Mr. Turner,' say

Mrs. Woodbourne; 'I be afraid your papa will be very much annoyed; but,

my dear, do not distress yourself, you could not know that it be

wrong.'



'yes; but, Mamma,' say Elizabeth, 'I do know that it be wrong to go

out without ask your leave.  simple obedience might have keep I

straight.  but now I will tell you all, and you shall judge what have

good be do about the Hazlebys and Fido.'



rather incoherently, and with many sob, Elizabeth tell the history of

the precede evening.  Mrs. Woodbourne listen to she with the utmost

kindness, and say all she could to soothe and console she, assure

she that Mr. Woodbourne could not be seriously displeased with she for

having transgress a command of which she be ignorant. Elizabeth be

much relieved by having be able to talk over her conduct in this

manner; and though she still feel that she have be very much to blame,

and by no mean sure that Mr. Woodbourne would pass over her fault so

lightly, be greatly comfort by her mamma's kindness.  she go away

to bathe her swollen eye, before she go down to the school-room to

read the Psalms and Lessons with her sister, as be their regular

custom when there be no service at the church, before they begin their

morning's work; Mrs. Woodbourne undertaking to call the child down

in a few minute, and say that she would speak to Katherine in the

course of the day.  she willingly promise to say nothing to Mrs.

Hazleby, and only wish she be quite sure that there be no symptom

of madness about Fido.



'what a strange girl Lizzie be!' cry Harriet, just as Elizabeth

depart on her search for her father or Mrs. Woodbourne.



'but, Harriet,' say Lucy, draw she aside to the window, 'what

difference be she say nothing to make?  Mamma will ask how Fido be

lose.'



'I be sure, Lucy, that be more your fault than mine,' say Harriet; 'I

could not be watch he all the time we be at that place.'



'then why do you take he there?' say Helen.



'because Lucy choose to run away without ever think what I be to

do,' say Harriet.



'but when you be lead he, and it must have be you who let go

his string,' say Helen; 'I cannot see how you can accuse Lucy of

having be the mean of lose he, when she be safe at home.'



Harriet be save from the necessity of find an answer, by hear

her mother call she in the passage, and she hasten to obey the

summon.



'do you know where Fido be?' be Mrs. Hazleby's question.



'no,' say Harriet, find she have only escape one dilemma to fall

into another.  she avoid any further question, however, by hasten

past her mother and run up-stair.



'Lucy, Lucy!' then call Mrs. Hazleby; and as Lucy come out of the

school-room, she repeat the inquiry.



'I do not know, Mamma,' answer Lucy in a low voice, but stand

quite still.



'go and ask for he in the kitchen then,' say Mrs. Hazleby.



'I be afraid it would be of no use. Ma'am,' say Lucy, firmly, but not

dare to raise her eye; 'we miss he when we come in from walk,

yesterday evening.'



'yesterday evening!' cry Mrs. Hazleby; 'and do you never speak of

it?  I never know anyone so careless as you be, in all my life.  it be

of no use to leave anything in your charge, you care for--'



here Lucy leant back and shut the door behind she, so that Anne and

Helen could distinguish nothing but the sound of Mrs. Hazleby's loud

angry voice raise to its high pitch.



'Poor Lucy!' sigh Helen.



'dreadful!' say Anne.



'and how can anyone say that Lucy be not one of the noble, most

self-devote creature upon earth?' exclaim Helen, with tear in her

eye; 'there she be, bear all that terrible scolding, rather than

say it be Harriet's fault, as everyone know it be.  I be sure no one

be like Lucy.  and this be go on continually about something or

other.'



'how can she exist?' say Anne.



'with her acute feeling and painful timidity,' say Helen, 'it be

bad for she than it would be for anyone else, yet how gently and

simply she bear it all! and old Mrs. Hazleby say that she be often

ill after these scolding, and she would have take she away to live

with she, as the Major propose, after Miss Dorothea Hazleby die, but

that she think it would be take away all the comfort of her

father's life.  oh! Anne,' cry Helen, walk up and down the room as

Mrs. Hazleby's voice become loud and louder, 'I cannot bear it; what

shall I do?  oh! if it be but right, if it would not make it bad for

Lucy, I could, I would go out and tell Mrs. Hazleby what everybody

think of she.'



'I do not wonder that Miss Hazleby be ready to do almost anything to

avoid such a scene,' say Anne.



'mean selfish creature!' say Helen; 'she run away on purpose that Lucy

might stay and bear all this.  Anne, I do believe that if martyr be

make, and crown be gain, by daily suffering and hourly

self-denial, that such a crown will be dear dear Lucy's.'



Anne's answer was--



          'and all the happy soul that ride

           Transfigured through that fresh abode,

           have heretofore in humble trust,

           Shone meekly 'mid their native dust,

           the glow-worm of the earth!'





'thank you, Anne,' say Helen, wipe away her tear; 'I will think of

Lucy as the light, the glow-worm of her family.  thank you; the thought

of her meek clear light in darkness need not be gloomy, as it have be.'



Anne have never think of Helen as possess so much enthusiasm, and

be almost more inclined to wonder at she than at Lucy.  while they have

be talk, Mrs. Hazleby's voice have cease, step be now hear in

the passage, and a letter be bring in and give to Helen. it be

from Fanny Staunton, but she have only just time to glance it over,

before the three child come in, follow by their mother and

Elizabeth.  Anne go to call her mother to join they in read the

Psalms and Lessons; and Winifred be send to summon Katherine, who have

purposely linger up-stair till all the rest be assemble.



Elizabeth's eye be very red, and she be afraid to trust her voice

to read the first verse of the Psalm, as it be always her part to do;

but little Dora, who sit next to she, and who seem in part to enter

into her feeling, although she say nothing, read the first verse for

she; and Elizabeth take Edward, who always look over her book, upon

her knee when the Lessons begin, so as to screen her face from her

aunt.  when they have finish, attention be draw away from she by

Edward, who be eagerly assure Lady Merton that the Bible and

Prayer-book which Uncle Edward, his godfather, have give he, be

quite safe, and he be to use they himself when Lizzie think he could

read well enough.  this Dora explain as meaning when he have for a

week abstain from guess word instead of spell they; and

Elizabeth propose to he to try whether he could read to-day without

one mistake.  Edward object to read at that time, as he be to go

out at half-past twelve, and there would be no time for lesson.

Elizabeth demonstrate that it be now only half-past ten, and that it

be impossible that he could spend two hour in put on his good

frock and trowser, and in settle what to buy with the bright

half-crown which Uncle Edward have give he; and Winifred assure he

that she mean to do all her lesson to-day.  Edward look round to

appeal to his mother, but both she and Lady Merton have leave the room,

and he be force to content himself with ask Anne whether she

think there be time.



'oh yes, Edward; I hope you will let I hear how well you can read; I

want to know whether the young robin see any more monster,' say Anne

good-naturedly.



winifre, rather inopportunely, be ready with the information, that

the nest be visit by two more monster; but Anne stop her ear,

and declare she would hear nothing but from Edward himself, and the

young gentleman be thus persuade to begin his lesson.



Helen do not wait to see how the question be decide, but go up to

her own room to enjoy Fanny Staunton's letter.  she pause however a

few moment, to consider whether she should go to Lucy, but think

that it must certainly be painful to she to speak of what have pass,

she proceed to her own room, there to send her whole heart and mind

to dykeland.



Fanny Staunton's letter be overflow with affection and with regret

for Helen's departure; and this, together with her description of her

own and her sister's amusement and occupation, make Helen's heart

yearn more strongly than ever after the friend she have leave.  Anne's

cheerful manner, and Lucy's quiet content, have, the day before, make

Helen rather ashamed of herself, and she have resolve to leave off

pine for dykeland, and to make herself happy, by be useful and

obliging, without think about little grievance, such as almost

everyone could probably find in their own home, if they search for

they.  when she have curl her hair, it be with the hope that the

sacrifice of her tail would convince Elizabeth that she have some

regard for her taste; unfortunately, however, her hair be rather too

soft to curl well, and after having be plait for the last three

month, it be most obstinate in hang deplorably straight, in a way

very uncomfortable to her feeling and irritate to her temper;

besides which, Elizabeth have be too much occupy by her own concern

all the morning, to observe the alteration, and indeed, if she have

remark it, she be not likely to feel as much flatter by this

instance of deference to her opinion, as Helen think she ought to be.

last night, Helen have lament that her own petulance have prevent she

from reason calmly with Elizabeth, and from set before her all

the argument upon which she have discourse so fluently to Lucy, after

the imprudent step have be take; but now, she throw the blame upon

Elizabeth's impetuosity and unkindness, and feel somewhat aggrieved,

because neither of her sister have express a full sense of her

firmness and discretion.  she compare Fanny's affectionate

expression, with Elizabeth's sharp and hasty manner; the admiration

which her friend have make rather too evident, with the wholesome

though severe criticism she sometimes meet with at home; the quietness

at dykeland, with the constant bustle at the vicarage; and end, by

think Mrs. Woodbourne the only person of the family who possess

any gentleness or kindness, and make up her mind that dykeland be

the only pleasant place in England, and that she herself be a most

ill-use person, whose merit be not in the least appreciate.



such be the feeling which gradually take possession of her mind,

while she be write her answer to Fanny's letter; and by the time she

have finish, have bring she into that agreeable frame, which be

disposed to be offend with the first person who do not act up to

its expectation.



Katherine's study, through the whole morning, be to avoid a private

interview with Mrs. Woodbourne; and she really shew considerable

ingenuity in evade she.  if Mrs. Woodbourne call she, she answer,

'yes, Mamma, I be come directly,' but she take care not to come till

she know that her mamma be no long alone; if Lady Merton want

anything which she have leave up-stair, Katherine would officiously

volunteer to fetch it, when particularly tell that she be not want;

if Mrs. Woodbourne move to the door, and make sign to Katherine to

follow she, she work with double assiduity, and never look up

unless to speak to Rupert or to Harriet; and thus she contrive to

elude the reproof she expect, until the whole party, except the two

gentleman, meet at twelve o'clock for an early luncheon, so that there

be no long any danger that Mrs. Woodbourne would find an opportunity

of speak to she, at present.



the three child be to dine late with the rest of the party, and

be in high glee at the prospect of the afternoon's amusement;

Elizabeth seem to have recover her spirit; Harriet be as noisy as

ever; and Lucy, if possible, a little quieter than be she willnot; Anne,

as usual, ready to be amuse with anything; and Rupert quite prepare

to amuse everyone.



Fido be again mention, and Rupert, who have hear about half of the

history of his loss, suggest the possibility of his having be

despatch by the railroad to London, there to be convert into

sausage.  Harriet, after many exclamation of 'o Mr. Merton!' declare

that if she believe such a thing could ever happen, she would never

eat another sausage in her life, and conclude as usual with, 'would

you, Lucy?'  Mrs. Woodbourne inquire anxiously after Winifred's hand.

Mrs. Hazleby be on the point of take fire at the imply suspicion

of her lament favourite's sanity, when Rupert avert the threaten

danger, by a grave examination of Winifred and Meg Merrilies, who have

both be wound, and conclude by recommend that as soon as puss

shewed symptom of hydrophobia, Winifred should be smother between

two feather-bed, to prevent further mischief.  everyone laugh,

except Dora, who think the proposal exceedingly shocking; and Rupert

argue very gravely with she on the expediency of the measure, until

she be call away to prepare for the walk.









CHAPTER X.





Dora re-consider her argument while put on her bonnet, and the

instant the walk party be outside the front door, she begin again.

'but, Rupert, it would be commit murder to kill Winifred, even if

she have the Fidophobia.'



'no, no, Dora,' say Rupert, 'it be your mamma and Lizzie who have the

Fidophobia.'



'what can you mean?' say Helen; 'how can you frighten the child so,

Rupert?'



'do not you know, Helen,' say Elizabeth, ''tis his vocation.  he be a

true Knight Rupert.'



'expound, most learn cousin,' say Rupert; 'you be too deep.'



'you must know,' say Elizabeth, 'that Knecht Ruprecht be the german

terrifier of naughty child, the same as the chimney-sweeper in

England, or Coeur de Lion in Palestine, or the Duke of Wellington in

France.



          'Baby, baby, he's a giant,

             tall and black as Rouen steeple;

           and he dine and sup, 'tis say,

             every day, on naughty people.'





'I should have think,' say Rupert, 'that consider my namesake's

babe-bolt propensity, and his great black dog, that he would have

be more likely to be hold up in terrorem in England.'



'I suppose there be some old grim Sir Rupert in Germany,' say

Elizabeth; 'but my dictionary be my only authority.'



'you be take knecht to mean a knight,' say Anne, 'contrary to your

argument last night.  Knecht Ruprecht's origin be not nearly so sublime

as you would make it out.  Keightley's Fairy Mythology say he be only

our old friend Robin good-fellow, Milton's lubber fiend, the Hob

Goblin.  you know, Rupert, and Robert, and Hob, be all the same name,

Rudbryht, bright in speech.'



'and a hobbish fellow mean a gentleman as clumsy as the lubber fiend,'

say Elizabeth.



'no doubt he wear hob-nail in his shoe,' say Rupert.



'and chimney hob be so call, because his cream bowl be duly set

upon they,' say Anne.



'and he be as familiar as the Robin Redbreast,' say Elizabeth.



'and wear a red waistcoat like he, and like Herb Robert,' say Anne.



'as shabby as this flower,' say Elizabeth, gather a ragged Robin

from the hedge.



'well do, etymology,' say Rupert; 'now for syntax and prosody.'



'I hope we have be talk syntax all this time,' say Elizabeth; 'we

will keep prosody for the evening, and then play at Conglomeration.'



they now come to some bright green water-meadow, which border the

little stream as soon as it leave the town.  there be a broad dry path

by the river side, and as they walk along it, there be no lack of

laughter or merriment in anyone but Helen, and she could find no

amusement in anything she see or hear.  at last, however, she be

highly delighted at the sight of some plant of purple loose-strife,

grow on the bank.  'oh!' cry she, 'that be the flower that be so

beautiful at dykeland.'



'what! the loose strife?' say Elizabeth, 'it be common enough in all

damp place.'



Poor Helen! as if this slight to the flower she admire be not a

sufficient shock to her feeling, Rupert, perfectly unconscious on what

tender ground he be treading, say, 'if it be a lover of damp, I be

sure it can nowhere be well suit than at dykeland.  do you grow

web-footed there, Helen?'



'o rupert,' say Helen, 'I be sure the garden be always quite dry.'



'except when it be wet,' say Elizabeth.



'that be certainly the case when I be there two year ago,' observe

Rupert; 'I could not stir two step from the door without meet with

a pool deep enough to swim a man-of-war.'



'Rupert,' say Elizabeth, 'I hereby give notice, that whosoever say

one single word against the perfect dryness, cleanliness, and beauty,

of dear dykeland, commit high treason against Miss Helen Woodbourne;

and as protect disconsolate damsel be the bounden duty of a true

knight and cavalier, I advise you never to mention the subject, on pain

of be consider a discourteous recreant.'



'Lizzie, how can you?' say Helen peevishly.



'how strange it be,' say Anne, 'that so many old family house should

have be build in damp place.'



'our ancestor be once apparently frog,' say Rupert; unhappily

remind Helen of her sister's parody.



'well,' say Elizabeth, 'I can understand why monastery should have

be build in damp place, near river or bog, both for the sake of

the fish, and to be useful in drain; but why any other mortal except

Dutchmen, tadpole, and newts, should delight in mud and mire, pass

my poor comprehension.'



Rupert point to a frog which Dora's foot have startle from its

hiding-place, and say, 'pray, why, accord to my theory, should not

the human kind have once be frog? leap-frog be only a return to

our natural mean of progression.'



'and bull-frog in a course of become stalwart gentleman,' say Anne.



'yes, we often hear of a croak disposition, do not we, Helen?' say

Elizabeth; 'you see both that propensity, and a love of marsh, be

but indication of a former state of existence.'



'and I be sure that your respectable neighbour, Mr. Turner, be a toad

on his hind leg,' say Rupert.



'minus the precious jewel,' say Elizabeth.



'by-the-bye,' say Rupert, 'be there not some mystery about that

gentleman?  this morning I hazard a supposition, in the drawing-room,

that the lose darling we have hear so much of, might have be

dissect for the benefit of Mr. Turner's pupil, and thereupon arise a

most wonderful whispering between Kate and one of your sweet cousin

there, Lizzie, about some nephew, an Adolphus or Augustus, or some such

name; but the more question I ask, the more dark and mysterious do

the young lady become.'



'I wonder if it be possible!' cry Elizabeth, with a sudden start.



'what be possible?' ask Anne.



'that Rupert should be right,' say Elizabeth; 'be Mrs. Hazleby in the

room when you speak?'



'yes, but what of that?' say Rupert.



'that you, talk at random,' say Elizabeth, 'very nearly betray

Harriet's grand secret.'



'really, the affair become quite exciting,' say Rupert; 'pray do not

leave I in suspense, explain yourself.'



'I do not think I can, Rupert,' say Elizabeth, not wish to expose

Harriet, for Mrs. Woodbourne's sake.



'then I be to understand,' say Rupert, 'that Miss Hazleby have

present Fido to this noble Adolphus, as a pledge of the tender

friendship, and that you and Kate act as confidant.'



'Nonsense, Rupert,' say Anne, try to check he by a look.



'and I suppose,' proceed Rupert, 'that the gentleman be to extract

poor Fido's faithful heart, and wear it next his own.  I never should

have devise so refined and sentimental a souvenir.  it be far beyond

forget-I-not and arrow.  so professional too.'



Elizabeth and Anne laugh so much that they could neither of they

speak for some moment; but when Anne recover, she take her brother

by the arm and whisper, 'Rupert, the less you say about the Turners

or Fido, the well.  I will explain it all to you when we have an

opportunity.'



Elizabeth thank she by a look; and at this moment Dora, who have be

far in advance with Katherine and the Hazlebys, come run back to

beg Rupert to gather for she some fine bulrush which grow on the

brink of the river.  Rupert be very willing to comply with her

request; but Elizabeth recommend Dora to leave they till they should

return, and not to take the trouble of carry they to Whistlefar

Castle and back again.



leave the river, they begin to ascend a steep chalky lane, which have

be wet all the winter, and be now full of rough harden wheel-rut

and hole make by slip horse.  Elizabeth think that Robert

Bruce's calthorp could hardly have make the ground more uneven, and

she be just go to say so, when Helen groan out, 'what a horrid

place!  I slip and bruise my ancle every minute.' upon which she

immediately take the other side of the question, and answer, 'it be

not nearly so bad as the long lane on the down, and you never complain

of that.'



'oh! but this be all up-hill,' say Helen.



'I be not in the least tired, Helen,' say Dora, who with Rupert's

assistance be take fly leap over the rut.



'you? no, I should think not,' say Helen, in so piteous a tone, that

rupert very good-naturedly wait till she come up to he, and then

offer she his arm.



on see this, Harriet be rather vex that she have not be first

notice by the gentleman, and begin to make heavy complaint of the

badness of the road, but no one pay much attention to she. Elizabeth

however give her arm to Lucy, who never could bear much fatigue.



after they have gain the top of the hill, they walk on for some

distance between high hedge, and as none of the party know the way

far than the river, except from some direction give they by Mr.

Walker, the Curate, they begin to think that they must have miss a

turn to the left, which he have tell they to take.  Harriet and Helen

both declare that they have pass the turning; Katherine be sure they

have not; and Elizabeth say that she have see a turn to the right some

way behind they, but that to the left be yet to come. as they could

not agree upon this question, Rupert walk onwards to explore, leave

the young lady to rest on the trunk of a tree lie by the side of

the road.  while he be go, Elizabeth draw Helen aside, say,

'Helen, you have well take care, I hope Rupert have not observe how

much out of humour you be.'



'I be not out of humour,' say Helen, accord to the usual fashion of

deny such a charge.



'then why do you look and speak as if you be?' say her sister; 'you

have well watch yourself.'



'I think you be enough to vex anyone, Lizzie,' say Helen; 'bring

I ever so far out of the way on such a road as this, and then scold

I for say I do not like it.'



'I see,' answer Elizabeth, 'you be not in a fit state to be reason

with.'



'no,' retort Helen, who have indulge in her ill-humour till she

hardly know what she say, 'you will never condescend to hear what I

have to say.  perhaps it might be as well sometimes if you would.'



'yes, Helen,' say Elizabeth, colour and turn away, 'it would

indeed.  I know I have give you a right to upbraid I.'



at this moment Rupert come back, cheer the droop courage of the

without a turning,' and he have find the one they be seek.



thing now go on well; they come to a shady green path by the side

little improve by the coolness.  soon, however, they have to cross two

quietly that she have no opportunity for one.  they now find themselves

'why, Helen, how soon you be tired!' say Rupert; 'I be afraid

dykeland do not agree with you.'



'Helen be only a little cross, she will be well presently,' say

Dora, in so comical a tone, that Rupert, Katherine, and Harriet all

laugh, and Helen say sharply, 'Dora, do not be pert.'



Rupert be really a very good-natured youth, but it would have require

more forbearance than he possess, to abstain from teaze so tempting

a subject as poor Helen be at this moment.



'and how do you know that Helen be a little cross, Dora, my dear?' say

he.



'because she look so,' say Dora.



'and how do people look when they be a little cross, Dora?'



'I do not know,' answer Dora.



'do they look so, my dear?' say Rupert, mimic poor Helen's

woe-begone face in a very droll way.



Dora laugh, and Helen be still more displeased.  'Dora, it be very

naughty,' say she.



'what! to look cross?' say Rupert; 'certainly, be it not, Dora?'



Elizabeth and Anne be far in the rear, reach for some botanical

any of the party concern.  Dora be rather a matter-of-fact little

'why not?' say Rupert, rather sorry to be check in the full

enjoyment of his own wit.



'because Helen do not like it,' say Dora.



'but, Dora,' say Rupert, wish to try the little girl rather

far, 'do not you think she deserve it, for be out of temper?'



'I do not know,' say Dora gravely, 'but I know it be not right or kind

to say what vexe she, and I shall not stay with you any long,

Rupert, if you will do it.'



so say, Dora, well-name Discreet Dolly, run away to Lucy, of whom

she be very fond.



Rupert be both amuse and surprise at Dora's behaviour, and perhaps,

at the same time, a little ashamed and pique by a little girl of seven

in the mean time Helen walk on alone, a little behind the rest of the

some moderately, other immoderately.



'what be the matter?' ask Helen.



'I do not know,' say Elizabeth, 'unless Rupert be halloo because he

be out of the wood.'

'never,' say several voice.



'then listen, listen, lady all,' say Rupert.  'you must know that

once upon a time there be a most beautiful princess, who live in a

splendid castle, where she receive all kind of company.  well, one

day, there arrive an old grim palmer, just like the picture of

Hopeful, in the Pilgrim's Progress, with a fine stripe cockle-shell

stick upright in his hat-band.  well, the cockle-shell tickle the

many suitor as Penelope) promise that he would steal it from he that

the palmer's couch, and gently abstract the cockle hat and staff,

the day before, the lady have order that the fool should be whip,

for mock she, when she could not get the marrow neatly out of a bone

all, however, have vanish away from vulgar eye, and can only be

bring to light by be thrice whistle for.  a slight tradition have

remain, and the place have ever since be know by the mysterious

name of Whistlefar.'



'and have no one ever find it?' say Dora.



'I cannot say,' answer Rupert.



'a deed of such high emprise can only be reserve for the great Prince

Rupert himself,' say Elizabeth.



'how can such nonsensical tradition be keep up?' say Harriet; 'I

think everyone have forget such absurd old story, only fit to

'oh! you know nobody believe they,' say Katherine.



'but, Rupert,' say Helen, 'this must be a modern story, it cannot be a

genuine old legend, it be really not accord to the spirit of those

time to say that a palmer could be an enchanter, or so revengeful.'



'oh!' say Rupert, 'you know everything bad be to be learn among the

'still,' say Helen, 'if you consider the purpose for which the Palmers

'the frock do not make the friar,' say Rupert, 'and this may have

be a bad palmer.  think of the Knights Templars.'



'besides,' say Helen, 'how could the squire see either palmer or

'I suppose there be lamp in the court,' say Rupert; 'but



          "I cannot tell how the truth may be,

           I tell the tale as 'twas tell to I."'





'but who tell you, Rupert?' say Helen.



'why, the story of Red Mantle, Helen, cannot you see?' say Elizabeth;

'it be on the table all the morning.'



'O Lizzie, be there ever anything so cruel?' cry Rupert; 'Edie

and you will not even let I enjoy the credit of originality.'



'I be sure I give you credit due,' say Elizabeth; 'it be really an

and Triermain, and the cockle-shell shield and star-fish spur form an

agreeable variation.'



'I never will tell another story in your presence, Lizzie,' say

'I really think you deserve it, Rupert,' say Anne; 'I cannot pity

'oh! I dare not open my lip before such devotee of crusading,' say

'and pray, Rupert,' say Elizabeth, 'what do you mean by compare I

at this moment, happily for Rupert, they come to the top of a small

it cannot be deny that the young party be a little disappointed by

on the way home the party be not quite so merry, although Helen be

'what?' say some of the girl.



'a dead dog, I believe,' say Rupert.



'oh! let I see,' cry Harriet, advance cautiously over the morass.

eat he in a sausage; you may be at rest on that score.'

haste, the island be sink fast.'



'I be splash all over, so I do not care.  can I have one more look?'

Rupert hand she back to the island, where she take she last farewell

'I hope,' say Rupert, as they proceed with their walk, 'that you be

Winifred's have the Fidophobia.'



'perhaps,' say Elizabeth, 'he have some knowledge of the frightful

suspicion which attach to he, and, like the irish varmint in St.

Patrick's day,



                      "go flop,

          slap bang into the water,

          and thus commit suicide

          to save himself from slaughter."'





they now begin to consider how Fido could have meet with his death.

'oh! that be no mystery at all,' say Elizabeth; 'we cross the bridge

'yes, Rupert,' say Elizabeth.

'still,' say Rupert, 'without appear as the preserver of the fair

'you, Rupert?' say Anne.



'Myself, Anne,' reply Rupert; 'you have no notion what friend Mrs.

'on speculation,' say Elizabeth.



'and finally, how she come to marry the Major.'



'o Rupert, that be too much; you must have invent it!' cry Anne.

'poor man!' say Elizabeth, 'I always wonder how he chance to fall

into her clutch.  but do you hear no more?'



'no more of her personal history,' say Rupert; 'she kindly employ

'oh! pray let we have the benefit of they,' say Anne, who have by this

'there be many regret that I be not in the army,' say Rupert, 'and

they find the hall table cover with parcel, which shew that Mrs.

Anne find her mamma in her room, as well as Sir Edward, who be

finish a letter.



'well, Mamma, have you a prosperous journey?' say she.



'yes, very much so,' say Lady Merton: 'Mrs. Hazleby be in high

'and what have you be do, Miss Anne?' say Sir Edward; 'you be

'I hope Mrs. Woodbourne will be happy again,' say Lady Merton.

'I cannot see what induce you to go,' say Sir Edward; 'I think

'I believe the actual impulse be give by a dispute between Lizzie and

'and so Rupert's friend, the Turners, be great authority in

'Midsummer madness,' say Sir Edward.



'but just tell I, Papa,' say Anne, 'do you not think Helen quite the

'I think Helen very much improve in appearance and manner,' say Sir

'do not wait to tell it now, Anne,' say Lady Merton, 'or Mrs.

'I will keep it all for the journey home,' say Anne, 'when Papa's ear

'and his tongue too, to give you a lecture upon Radicalism, Miss,' say

'poor little fellow! your aunt will be very sorry,' be kind Mrs.

'but now, Mamma,' say Elizabeth, 'you may be quite easy about

'how could he have fall in, poor little dog?' say Mrs. Woodbourne.

'I wish the loss of Fido be all the mischief likely to come of it, my

'my dear child,' say Mrs. Woodbourne, 'do not go on make yourself

future.  now do put your scarf even, and let I pin this piece of lace

straight for you, it be high on one side than the other, and your

band be twist.'



on her side, Lucy, tremble as she enter her mother's room, but firm

in her purpose of preserve her sister from the temptation to

Hazleby.  her information be receive with a short angry 'h--m,' and

Harriet some wonderful bargain which she have meet with at Baysmouth.









CHAPTER XI.





as soon as Mrs. Hazleby make her appearance in the drawing-room before

dinner, Rupert begin repeat,



          'the wound it seem both sore and sad

             to every christian eye,

           and while they swear the dog be mad,

             they swear the child would die,



           but soon a wonder come to light,

             that shew the rogue they lie,

           the child recover of the bite,

             it be the dog that die.'





'I beg to offer my congratulation,' continue he, set a chair for

she.



Mrs. Hazleby look surprised.



'on the demonstration we have this day receive of your superior

judgement, Ma'am,' say Rupert, 'though indeed we could hardly have

doubt it before.'



'pray let I understand you, Mr. Merton,' say Mrs. Hazleby.



'have you not hear of the circumstance to which I allude?' say

Rupert; 'for if you be not already aware of it, I must beg to be

excuse from relate it; I could not bear to give so great a shock to

a lady's feeling.'



'oh! you mean about Fido,' say Mrs. Hazleby, almost smile; 'yes,

Lucy tell I that you have find he.  really, my girl be so careless,

I can trust nothing to they.'



'indeed, Madam,' say Rupert, 'I assure you that nothing could have

be more heart-rend than the scene present to our eye when the

Miss Hazlebys first become aware of the untimely fate of their

favourite.  who could behold it with dry eye--or dry foot?' add he,

in an under-tone, with a side glance at Anne.



Rupert contrive to talk so much nonsense to Mrs. Hazleby, that he

charm she with his attention, give she no time to say anything about

Fido, and leave Anne much surprised that she have never find out that he

be laugh at she.  at dinner, the grouse he have bring come to

their aid; Mrs. Hazleby be delighted to taste a blackcock once more,

and be full of reminiscence of Inchlitherock; and by mean of these

recollection, and Rupert's newly import history, Sir Edward and

Mr. Woodbourne contrive to make the conversation more entertaining

than Elizabeth think it ever could be in any party in which Mrs.

Hazleby be present.



afterwards in the drawing-room, Dora's bulrush and the other

child's purchase be duly admire, and the little people, be

rather fatigued, be early send to bed, although Edward vehemently

insist, with his eye half shut, that he be not in the least sleepy.

the elder girl then arrange themselves round the table. Helen be

work a bunch of rose of different colour; Anne admire it very

much, but critic be not want to this, as to every other

performance of Helen's.



'it be all very pretty except that rise,' say Katherine, 'but I be

sure that be an unnatural colour.--Is it not, Anne?'



'I do not think that I ever see one like it,' say Anne; 'but that be

no proof that there be no such flower.'



'what do you think, Lizzie?' say Katherine; 'ought not Helen to alter

it?'



Anne be rather alarm by this appeal; but Elizabeth answer

carelessly, without look up, 'oh! you know I know nothing about that

kind of work.'



'but you can tell what colour a rose be,' persist Katherine; 'now do

not you think Helen will spoil her work with that orange-coloured rose?

who ever hear of such a thing?'



Helen be on the point of say that one of the gable-end of the

she remember that dykeland be not a safe subject, and refrain.



'come, do not have a York and Lancaster war about an orange-colour

rise, Kate,' say Elizabeth, come up to Helen; 'why, Anne, where be

your eye? do you never see an austrian briar, just the the colour of

Helen's lamb-wool?'



though this be a mere trifle, Helen be pleased to find that Elizabeth

'why, Anne,' say Elizabeth, presently after, 'you be do that old

'yes,' say Anne; 'it be a pattern which I like very much.'

be read it to we, when I be at work upon it.'



'there certainly seem to be a use and pleasure in never sit down

'Anne think as I do,' say Mrs. Woodbourne; 'I find carpet-work quite

'yes,' say Katherine; 'you always read with Meg in your lap, when you

Elizabeth do not however long continue work, for as soon as tea be

Consent be give, and while the candle be be bring, and

something unpleasant will surely come of it.'



'oh! Anne,' be the answer, 'you have rob I of my good couplet--



          weeping like forsaken Dido,

          when she find the slaughter Fido.



where be the use of play if there be to be no fun?'



''where be the use of fun?' say the cockchafer to the boy who be

spin it,' say Anne.



'Impertinence, impertinence, impertinence,' say Rupert, shake his

head at she.



by this time all be ready, and Elizabeth call the brother and sister

'HARROGATE,' say he, 'what be to be do with such a word?'

'BOUNCE, no,' say Katherine; 'it be BONNET, I write it myself.'

'I must give you a lesson in silence, Mr. Rupert,' say Elizabeth.



'I obey,' say Rupert, with a funny face of submission, and take up

'so this be all that you have to shew for yourself,' say Elizabeth,

hold up a scrap of paper; 'what be all this?'



'a portrait of Miss Merton,' say Rupert; 'do not you see the poet's

eye in a fine frenzy roll?'



'be it?' say Elizabeth; 'I take it for Miss Squeers in the agony of
