it, at least.



    do ever you see a stupider POEM?

    pray who be the author?  I know he, I know he,

    he go to school to Mr. Squeers,

    who often make the youth shed TEARS.



now for the next, which be nearly as short.





    I will write a POEM,

    clear and flow,

    it will make you shed TEARS,

    and excite your fear.

    'tis about a witch,

    drown in a ditch,

    your tear come from your eyes.

    if you be wise,

    don't make a bounce,

    or you'll tear your flounce,

    and upset the sugar JAR,

    which I cannot spare,

    I must give some to FRANCIS,

    so well he dance;

    Sugar cane pack up in LEAVES,

    the cane be tie up like wheat sheaf;

    Francis wear a scarlet jacket,

    he make a dreadful racket

    at HARROGATE,

    because he have to wait,

    in a field of BARLEY,

    to hold a parley,

    about a bone of marrow;

    his heart be transfix by an ARROW,

    by a lady in VELVET,

    and he be her pet.'





all laugh heartily at this poem, which perhaps divert they more

than a well would have do; Harriet be highly delighted with what

she consider their applause, though she know that of all the rhyme,

scarcely three have be find by herself.



'why, Mr. Merton, what be you do?' ask Harriet; 'be you write

any more?'



'oh! I hope he will tell we about Mr. Squeers,' say Katherine.



no one could doubt that the next which Elizabeth read be her own.





    I'm afraid you expect a beautiful poem,

    though I make a long and tedious proem,

    but great and dreadful be my fear,

    no poem of mine will put you in TEARS.

    my genius suggest neither fairy nor witch,

    my tale to adorn with cauldron of pitch,

    Alarm the world with fiery eyes,

    and from the hero snatch his prize,

    leap out from her den with a terrible bounce,

    and on the trembling damsel pounce,

    and bottle she up in a close cork JAR,

    or whirl she away in a flaming car;

    then her knight, the brave Sir FRANCIS,

    upon his noble steed advance,

    all his armour off he leave,

    Preserves alone his polished greave,

    his defence be a buff JACKET,

    nor sword nor axe nor lance can crack it,

    it be make at HARROGATE,

    by a tailor whose shop have a narrow gate;

    the elf attack with spear of BARLEY,

    but he drive they off, oh! rarely,

    then they shoot he with an ARROW,

    from bow-string grease with ear-wig' marrow,

    the feather, moth-wing downy VELVET,

    the bow-string, of the spider's net:

    thousand come, arm in this PATTERN,

    which prove their mistress be no slattern;

    some wear the leg and hoof of PAN,

    and some be in the form of man;

    but the knight be arm, for in his pocket

    he have a talismanic locket,

    which once belong to HERCULES,

    who wear it on his bunch of key;

    the fairy come, quite old and fat,

    Mounted upon a monstrous BAT;

    around the knight a web she weave,

    and hold he fast, and there she leave

    Sir Francis weep for his charmer,

    and long for his knightly ARMOUR.

    but his sword be cast in the self-same forge

    as that of the great champion GEORGE;

    thus he defy the witch's army,

    he break his band; 'ye elf, beware I,

    I fear not your leviathan,

    no spell can stop a desperate man.'

    away in terror fly the rear-GUARD,

    he seize on the witch abhor,

    confine she in a COCKLE SHELL,

    and break all her enchantment fall,

    catch her principal LIEUTENANT,

    make he of a split pine the tenant;

    carry away the lady, nimble,

    as e'er Miss Merton ply her thimble;

    oh! this story would your frown unbend.

    could I tell it to the END.





'oh!' say Rupert, glad to seize an opportunity of retaliate upon

Elizabeth; 'I give you credit; a very ingenious compound of Thalaba,

pigwiggin, and the Tempest, and the circumstance of the witch whirling

away the lady be something new.'



'no, it be not,' say Elizabeth; 'it be the beginning of the story of

the Palace of Truth, in the Veillees du Chateau.  I only profess to

conglomerate the word, not to pass off my story as a regular old

traditional legend.'



'well, well,' say Rupert; 'go on; have you only two more?'



'only two,' say Elizabeth; 'Kate and Lucy behave as shabbily as you

do.  Helen, I believe you must read your.  I can never read your

writing readily, and besides, I be grow hoarse.'



Helen obey.





    how hard it be to write a POEM,

      graceful and witty, plain and clear,

    hard than ploughing--'tis, or sowing,

      so hard that I should shed a tear.



    do I not know the high pitch

      of merit, in the poet's eyes

    be but to laugh, a height to which

      'tis not so hard for I to rise.



    for badness soon be gain, forth BOUNCE

      my rhyme such as they be;

    good critic, on my line don't pounce,

      though on the ear they JAR.



    I've have a letter from dear france,

      who say, through the light plane tree leave,

    upon the lawn the sun-beam glance,

      the wheat be bind up in its sheaf



    by Richard, in the fustian JACKET

      his mistress buy at HARROGATE,

    and up in lofty rick they stack it,

      there for the threshing will it wait.



    then will they turn to field of BARLEY,

      Bearded and barb with many an ARROW,

    just where the fertile soil be marly,

      and in the spring be use the harrow.



    draw by the steed in coat of VELVET,

      Old Steady, Jack, and Slattern,

    their mane well comb, and black as jet,

      their tail in the same PATTERN.



    while Richard's son, with pipe of PAN,

      his hand within his pockets,

    walk close beside the old plough-man,

      dreaming of squibs and rocket.



    that youth, he greatly love his ease,

      he's grow much too fat,

    and though as strong as HERCULES,

      he'll only use his BAT.



    he willn't sweep up the autumn LEAVES,

      the tree's deciduous ARMOUR,

    no scold Dickey's spirit grieve

      like work like a farmer,



    or labour like his cousin GEORGE,

      with arm all bare and brawny,

    within the blacksmith's glow forge;

      he would be in the ARMY.



    but no, young Dick, you're not the man

      our realm to watch and ward,

    for bad than a LEVIATHAN

      you'd dread the foe's rear-guard,



    and in the storm of shot and SHELL,

      you'd soon desert your pennant,

    care nought for serjeant, corporal,

      or general LIEUTENANT,



    but prove yourself quite swift and nimble,

      and thus would meet your END;

    no, well take a tailor's thimble

      and learn your way to mend.





'Capital, Helen!' say Elizabeth.



'how very pretty!' say Lucy.



'and very well describe,' say Anne; 'you have bring in those

ungainly word most satisfactorily.'



'now, Helen, here be Anne's,' say Elizabeth; 'it be a choice one, and

I have keep it for the last.'



'let I read Anne's,' say Rupert; 'no one can decypher her writing as

well as I can.'



'as be prove by the thorough acquaintance you shew with the

content of her last letter,' say Elizabeth.



Rupert begin as follow:



    now must I write in number flow

    extemporaneously a POEM?





'why, Rupert,' cry Anne, 'you must be read Kate's.  mine begin

with--'



'I declare that I have yours in my hand, Anne,' say Rupert.



'and I do not write one,' say Katherine.





    now must I write in number flow

    extemporaneously a POEM?

    one that will fill your eye with TEARS,

    while I relate how our bad fear

    be realize in yonder ditch.

    convey there by some water-WITCH,

    we find, sad sight for long EYES!

    Fido, much love, though small in size.

    hard fate, but while our tear bemoan it,

    let we take up the corpse and bone it,

    then place the mummy in a JAR,

    keep it from sausage-maker far,

    extract his heart to send to FRANCIS;

    this gift from she, his soul entrance,

    within his scarlet gold-laced jacket

    his heart make a tremendous racket;

    vision of bliss arise, a surrogate,

    ay, and a wedding tour to HARROGATE.





when Rupert come to Fido, Anne utter one indignant 'Rupert!' but as

he proceed, she be too much confound to make the slight

'what be a pennant?' say Elizabeth; 'I do not like word to be twist

for the sake of the rhyme.'



a flag,' say Helen.



'I never doubt that you intend it for a flag,' say Elizabeth; 'but

what I complain of be, that it be a transmogrified pennon.'

            "I'll tell you a story

          about Jack A'Nory,

             and now my story's begin;

          I'll tell you another

             about Jack and his brother,

          and now my story be do."



and that your friend Francis must have be the hero who complain so

but Anne, with Helen's paper in her hand, have already attack Sir

Helen be hardly ever in a state of great surprise and pleasure than

when Mr. Woodbourne ring the bell, Elizabeth gather up all the

Anne laugh,



'but do it not seem incredible,' say Elizabeth, 'that I, Elizabeth

          "forward he rush with double glee

           into the tide of revelry."



and I believe that have anything on my mind put I in wilder

spirit, apparently, than usual, but I be sure that my merriment to-day

be no proof that I be happy.  it be partly, I believe, from a mad

but Anne's silence be often very expressive to those who could

the toilette of Katherine and Helen pass in a very different manner

about the game they have be play at, in order to prevent Helen from

say anything about the result of their excursion the evening before,

and to keep herself from think of the cowardly part she have be

act all day.  Helen only wish to be leave in peace, to think over

her share in all these transaction, and to consider how she might

become a tolerably useful member of society for the future; and on she

make no reply to one of Katherine's speech, the latter suddenly

become silent, and she be leave to her own reflection.









CHAPTER XII.





Elizabeth be always fully employ on a Sunday, and on that which

follow the Consecration she have perhaps more on her hand even than

usual, so that she have little opportunity for speak, or even for

thinking, of her trouble.



Mr. Woodbourne be go to assist Mr. Somerville in the service at

St. Austin's, leave Mr. Walker to do the duty at St. Mary's, as the

old church be now to be always call.



Mr. Somerville have ask Mrs. Woodbourne to bring all her party to

luncheon at his house, and have add a special invitation to the

child to be present at the opening of the new Sunday-school, which

be to take place between the service.  it be however necessary that

someone should stay and superintend what the young people call,

rather contemptuously, 'the old school;' and this Elizabeth undertook,

say that she do not like to lose one Sunday's teaching of her own

class.  Anne be about to offer to remain with she and assist she, but

on Helen's make the same proposal, she think it well to give the

sister an opportunity of be alone together, and, as she be more

desirous of do right than of appear eager to be useful, she say

nothing of what she have intend.  Elizabeth be much gratified by her

sister's voluntary proffer of assistance, for the head and front of

Helen's offence on her return from dykeland, have be, that she have

loathe the idea of help to train the scream school-girl to sing

in church, and have altogether shew far less interest in parish matter

than Elizabeth think their due.



'I be sure,' say Elizabeth, as they be walk from school to

church, 'it be worth while to stay to see the aisle now it be clear of

the bench, and there be breathing room leave in the dear old church.

and listen to the bell! do not it seem as if the two church be

exchange greeting on St. Austin's first Sunday? yes, St. Mary's be

our home, our mother church,' add she, as she walk under the heavy

stone porch, its groin roof rich with quaint boss, the support of

many a swallow's nest, and come in sight of the huge old square font,

stand on one large column and four small one, where she herself and

all her brother and sister have be christen.



the three little child be not to go to St. Austin's in the

morning, but Katherine have promise to come back to fetch they in time

for the luncheon at Mr. Somerville's, and thus Dora have the full

advantage of study the Puddington monument before the service begin.



Katherine and Harriet come back whilst Elizabeth and Helen be at

luncheon, and after give they a list of half the people who be at

church, they call the child to come to Mr. Somerville's with they.



'why do not you put on your bonnet, Dora?' say Winifred.



'I be not go,' say Dora.



'why not?' ask Winifred.



'because I have rather not,' be the answer.



'why, you silly little child,' say Katherine; 'be you shy of Mr.

Somerville? look there, Edward and Winifred be not shy, and you be

quite a great girl.  how Horace would laugh!'



'I cannot help it,' say Dora; 'I have rather not go.'



'if you be think of your little class, Dora,' say Elizabeth, 'I

will hear they for you; you will trust they with I, will you not? and

I will remember who be first.'



'thank you,' say Dora; 'I have rather go to church and school with you.'



'Nonsense, Dora,' say Katherine; 'I wish you would come.'



'now do,' say Harriet; 'you cannot think what a nice luncheon Mr.

Somerville will have for you.'



'there be a very nice luncheon here,' say Dora.



'oh! but not like a company luncheon,' say Harriet; 'besides, Mr.

Somerville will be so disappointed if you do not come.  Poor Mr.

Somerville, willn't you be sorry for he, Dora?'



'oh no, he do not want I--do he, Lizzie?' say Dora.



'no, I do not suppose he do,' say Elizabeth; 'he only ask you out

of good nature.'



'well, if Dora will not come,' say Katherine, 'there be no use in

staying.--Come, Winifred and Edward.'



Elizabeth be sure that Dora have reason of her own for choose to

remain with she, but she think it well to ask no question; and the

reason appear, when, as they come into the Alms-house Court after

evening service, Dora press her hand, say, in a low mysterious

tone, 'Lizzie, will you shew I what you promise?'



Elizabeth know what she mean, and return through the church into

the church-yard, lead the way to the east end, where, close beside a

project buttress, Dora behold a plain flat white stone, with three

small crosse engraven on it, and with a feeling between awe and

wonder, read the simple inscription.





                               KATHERINE,



                   wife of the REV. HORATIO WOODBOURNE,



                    vicar of ABBEYCHURCH ST. MARY'S,



                             MAY 14TH, 1826,



                                AGED 28.





it be the first time that Elizabeth and Helen have stand together at

their mother's grave, for Helen be but three year old at the time she

have be deprive of she, and, after their father's second marriage, a

kind of delicacy in Elizabeth, young as she be, have prevent she from

ever mention she to her young sister.



after a few minute, during which no one speak, the three sister

turn away, and re-enter the church.  Helen and Dora have reach the

north door, and be leave the church, when they miss Elizabeth,

and look round, see she sit in one of the low pew, in the

centre aisle, her face raise towards the flamboyant tracery of the

east window.  Dora, who seem to have a sort of perception that her

presence be a restraint upon her sister, whisper, 'I be go to

feed the dove,' and hasten across the quadrangle, while Helen come

back to Elizabeth's side.  her sister rise, and with her own bright

smile, say, 'Helen, I could not help come here, it be where I sit

at the day of the funeral, and I want to look at that flame-shape

thing in the top of the window, as I do all through the reading of the

Lesson.  do you see?  what strange thought be in my head, as I sat

look at that deep blue glass, with its shape like an angel's head

and meet wing, and hear of glory celestial! I never hear those

word without see that form.'



with these word Elizabeth and Helen leave the church; Helen put her arm

into her sister's, a thing which Elizabeth very seldom like anyone to

do, even Anne, but now the two girl walk slowly arm-in-arm, through

the quadrangle, and along the broad gravel path in the Vicarage garden.



'then you be at her funeral?' be the first thing Helen say.



'yes,' say Elizabeth; 'Papa wish it, and I be sure I be very glad

they let I go.'



no more be speak till Helen begin again.  'when I be at dykeland,

Mrs. Staunton use often to talk to I about our mother, and I begin to

try to recollect she, but I have only an impression of something kind,

some voice I should know again, but I could not remember she in the

least.'



'ah! I wish you could,' say Elizabeth thoughtfully.



'I suppose you remember she quite well,' say Helen, 'and all that

happen?'



'yes,' say Elizabeth, 'I remember some thing as well as if they have

happen yesterday, and other be all confusion in my mind; I quite

remember go to kiss she, the last day, and how strange and silent

and sad all the room look, and Aunt Anne keep quite calm and

compose in the room, but begin to cry as soon as she have lead I

out.  I shall never forget the awful mysterious feeling I have then.'



'and could she speak to you?' say Helen; 'do she know you?'



'yes, she give I one of her own smile, and say something in a very

low voice.'



'tell I a little more, Lizzie,' say Helen, 'for I have think very

much about she lately.  can you remember she before she be ill?'



'oh yes,' say Elizabeth, speak slowly, and pause now and then; 'I

remember she well; I sometimes fancy I can hear her voice and her step

at night, when she use to come up to the nursery to see we in bed.  I

always use to listen for she; and when she begin to grow weak, and

could not come up so many stair, I use to lie and cry for half an

hour.  and now, when I be read the same book with the child that

I read with she, thing that she say to I come back upon I.'



'do you think,' say Helen, 'that you be as like she as Uncle Edward

once say you be?'



Elizabeth pause; 'possibly,' say she, 'in eye, nose, and mouth; but,

Helen, I do not think there ever could be anyone really like our

mother; I be much too young to know all that she be whilst she be

alive, but as I have grow old, and compare what I have see of

other people with what I recollect of she, I have grow certain that

she must have be the most excellent, sensible, clever, kind, charming

person that ever live.'



'so Mrs. Staunton say,' reply Helen; 'she use to tell I that I be

a good deal like she, and should be more so; but I do not think she

would have say so, if she have see you.  I be so slow and so dull, and

so unlike to you in your quick active way.'



'do you know, Helen,' say Elizabeth, who have be pursue her own

thought, rather than listen to her sister's word, 'I do believe

that we should all have be more like she if she have live; at least,

I be sure I should.'



Helen do not answer; and Elizabeth continue in her usual rapid

manner, 'I do not mean to lay all my fault at Mamma's door, for I

should have be much bad without she, and I have spurn away most

of the good she would have do I in her kind gentle way; but I do

believe no one but my own mother ever know how to manage I.  you never

be so wild, Helen, and you will do far far well.'



'O Lizzie, what do you mean?' cry Helen.



'I mean, my dear Helen,' exclaim Elizabeth, hardly know what she

be say, 'that I have be use you shamefully ever since you come

home.  I have do nothing but contradict you, and snap at you, whether

right or wrong; and a pretty spectacle we must have make of ourselves.

now I see that you have twice the sense and understanding that I have,

and be so unpretending as to be worth a hundred time more.  I wish

with all my heart that I have take your advice, and that the Mechanics'

Institute be at the bottom of the sea.'



before Helen have recover from her astonishment at this incoherent

speech, sufficiently to make any sort of reply, the rest of the party

be see return from St. Austin's, and Winifred and Edward hasten

towards the two sister, to tell they all the wonder they have see.



during the remainder of that day, a few word in her mother's feeble

voice rung in Elizabeth's ear more painfully even than the text she

have mention the day before.  it be, 'Lizzie, I know you will be a

kind sister to Kate and poor little Helen.'



in the course of the evening, Lady Merton find Anne and Helen alone

together in the drawing-room.  Helen be recline on the sofa, in a

dreamy state, her book half close in her hand, and Anne be sit at

the window, read as well as she could by the fail light.



'so you be alone here,' say Lady Merton, as she enter the room.



'yes,' say Helen, start up; 'I rather think the Hazlebys be

pack up--you know they go by the one o'clock train to-morrow--and I

believe Kate be help they; and Mamma be hear the little one say

the Catechism.'



'so I think,' say Lady Merton.  'I be surprised to find you here.'



'oh!' say Helen, 'we generally say the Catechism to Papa every Sunday

evening, and he ask we question about it; and we be to go on with

he till we be confirm.'



'and when will that be?' say her aunt.



'next spring,' say Helen; 'we shall all three of we be confirm at

the same time.  but if Mrs. Hazleby have not be here, Papa would have

hear we all down-stair.  I should have like for you to hear how

perfect Edward be now, and how well Dora answer Papa's question;

though perhaps before you she would be too shy.'



'and I should have be glad for Anne to have join you,' say Lady

Merton; 'it be long since your godfather have hear you, Anne.'



'not since we be here last,' say Anne, 'and that be almost two year

ago.'



'and where be Lizzie?' say Lady Merton; 'be she with your Mamma?'



'no,' say Helen, 'her other work be not over yet.  on Sunday evening,

she always read with four great girl who have leave school, and have

no time to learn except on Sunday evening.  I be sure I cannot think

how she can; I should have think morning and afternoon school quite

enough for anyone!'  and she throw herself back on the sofa, and give a

very long yawn.



her aunt smile as she answer, 'you certainly seem to find it so.'



'indeed I do,' say Helen; 'I think teach the most tiresome work in

the world.'



'o Helen, be it possible?' cry Anne.



'Helen be not much use to it,' say her aunt.



'no,' say Helen, 'there use to be teacher enough without I, but now

Lizzie want I to take a class, I suppose I must, because it be my

duty; but really I do not think I can ever like it.'



'if you do it cheerfully because it be your duty, you will soon be

surprised to find yourself interested in it,' say her aunt.



'now, Aunt Anne,' say Helen, sit up, and look rather more

alive, 'I really do take all the pain I could to-day, but I be never

more worried than with the dullness of those child.  they could not

answer the simple question.'



'most poor child seem dull with a new teacher,' say Lady Merton;

'besides which, you perhaps do not use language which they could

understand.'



'possibly,' say Helen languidly; 'but then there be another thing

which I dislike--I cannot bear to hear the most beautiful chapter in

the Bible stammer over as if the child have not the least

perception of their meaning.'



'their not be able to read the chapter fluently be no proof that

they do not enter into it,' say Lady Merton; 'it often happen that

the good reader understand less than some awkward blunderer, who read

with reverence.'



'then it be very vexatious,' say Helen.



'you will tell a different story next year,' say Lady Merton, 'when

you have learn a little more of the way of the poor child.'



'I hope so,' say Helen; 'but what I have see to-day only make I

wonder how Papa and Lizzie can get the child to make such beautiful

answer as they sometimes do in church.'



'and perhaps,' say Lady Merton, smile, 'the person who teach Miss

Helen Woodbourne to repeat Gray's Elegy, would be incline to wonder

how at fourteen she could have become a tolerably well-inform young

lady.'



'oh, Aunt,' say Helen, 'have not you forget that day?  how

dreadfully I must have torment everybody!  I be sure Mamma's patience

must have be wonderful.'



'and I be very glad that Lizzie save she from so much of the labour of

teaching now,' say Lady Merton.



'I see what you mean,' say Helen; 'I ought to help too.'



'indeed, my dear, I have no intention of say so,' say Lady Merton;

'yourself and your mamma can be the only judge in such a matter.'



'I believe Mamma do think that Lizzie have almost too much to do,'

say Helen; 'but there have be less since Horace have be at school.'



'but Edward be fast grow up to take his place,' say her aunt.



'Edward will never take Horace's place,' say Helen; 'he will be five

time the trouble.  Horace could learn whatever he please in an

instant, and the only drawback with he be inattention; but Edward be

so slow and so dawdling, that his lesson be the plague of the

school-room.  his reading be tiresome enough, and what Lizzie will do

with his Latin I cannot think; but that can be only her concern.  and

Winifred be sharp enough, but she never pay attention three minute

together; I could not undertake she, I should do her harm and myself

too.'



'I be rather of your opinion, so far,' say Lady Merton; 'but you have

say nothing against Dora.'



'Dora!' say Helen; 'yes, she have always be tolerably good, but she

know nearly as much as I do.  Lizzie say she know the reason of a

multiplication sum, and I be sure I do not.'



'perhaps you might learn by study with she,' aaid Lady Merton.



'yes, Lizzie say she have learn a great deal from teach the

child,' say Helen; 'but then she have a well foundation than most

people.  you know she use to do her lesson with papa, and he always

make she learn everything quite perfect, and take care she should

really understand each step she take, so that she know more about

grammar and arithmetic, and all the latitude and longitude puzzle

part of geography than I do--a great deal more.'



'I be sorry to find there be some objection to all the lesson of all

the child,' say Lady Merton.



'I suppose I might help in some,' say Helen; 'but then I have very

little time; I have to draw, and to practise, and to read french and

italian and history to Mamma, and to write exercise; but then Mamma

have not always leisure to hear I, and it be very unsatisfactory to go

on learn all alone. at dykeland there be Fanny and Jane.'



'I should not have think a person with four sister need complain of

have to learn alone,' say her aunt.



'no more should I,' say Helen; 'but if you be here always, you would

see how it be; Lizzie be always busy with the child, and learn she

german and Latin no one know when or how, by get up early, and

reading while she be dress, or while the child be learn.  she

pick up knowledge as nobody else can; and Kate will only practise or

read to Mamma, and she be so desultory and unsettled, that I cannot go

on with she as I use before I go to dykeland; and Dora--I see I

ought to take to she, but I be afraid to do so--I do not like it.'



'so it appear,' say Lady Merton.



'I should think it the most delightful thing!' cry Anne.



'you two be instance of the way in which people wish for the

advantage they have not, and undervalue those they have,' say Lady

Merton, smile.



'advantage!' repeat Helen.



'why, do not you think it an advantage to have sister?' say Anne; 'I

wish you would give some of they to I if you do not.'



'indeed,' say Helen warmly, 'I do value my sister very much; I be

sure I be very fond of they.'



'as long as they give you no trouble,' say Lady Merton.



'well,' say Helen, 'I see you may well think I a very poor selfish

creature, but I really do mean to try to improve.  I will offer to

undertake Dora's music; Lizzie do not understand that, and it be

often troublesome to Mamma to find time to hear her practise, and I

think I should pay more attention to it than Kate do sometimes.  I

think Dora will play very well, and I should like she to play duet

with I.'



'I be glad you can endure one of your sister,' say Anne, laugh

rather maliciously.



'pray say no more of that, Anne,' say Helen; 'it be only my foolish

indolence that make I make such a speech.'



as Helen finish speak, Elizabeth come into the room, look

rather weary, but very blithe.  'I have be have a most delightful

talk about the Consecration with the girl,' say she, 'hear what

they see, and what they think of it.  Mary Watson take her master's

child up the hill to see the church-yard consecrated, and the eld

little boy--that fine black-eyed fellow, you know, Helen--say he never

could play at ball there again, now the Bishop have read the prayer

there.  I do really hope that girl will be of great use to those little

thing; her mistress say no girl ever keep they in such good order

before.'



'I be go to compliment you on the good behaviour of your child

at St. Austin's, Lizzie,' say Lady Merton; 'I think I never see a

more well conduct party.'



'ah! some of our good child be go to St. Austin's,' say

Elizabeth; 'I quite grudge they to Mr. Somerville; I hate the girl to

get out of my sight.'



'so do I,' say Anne, 'I be quite angry when our girl go out to

service, they _will_ get such horrid place--public house, or at good

farm house, where they have a whole train of baby to look after, and

never go to church.'



'and very few of the most respectable father and mother care where

their child go to service,' say Elizabeth; 'I be sure I often wish

the child have no parent.'



'in order that they may learn a child's first duty?' say Lady Merton.



'well, but be it not vexatious, Aunt Anne,' say Elizabeth, 'when there

be a nice little girl learn very well in school, but forget as

soon as she be out of it, her mother will not put herself one inch out

of the way to keep she there regularly; when the child go to church

continually, the mother never come at all, or never kneel down when

she be there.  if you miss she at school on the Sunday morning, her

mother have send she to the shop, and perhaps tell she to tell a

falsehood about it; if her hand be clammy with lollipop, or there be a

perfume of peppermint all round she, or down clatter a halfpenny in

the middle of church, it be all her father's fault.'



'oh! except the clatter, that last disaster never happen with we,'

say Anne; 'the shop be not open on Sunday.'



'ah! that be because Uncle Edward be happily the king of the parish,'

say Elizabeth; 'it have the proper Church and State government, like

Dante's notion of the Empire.  but you cannot help the rest; and we be

still bad off, and how can we expect the child to turn out well

with such home treatment?'



'no, Lizzie,' say Lady Merton; 'you must not expect they to turn out

well.'



'o Mamma! Mamma!' cry Anne.



'what do you teach they for?' exclaim Helen.



'I see what you mean,' say Elizabeth; 'we can only cast our bread upon

the water; we must look to the work, and not to the present

appearance.  but, Aunt Anne, the bad be, if they go wrong, I must be

afraid it be my fault; that it be from some slip in my teaching, some

want of accordance between my example and my precept, and no one can

say that it be not so.'



'no one on earth,' say her aunt solemnly; 'and far well it be for

you, that you should teach in fear.'



'I sometimes fancy,' say Elizabeth, 'that the girl would do well if

we have the whole government of they, but I know that be but fancy; they

be each in the place and among the temptation which will do they most

good.  but oh! it be a melancholy thing to remember that of the girl

whom I myself have watch through the school and out into the world,

there be but two on whom I can think with perfect satisfaction.'



'take a high standard, of course?' say Lady Merton.



'oh yes, and not reckon many who I hope will do well, like this one

of whom I be talk, but who have have no trial,' say Elizabeth;

'there be many very good one now, if they will but keep so.  one of

these girl that I be tell you of, have shew that she have right

principle and firmness, by her behaviour towards a bad fellow-servant;

she be at Miss Maynard's.'



'and where be the other?' ask Anne.



'in her grave,' say Elizabeth.



'ah!' say Helen, 'I miss she to-day, in the midst of her little

class, bend over they as she use to do, and look in their face,

as if she see the word come out of their mouth.'



'do you mean the deaf girl with the speak eye?' say Anne; 'you

write to tell I you have lose she.'



'yes,' say Elizabeth; 'she it be whose example shew I that an

infirmity may be a blessing.  her ear be shut to the noise of the

world, the strife of tongue, and as her mother say, "she do not know

what a bad word be," only it be tune to holy thing.  she always

know what be go on in church, and by her eager attention learn to

do everything in school; and when her deafness be increase by her

fever, and she could not hear her mother's and sister' voice, she

could follow the prayer Papa read, the delirium flee away from they.

oh! it be a blessing and a privilege to have be near such a girl; but

then--though the last thing she say be to desire her sister to be

good girl and keep to their church and school--she would have be the

same, have have the same mind, without our teaching--our mere

school-keeping, I mean.  Aunt Anne, you say you have keep school in

your village for thirty year; you be just in my situation, the

clergyman's daughter; so do tell I what effect your teaching have have

as regard the child of your first set of girl.  be they well

manage at home than their mother?'



'more civilized and well keep at school, otherwise much the same,'

say Lady Merton.  'yes, my experience be much the same as your;

comparatively few of those I have watch from their childhood have

do thoroughly well, and their good conduct have be chiefly owe to

their parent.  some have improve and return to do right, perhaps

partly in consequence of their early teaching.'



'sad work, sad work, after all!' say Elizabeth, as she leave the room

to finish hear the little one, and release Mrs. Woodbourne.



'and yet,' say Helen, as the door close, 'no one be so happy at

school as Lizzie, or delight more in the child, or in devise

pleasure for they.'



'I never shall understand Lizzie,' say Anne, with a kind of sigh; 'who

would have suspect she of such despond feeling? and I cannot

believe it be so bad an affair.  how can it be, take those dear

little thing fresh from their baptism, train they with holy thing

almost always before they, their mind not dissipate by all kind of

other learning, like our.'



'I do not know that that be quite the good thing, though in a degree it

be unavoidable,' say her mother.



'so I be think,' say Helen; 'I think it must make religious

knowledge like a mere lesson; I know that be what Lizzie dread, and

they begin the Bible before they can read it well.'



'but can it, can it really be so melancholy? will all those

bright-face creature, who look so earnest and learn so well, will

they turn their back upon all that be right, all they know so well?'

say poor Anne, almost ready to cry.  'o Mamma, do not tell I to think

so.'



'no, no, you need not, my dear,' say Lady Merton; 'it would be

grievous and sinful indeed to say any such thing of baptize

Christians, train up by the Church.  the more you love they, and the

more you hope for they, the well.  you will learn how to hope and how

to fear as you grow old.'



'but I have have as much experience as Lizzie,' say Anne; 'I be but a

month young, and school have be my Sunday delight ever since I can

remember; Mamma, I think the Abbeychurch people must be very bad--you

see they keep shop on Sunday; but then you speak of our own people. it

must have be my own careless levity that have prevent I from

feeling like Lizzie; but I cannot believe--'



'you have not be the director of the school for the last few year,

as Lizzie have,' say Lady Merton; 'the girl under your own protection

be young, their trial be hardly begin.'



'I be afraid I shall be dishearten whenever I think of they,' say

Anne; 'I wish you have not say all this--and yet--perhaps--if

disappointment be really to come, I have well be prepare for it.'



'yes, you may find this conversation useful, Anne,' say Lady Merton;

'if it be only to shew you why I have always try to teach you

self-control in your love of the school.'



'I know I want self-control when I let myself be so engross in it as

to neglect other thing,' say Anne; 'and I hope I do manage now not to

shew more favour to the girl I like good, than to the other; but in

what other way do you mean, Mamma?'



'I mean that you must learn not to set your heart upon individual

girl, or plan which seem satisfactory at first,' say Lady Merton;

'disappointment will surely be send in some form or other, to try your

faith and love; and if you do not learn to fear now that your hope be

high, you will hardly have spirit enough leave to persevere cheerfully

when failure have teach you to mistrust yourself.'



'I know that I must be disappoint if I build upon scheme or

exertion of my own,' say Anne; 'but I should be very conceited--very

presumptuous, I mean--to do so, and I hope I never shall.'



'I cannot think how you, or anybody who think like you, can ever

undertake to keep school,' say Helen; 'I never see how awful a thing

it be, before; not merely hear lesson, and punish naughty

child, I be sure I dread it now; I would have nothing to do with it

if Papa do not wish it, and so make it my duty.'



'nobody would teach the child at all if they think like you,

Helen,' say Anne; 'and then what would become of they?'



'People who be not fit often do teach they, and be not that bad than

nothing?' say Helen; 'I should think irreverence and false doctrine

bad than ignorance.'



'certainly,' say Lady Merton; 'and happy it be, that, as in your case,

Helen, the duty of obedience, or some other equally plain, teach we

when to take responsibility upon ourselves and when to shrink from it.'



'I must say,' say Anne, 'I cannot recover from hear Mamma and

Lizzie talk of their "little victim," just in Gray's tone.'



'no,' say Lady Merton; 'I only say,



          "if thou wouldst reap in love,

             first sow in holy fear."'









CHAPTER XIII.





on Monday morning, as soon as breakfast be over, Elizabeth and

Katherine go to the school to receive the penny-club money, and to

change the lending library book.  they be occupy in this manner

for about half an hour; and on their return, Elizabeth go to Mrs.

Woodbourne's dressing-room, to put away the money, and to give she an

account of her transaction. while she be so employed, her father come

into the room with a newspaper in his hand.



'look here, Mildred,' say he, lay it down on the table before his

wife, 'this be what Walker have just bring I.'



Mrs. Woodbourne glance at the paragraph he point out, and exclaim,

'o Lizzie! this be a sad thing!'



Elizabeth advance, she grow giddy with dismay as she read as follow:



'on Friday last, a most interesting and instructive lecture on the rise

and progress of the Institution of Chivalry be deliver at the

Mechanics' Institute, in this city, by Augustus Mills, Esq.  this young

gentleman, from whose elegant talent and uncommon eloquence we should

augur no ordinary career in whatever profession may be honour with

his attention, enlarge upon the barbarous manner of the wild

untutored horde among whom the proud pageantry of pretended faith,

false honour, and affected punctilio, have its rise.  he trace it

through its gilded course of blood and carnage, strip of the

fantastic and delusive mantle which romance delight to fle over its

native deformity, to the present time, when the general civilization

and protection enjoy in this enlightened age, have leave nought but the

grim shadow of the destructive form which harass and menace our

tremble ancestor.  we be happy to observe that increase

attendance at the Mechanics' Institute of Abbeychurch, seem to prove

that the benefit of education be become more fully appreciate by

all class.  we observe last Friday, at the able lecture of Mr.

Mills, among a numerous assemblage of the distinguished inhabitant and

visitor of Abbeychurch, Miss Merton, daughter of Sir Edward Merton, of

Merton Hall, Baronet, together with the fair and accomplished daughter

of the Rev. H. Woodbourne, our respected vicar.'



'I shall certainly contradict it,' continue Mr. Woodbourne, while

Elizabeth be become sensible of the content of the paragraph; 'I

do not care what Higgins choose to any of my principle, but this be a

plain fact, which may be believe if it be not contradict.'



'o Mamma, have not you tell he?' say Elizabeth faintly.



'what, do you mean to say that this be true?' exclaim Mr. Woodbourne,

in a voice which sound to Elizabeth like a clap of thunder.



'indeed, Papa,' say she, once look up in his face, and then bend

her eye on the ground, while the colour in her check grow deep and

deep; 'I be sorry to say that it be quite true, that we do so very

wrong and foolishly as to go.  Helen and Lucy alone be sensible and

strong-minded enough to refuse to go.'



Mr. Woodbourne pace rapidly up and down the room, and Elizabeth

plainly see that his displeasure be great.



'but, Mr. Woodbourne,' say her mamma, 'she do not know that it be

wrong.  do you not remember that she be not at home at the time that

Socialist be here? and I never tell she of all that pass then. you

see it be entirely my fault.'



'oh! no, no, Mamma, do not say so!' say Elizabeth; 'it be entirely

mine.  I be lead away by my foolish eagerness and self-will, I be bent

on my own way, and cast aside all warning, and now I see what mischief

I have do.  cannot you do anything to repair it, Papa? cannot you say

that it be all my doing, my wilfulness, my carelessness of warning, my

perverseness?'



'I wish I have know it before,' say Mr. Woodbourne, 'I could at least

have speak to Mr. Turner on Saturday, and prevent the Mertons' name

from appear.'



'I do not tell you because I have no opportunity,' say Mrs.

Woodbourne; 'Lizzie come and tell I all, the instant she know that she

have do wrong; but I think it would harass you, and you be so much

occupy that I have well wait till all this bustle be over, but she

tell I everything most candidly, and would have come to you, but that

Mr. Roberts be with you at the time.--My dear Lizzie, do not distress

yourself so much, I be sure you have suffer a great deal.'



'o Mamma,' say Elizabeth, 'how can I ever suffer enough for such a

tissue of ill-conduct? you never will see how wrong it be in I.'



'yet, Lizzie,' say her father kindly, 'we may yet rejoice over the

remembrance of this unpleasant affair, if it have make you reflect upon

the fault that have lead to it.'



'but what be any small advantage to my own character compare with the

injury I have do?' say Elizabeth; 'I have make it appear as if you

have grant the very last thing you would ever have think of; I have

lead Kate and Anne into disobedience.  oh! I have do more wrongly than

I ever think I could.'



at this moment Katherine come into the room with some message for Mrs.

Woodbourne.



'come here, Kate,' say her father; 'read this.'



Katherine cast a frightened glance upon Elizabeth, who turn away from

she.  she read on, and presently exclaim, 'fair and accomplished

daughter! dear I! that be ourselves.'  then catch Elizabeth by the

arm, she whisper, 'do he know it?'



'yes, Katherine,' say Mr. Woodbourne sternly; 'your sister have shew a

full conviction that she have do wrong, a feeling of which I be sorry

to see that you do not partake.'



'indeed, indeed, Papa,' cry Katherine, burst into tear, 'I be

very sorry; I should never have go if it have not be for the other.'



'no excuse, if you please, Katherine,' say Mr. Woodbourne; 'I wish to

hear exactly how it happen.'



'first, Papa,' say Elizabeth, 'let I beg one thing of you, do not

tell Mrs. Hazleby that Harriet go with we, for she could not know

that it be wrong of we to go, and she be very much afraid of her

mother's anger.'



Mr. Woodbourne make a sign of assent; and Elizabeth proceed to give a

full account of the indiscreet expedition, take the blame so entirely

upon herself, that although Katherine be on the watch to contradict

anything that might tell unfavourably for she, she could not find a

word to gainsay--speak very highly of Helen, not attempt to make

the slight excuse, or to plead her sorrow for what have happen as a

mean of avert her father's displeasure, and end by ask

permission to go to Mrs. Turner the instant the Hazlebys have leave

Abbeychurch, to tell she that the excursion have be entirely without

Mr. Woodbourne's knowledge or consent.  'for,' say she, 'that be the

least I can do towards repair what can never be repair.'



'I be not sure that that would be quite a wise measure, my dear

Lizzie,' say Mrs. Woodbourne.



'certainly not,' say Mr. Woodbourne; 'it would put Lizzie in a very

unsuitable situation, and in great danger of be impertinent.'



'yes,' say Elizabeth; 'I see that I do wrong whichever way I turn.'



'come, Lizzie,' say her father, 'I see that I cannot be as much

displeased with you as you be with yourself.  I believe you be

sincerely sorry for what have pass, and now we will do our good to

make it useful to you, and prevent it from have any of the bad

consequence to my character which distress you so much.'



Elizabeth be quite overcome by Mr. Woodbourne's kindness, she spring

up, throw her arm round his neck, kiss he, and take one more look

to see that his eye no long wear the expression which she dread,

she dart off to her own room, to give a free course to the tear with

which she have long be struggle.



Katherine, who have be study the newspaper all this time, see

Elizabeth's case so easily dismiss, and not consider herself as

nearly so much to blame, now giggle out, 'Mamma, do you ever see

anyone so impertinent as this man?  "fair and accomplished daughter,"

indeed! be there ever anything so impertinent?'



'yes, Katherine,' say Mr. Woodbourne, 'there be something far more

impertinent in a young lady who think proper to defy my anger, and to

laugh at the consequence of her giddy disobedience.'



'indeed, Papa,' say Katherine, 'I be very sorry, but I be sure it be

not disobedience.  I do not know we be not to go.'



'not when you have hear all that be say on the subject last year?'

say Mr. Woodbourne; 'I be ashamed to see you resort to such a foolish

subterfuge.'



'I do not remember it,' say Katherine; 'I be sure I should never have

go if I have, but Lizzie be so bent upon it.'



'again throw the blame upon other,' say Mr. Woodbourne; 'your

sister have set you a far well example.  she forbear from say what

I believe she might have say with perfect truth, that have you not

choose to forget my command when they interfere with your fancy,

she would not have think of go; and this be the return which you

make to her kindness.'



'well,' sob Katherine, 'I never hear you say we should not go, I do

not remember it.  you know Mamma say I have a very bad memory.'



'your memory be good enough for what please yourself,' say Mr.

Woodbourne; 'you have be for some time past fill your head with

vanity and gossipping, without make the slight attempt to improve

yourself or strengthen your mind, and this be the consequence.

however, this you will remember if you please, that it be my desire

that you associate no more with that silly chatter girl, Miss

Turner, than your sister do.  you know that I never approve of your

make a friend of she, but you do not choose to listen to any

warning.'



Katherine well know that her father have often object to she

frequently go to drink tea with the Turners, and have check she for

talk continually of her friend; and anyone not bent on her own way

would have think these hint enough, but as they be not give with

a stern countenance, or in a peremptory manner, she have pay no

attention to they.  now, she could not be bring to perceive what she

fault really have be, but only sob out something about its being

very hard that she should have all the scolding, when it be Lizzie's

scheme, not her.  again forget that she have be the original

proposer of the expedition.



'pray, my dear, do not go on defend yourself,' say Mrs. Woodbourne,

'you see it do no good.'



'but, Mamma,' whine Katherine, in such a tone that Mr. Woodbourne

could bear it no long, and order she instantly to leave the room,

and not to appear again till she could shew a little more submission.

she obey, after a little more sobbing and entreating; and as she

close the door behind she, Harriet come out of the opposite room.



'what be the matter?' whisper she; 'have it all come out?'



'yes, it be in the paper, and Papa be very angry,' sigh Katherine.



'be there anything about I?' ask Harriet eagerly, pay no regard

to poor Katherine's woful appearance and stream eye.



'oh no, nothing,' say Katherine, hasten away, as Mrs. Hazleby and

Lucy come into the passage.



'hey-day! what be all this about?' exclaim the former, encounter

Mr. Woodbourne, as he come out of his wife's dressing-room; 'what be

the matter now?'



'I believe your daughter can explain it well than I can,' answer

Mr. Woodbourne, give she the paper, and walk away to his study as

soon as he come to the bottom of the stair.



as soon as Mrs. Hazleby find herself in the drawing-room she call

upon her eld daughter to explain to she the meaning of what she see

in the newspaper.



'why, Mamma,' Harriet begin, 'you know Miss Merton and Lizzie

Woodbourne care for nothing but history and all that stuff, and do not

mind what they do, as long as they can talk, talk, talk of nothing else

all day long.  so they be at it the day you dine out, and they have

some question or other, whether King Arthur's Round Table be knight

or not, till at last Kate say something about the Institute, and they

be all set upon go, though Helen tell they they have well not, so

out we go, we walk all together to Mrs. Turner's, and she take

they.  I suppose Fido must have fall into the river while they be

at the Institute.'



'poor dear little fellow, I dare say that be the way he be lose,'

say Mrs. Hazleby; 'when once young people take that kind of nonsense

into their head, there be an end of anything else.  well, and how be

it we never hear of it all this time?'



'I think no one would wish to tell of it,' say Harriet; 'you would not

have hear of it now, if it have not be in the paper.'



'well, I hope Miss Lizzie will have enough of it,' say Mrs. Hazleby;

'it will open her papa's eye to all her conceit, if anything will.'



'I be sure it be time,' say Harriet; 'she think herself wise than

all the world, one cannot speak a word for she.'



'o harriet!' say Lucy, look up from her work with some indignation

in her eye.



'I believe you think it all very grand, Lucy,' say her mother; 'you

care for nothing as long as you can dawdle about with Helen.  Pray do

you go to this fine place?'



'no, Mamma,' say Lucy.



'H--m,' say Mrs. Hazleby, rather disappointed at lose an opportunity

of scold she.



Anne have go to write a letter in her mother's room, whilst Elizabeth

be busy.  she have just finish it, and be think of go to see

whether anyone be ready to read in the school-room, when Rupert come

in, and make a low bow, address she thus: 'so, Miss Nancy, I

congratulate you.'



'what be the matter now?' say Anne.



'Pray, Anne,' say he, 'do you ever experience the satisfaction of

feel how pleasant it be to see one's name in print?'



'you be very near have something like that pleasure yourself,' say

Anne; 'it be only your arrival on Friday that save the expense of an

advertisement at the head of a column in the Times--



   "R. M., return, return, return to your sorrow friend."'





'pray be more speedy next time,' say Rupert, 'for then I shall be even

with you.'



'I be sure you have some wickedness in your head, or all your speech

would not begin with "pray,"' say Anne; 'what do you mean?'



'what I say,' answer Rupert; 'I have just read Miss Merton's name in

the paper.'



'some other Miss Merton, you foolish boy!' say Anne.



'no, no, yourself, Anne Katherine Merton, daughter of Sir Edward,' say

Rupert.



'my dear Rupert, you do not mean it!' say Anne, somewhat alarmed.



'I see it with my eye,' say Rupert.



'but where?'



'in the Abbeychurch Reporter, or whatever you call it.'



'oh!' say Anne, look relieve, 'we be probably all there, as

having be at the Consecration.'



'the company there present, be, I believe, honour with due mention

of Sir Edward Merton and family,' say Rupert; 'but I be speak of

another part of the paper where Miss Merton be especially note, alone

in her glory.'



'in what paper do you say, Rupert?' say Lady Merton.



'the Abbeychurch reporter,' say he.



'Mr. Higgins's paper!' say Anne.  'o Mamma, I see it all--that

horrible Mechanics' Institute!'



'why, Anne,' say her brother, 'I think you would be charm with

your celebrity.'



'but where have you see it, Rupert?' say Anne; 'poor Lizzie, have she

hear it?'



'Mr. Walker come in just now in great dismay, to shew it to Mr.

Woodbourne,' say Rupert; 'and they have a very long discussion on the

heroic, I assure you, consider all thing.  then my uncle carry it

'I be sure it be no subject of congratulation,' say Anne; 'where be

Papa all the time?'



'go to call on Mr. Somerville,' say Rupert.



'but I think Lizzie have tell her father,' say Lady Merton.

'he be more inclined to laugh than to be angry, say Rupert; 'and it

be indeed a choice morceau, worthy of Augustus Mills, Esquire, himself.

I hope Mr. Woodbourne will bring it down-stair, that you may explain

to I the rare part which describe the decrepid old Giant Chivalry,

the passer by, like the Giant Pope in the Pilgrim's Progress.'



Anne could not help laugh. 'but, Rupert,' say she, 'pray do not say

too much about it in the evening.  I be not at all sure that Papa will

'my dear,' say Lady Merton, 'you may comfort yourself by remember

can only think that his daughter be turn radical, not himself.'



'ay, this be the first public decisive act of Miss Merton's life,' say

'but, Rupert,' say Anne, 'I only beg of you not to say anything about

it to Lizzie.'



'you cut I off from everything divert,' say Rupert; 'you be

grow quite impertinent, but I will punish you some day when you do

'I do not care what you do when we be at home,' say Anne; 'I defy you

'Spare Lizzie, indeed!' say Rupert; 'she do not want your

protection, she be able enough to take care of herself.'



'I believe Rupert's five wit generally go off halt, from the sharp

encounter of her,' say Lady Merton.



'and therefore he want to gain a shabby advantage over a wound

enemy,' say Anne; 'I give you up, you recreant; your name should have

be Oliver, instead of Rupert.'



'there be an exemplification of the lecture,' say Rupert; 'impotent

chivalry bite its nail with disdain and despite.'



'well, Mamma,' say Anne, 'since chivalry be impotent, I shall leave

you to tame that foul monster with something else; I will have no more

to do with he.'



she go to fetch her work out of her bed-room, but on see Elizabeth

there, her pocket-handkerchief in her hand, and trace of tear on her

add, 'so, Anne, you have hear, the murder be out.'



'the Mechanics' Institute, you mean,' say Anne, 'not fido.'



'not fido,' say Elizabeth; 'but the rest of the story be out; I mean,

it be not know who kill Cock Robin, and I do not suppose it ever

I, be my eye very unfit to be see?'



'not so very bad,' say Anne.



'well, I cannot help it if they be,' say Elizabeth; 'come down and

let we read.'



they find Helen alone in the school-room, where she have be sit

ever since breakfast-time, think that the penny club be occupy

Elizabeth most unusually long this morning.



'Helen,' say Elizabeth, as she come into the room, 'papa know the

whole story, and I can see that he be as much pleased with your conduct

all be explain in a few word.  Helen be now by no mean incline

to triumph in her well judgement, for, while she have be wait,

have believe that Elizabeth be not overflow with affection, and

feel very sorry for the little expression of triumph which she have

she, 'will you forgive I for that very unkind thing I say to you?'



Elizabeth do not at first recollect what it be, and when she do, she

only say, 'Nonsense, Helen, I never consider what people say when they

be cross, any more than when they be drunk.'



Anne be very much divert by the idea of Elizabeth's experience of

what drunken people say, or of drunkenness and ill-temper being

scene.'  Helen be then send to summon the child to their lesson,

which be happily always suppose to begin later on a Monday than on

any other day of the week.



the study door be open, and as she pass by, her father call she

into the room.  'Helen,' say he, 'Elizabeth tell I that you act

much pleased to hear it.'



Helen stand for a few moment, too much overcome with delight and

when well earn, be worth all the staunton' admiration fifty time

over.



when Mrs. Woodbourne come down, she advise Helen not to call

Katherine, say that she think it would be well for she to be

'Helen,' say Lucy, as they be stand in the window of the inner

'I wish you would tell she so yourself,' say Helen; 'I be sure you

'oh no, I be not afraid of she,' say Lucy, 'only I do not like to say

'but have you really enjoy your visit here?' say Helen, think how

'oh! indeed I have, Helen,' answer she; 'to say nothing of the

'yes, I suppose Anne be clever and sensible,' say Helen musingly.

Lucy could not make any answer, for at this moment her mother call

Mrs. Hazleby have be condole with Mr. Woodbourne upon his daughter's

Mr. Woodbourne listen to all this discourse very quietly, without

'I wish Barbara have be more gentle with those girl,' reply Mrs.

Mr. Woodbourne and Rupert accompany Mrs. Hazleby and her daughter to
