heavy of they, which generally fall to her share.









CHAPTER XIV.





'well,' say Elizabeth, draw a long breath, as she go out to walk

with Anne and Helen, 'there be the even-handed justice of this world.

of the four delinquent of last Friday, there go one with flying

colour, in all the glory of a successful deceit; you, Anne, who, to

say the good of you, act like a very great goose, be consider as

wise as ever; I, who lead you all into the scrape with my eye wilfully

blinded, be only pitied and comfort; poor Kitty, who have less idea of

what she be do than any of we, have have more cry and scold

than anybody else; and Lucy, who behave so well--oh! I cannot bear to

think of she.'



'it be a puzzle indeed,' say Helen; 'I mean as far as regard Harriet

and Lucy.'



'not really, Helen,' say Elizabeth; 'it be only a failure in story

book justice.  Lucy be too noble a creature to be reward in a

story-book fashion; and as for Harriet, impunity like hers be in

reality a great punishment than all the reproof in the world.'



'how could she sit by and listen to all that Papa and Mrs. Hazleby be

say?' say Helen.



'how could she bear the glance of Papa's eye?' say Elizabeth; 'do you

watch it?  I think I never see it look so stern, and yet that

contemptible creature sit under it as contentedly as possible.  oh! it

make I quite sick to watch she.'



be you quite sure that she know whether my uncle be aware of she

share in the matter?' say Anne.



'she must have see it in that glance, or have be the most insensible

creature upon earth,' say Elizabeth.



'ah!' say Anne, 'I have some notion what that eye of your papa's can

be.'



'you, Anne?' say Elizabeth; 'you do not mean that you could ever have

do anything to make he look at you in that way?'



'indeed I have,' say Anne; 'do not you remember?'



'no, indeed,' say Elizabeth.



'however, it be not quite so bad as this,' say Anne.



'but do tell we what it be,' say Elizabeth, 'or I shall think it

something uncommonly shocking.'



'I never speak of it since, because I be too much ashamed,' say Anne;

'and it be very silly of I to do so now.'



'but when be it?' say Elizabeth.



'two year ago,' say Anne, 'when you be all stay at Merton Hall,

just before that nice nursery-maid of your, Susan, marry our man

Evans.'



'yes, I remember,' say Elizabeth; 'but what have that to do with your

crime, whatever it may be?'



'a great deal,' say Anne; 'do not you recollect our hunting all over

the garden one day for Winifred and Dora, and at last our ask old

Ambrose whether he have see they?'



'oh yes, I think I do,' say Elizabeth; 'and he say that he have see

Susan and the child go down the blind walk.  then I say Dora have

talk of see a blackbird's nest there, and he answer, with a most

comical look, 'ah! ha! Miss Woodbourne, I fancy they be two-legged

blackbird as Susan be go to see.''



'why, blackbird have but two leg,' say Helen, look mystify;

'what do he mean'?'



'that be exactly what Kate say,' say Elizabeth; 'but really I think

you be sharp, Helen.  cannot you guess?'



'not in the least,' say Helen.



'that Evans be clip the hedge,' say Anne.



Elizabeth and Anne indulge in a good laugh at Helen, as much as at

Ambrose, and presently Elizabeth say, 'well, but, Anne, where be your

crime?'



'oh! I think you have remember, and would spare I,' say Anne.



'but we have not,' say Elizabeth; 'so now for it.'



'then if I be to tell,' say Anne, 'do not you recollect that I begin

to tell Rupert the story in the middle of dinner, when all the servant

be there?'



'o Anne, I never fancy you such a goose!' say Elizabeth.



'my delinquency make very little impression on you, then,' say Anne;

'I go on very fluently with the story till just as I have pronounce

the word, "two-legged blackbird," I see Uncle Woodbourne's eye upon

I, as he sit just opposite, with all its cold heavy sternness of

expression, and at the same moment I hear a strange suppress snort

behind my chair.'



'poor creature!' say Elizabeth; 'but you certainly deserve it.'



'I be ready to sink under the table,' say Anne; 'I do not dare to

look up to Papa or Mamma, and I have be very much oblige to Mamma

ever since for never allude to that terrible dinner.'



'it be a regular proof that Fun be one of the most runaway horse in

existence,' say Elizabeth; 'very charming when well curb, but if you

give he the rein--'



'yes, I have be learn that by sad experience all my life,' say

Anne, with a sigh.



'you will never be silly enough to give he up, though,' say Elizabeth.



'silly, do you call it?' say Helen.



'People think so differently on those matter,' say Anne.



'yes, but a "spirit full of glee" be what I think the most delightful

thing in the world,' say Elizabeth, 'and so do you.'



'yes, in old age, when its blitheness have be prove to be something

beyond animal spirit,' say Anne.



'and it be right that people should have animal spirit in their

youth,' say Elizabeth, 'not grey head on green shoulder, like some

people of my acquaintance.--Do not be affront, Helen; I dare say your

head will grow greener all your life, it be well to-day than it be

on Saturday morning.'



'but the bad of it be,' say Anne, 'that I believe it be very silly

of I, but I be afraid Uncle Woodbourne have always think I a most

foolish girl ever since, and I do not like the idea of it.'



'who would?' say Elizabeth; 'I be afraid I cannot tell you what he

think of your sense, but of this I be sure, that he must think you the

choice damsel of his acquaintance, and wish his daughter be more

like you.'



'and there could not have be the same meaning in his eye when he

look at you, as when he look at Harriet,' say Helen.



'oh no, I hope not,' say Anne.



'and you understand it a little well than one who can only feel

personal inconvenience,' say Elizabeth; 'but how can I blame Harriet

when I be the occasion of her fault? it be a thing I can never bear to

think of.'



as Elizabeth say this, they come to a shop where Anne wish to buy

some little present for some child in the village at home, who, she

say, would value they all the more for not be the production of the

town near they.  they pursue their search for gay remnant of

coloured print, little shawl, and picture pocket-handkerchief, into

the new town, and pass by Mr. Higgins's shop, the window of which be

adorn with all the bad caricature which have find their way to

Abbeychurch, the portrait of sundry radical leader, emboss within a

halo of steel-pen, and a notice of a lecture on 'Personal

respectability,' to be give on the ensue Friday at the Mechanics'

Institute, by the Rev. W. Pierce, the dissent preacher.



Mr. Higgins appear at the shop door, for the express purpose, as it

seem, of honour Miss Merton and Miss Woodbourne each with a very

low bow.



'there, Helen, be my punishment,' say Elizabeth; 'since you be

desirous of poetical justice upon I.'



'not upon you,' say Helen, 'only upon Harriet.'



'Harriet have lose Fido,' say Elizabeth.



here Rupert come to meet they, and no more be say on the subject.



Rupert obey his sister tolerably well during most of the day, though

he be sorely tempt to ask Elizabeth to send Anne an abstract, in

short-hand, of the lecture on Personal Respectability; but he

refrain, for he be really fond of his cousin, and very good-nature,

except when his vanity be offend.



Anne however be in a continual fright, for he delight in tormenting

she by go as near the dangerous subject as he dare; and often, when

no one else think there be any danger, she know by the expression of

his eye that he have some spiteful allusion on his lip.  besides, he

think some of the speech he have make in the morning too clever to

be waste on his mother and sister, when his cousin be there to hear

they, and Anne could not trust to his forbearance to keep they to

himself all day, so that she keep a strict watch upon he.



in the evening, however, Mr. Woodbourne call she and Helen to play

some Psalm tune from which he want to choose some for the Church. he

speak to she in a way which make her hope that he do not think she

quite foolish, but she would have be glad to stay and keep Rupert in

order.  however, she be rejoice to hear Elizabeth propose to he to

play at chess, and she see they sit down very amicably.



this proposal, however, prove rather unfortunate, for Elizabeth be

victorious in the first battle, the second be a draw game, and Rupert

lose the third, just as he think he be win it, from forget

to move out the castle's pawn after castle his king. he could not

bear to be conquer, and push away the chess-board rather pettishly.



'good morning to you, Prince Rupert,' say Elizabeth triumphantly; 'do

you wish for any more?'



Rupert make no answer, but pull the inkstand across the table, open

the paper-case, and take up a pen.



'oh!' say Elizabeth, 'I suppose we may expect a treatise on the art of

fortification, salient angle, and cover way, not forget the

surrender of Bristol.'



no reply, but Rupert scratch away very diligently with his pen, the

inkstand prevent Elizabeth from see what he be about.



'Anne,' say Elizabeth, lean back, and turn round, 'I be think

of make a collection of the hero who could not bear to be beat at

chess, begin with Charlemagne's Paladins, who regularly beat out

each other's brain with the silver chess-board, then the Black Prince,

and Philippe of Burgundy.  can you help I to any more?'



Anne do not hear, and Rupert remain silent as ever; and Elizabeth,

determine to let he make himself as silly as he please, take up her

work and sew on her braid very composedly.  Katherine have come down

again at dinner-time, and be work in silence.  she have be

stand by the piano, but find that no one ask she to play, or

take any notice of she, she have come back to the table.



'dear I, Prince Rupert,' say she, look over his shoulder, 'what

strange thing be you do there?'



'a slight sketch,' say he, 'to be place in Lizzie's album as a

companion to a certain paragraph which I believe she have study.'



Rupert throw his pen-and-ink drawing down before Elizabeth.  it be

really not badly do, and she see in a moment, by the help of the

name which he have scribble below in his bad of all bad writing,

that it represent the Giants, Pope and Pagan, as describe in the

Pilgrim's progress, while, close to Pope, be place a delineation very

like Don Quixote, purport to be the superannuated Giant Chivalry,

bite his nail at a dapper little personification of 'civil and

Religious Liberty.'  a figure whose pointed head, lame foot, and stout

walk-stick, shew he to be intend for Sir Walter Scott, be

throw over he an embroidered surcoat, which a most striking and

ludicrous likeness of Mr. Augustus Mills be pull off at the other

end; and the scene be embellish by a ruin castle in the distance,

and a quantity of skull and cross-bone in the fore-ground.  Elizabeth

could not but think it unkind of he to jest on this matter, while her

eye-lid be still burn and heavy from the tear it have cause she

to shed; but she know Rupert well enough to be certain that it be only

a sign that he be out of temper, and have not yet conquer his old

boyish love of teazing. she put the paper into her basket, say, in a

low tone, 'thank you, Rupert; I shall keep it as a memorial of several

thing, some of which may do I good; but I fear it will always put I

in mind that cavalier of the present day would have little objection

to such battle as I be speak of, even with woman, if this poor old

gentleman do not retain a small degree of vitality.'



Rupert be vex, both at be set down in a way he do not expect,

and because he be really sorry that his wound self-conceit bad lead

he to do what he see have mortify Elizabeth more than he have intend.



'what be it? what be it?' ask Katherine.



'never mind, Kate,' say Rupert.



'well, but what fun be it?' persist Katherine.



'only downright nonsense,' say Rupert, look down, and unconsciously

draw very strange device on the blot paper, 'unworthy the

attention of so wise a lady.'



'only the dry bone of an ill-natured joke,' say Lady Merton, who have

see all that pass, from the other end of the table.  she speak so

low as only to be hear by her son; but Elizabeth see his colour

deepen, and, as he rise and go to the piano, she feel sorry for he,

and soon find an opportunity of remind he that he have promise to

draw something for Edward's scrap-book, and ask he if he would do so

now.



'willingly,' say Rupert, 'but only on one condition, Lizzie.'



'what?' say Elizabeth.



'that you give I back that foolish thing,' say Rupert, fix his

eye intently on the coach and horse which he be draw.



'there it be,' say Elizabeth, restore it to he.  'no, no, Rupert,

do not tear it up, it be the clever thing you ever draw, Sir Walter

be excellent.'



yet, in spite of this commendation, Rupert have tear his performance

into the small scrap, before his sister come back to the table.



Anne have be in some anxiety ever since the conclusion of the game;

but Sir Edward and Mr. Woodbourne be stand between she and the

table, so that she could neither see nor hear, and when at length she

have finish play, and be release, she find Rupert and Elizabeth

so quiet, and so busy with their several employment, that she greatly

dread that all have not go right.  she bethink herself of the

sketch Rupert have make in Scotland, ask he to fetch they, and by

their help, she contrive to restore the usual tone of conversation

between the cousin, so that the remainder of the evening pass away

very pleasantly.



when Anne and Elizabeth awake the follow morning, Anne say that she

have remember, the evening before, just when it be too late to do

anything, that the last Sunday Rupert have leave his prayer-book behind

he at St. Austin's; and as they be to set off on their journey

homeward immediately after breakfast, she ask Elizabeth whether

there would be time to walk to the new church and fetch it before

breakfast.



'I think it would be a very pleasant walk in the freshness of the

morning, if you like to go,' say she.



'oh yes,' say Elizabeth, 'there be plenty of time, and I should like

the walk very much; but really, Anne, you spoil that idle boy in a

terrible way.'



'ah! Rupert be an only son,' say Anne; 'he have a right to be spoil.'



'then I hope that Horace and Edward will save each other from the same

fate,' say Elizabeth; 'I do not like to see a sister make such a slave

as you have be all your life.'



'wait till Horace and Edward be at home in the holiday before you

talk of slavery,' say Anne; 'there will be five slave and two

master, that will be all the difference.'



'well be the male kind call baron in heraldry,' say Elizabeth;

'there be no deny that they be a lordly race; but I think I would

have send Mr. Rupert up the hill himself, rather than go before

breakfast, with a day's journey before I.'



'suppose he would not go?' say Anne.



'let he lose his Prayer-book, then,' say Elizabeth.



'but if I have rather fetch it for he?' say Anne.



'I can only answer that there be no slave as willing as sister,'

say Elizabeth.



the two cousin have a pleasant morning walk up the hill, enjoy the

freshness of the morning air, and watch the various symptom of

waken in the town.  they carry the key of the church with they,

as no clerk have as yet be appoint, and they be still in Mr.

Woodbourne's possession, so that it be not necessary to call anyone to

open the door for they.



Whilst Anne be search for the Prayer-book, Elizabeth stand in the

aisle, her eye fix on the bright red cross in the centre window over

the Altar.  the sun-beam be light it up gloriously, and from it,

her gaze fall upon the Table of Commandments, between it and the Altar.

presently, Anne come and stand by her side in silence. 'Anne,' say

Elizabeth, after a few minute, 'I will tell you what I have be

think of.  on the day when Horace lay the first stone of this

church, two year ago, something put I, I be sorry to say, into one of

my old fit of ill temper.  it be the last violent passion I ever be

in; I either learn to control they, or outgrow they.  and now, may

this affair at the Consecration be the last of my self-will and

self-conceit; for indeed there be much that be fearfully wrong in I to

be correct, before I can dare to think of the Confirmation.'



perhaps we cannot take leave of Elizabeth Woodbourne at a well

moment, therefore we will say no more of she, or of the other

inhabitant of the vicarage, but make a sudden transition to the

conversation, which Anne have hope to enjoy on the journey back to

Merton Hall.



she have tell her father of nearly all her adventure, have give Fido's

history more fully, inform Rupert of all that he have miss, and be

proceed with an account of Helen.  'really,' say she, 'I have much

more hope of she be happy at home, than I have at first.'



'I will answer for it that she will be happy enough,' say Rupert; 'she

have be live on flummery for the last half-year, and you cannot

expect she to be content with mutton-chop just at first.'



'Helen do not find so much fault with the mutton-chop as with the

pepper Lizzie add to they,' say Anne.



'I should be sorry to live without pepper,' say Rupert.



'I be not so sure of that,' say Lady Merton.



'at least you do not wish to have enough to choke you,' say Anne; 'you

must have it in moderation.'



'I think Lizzie be learn moderation,' say Lady Merton; 'she be

acquire more command of impulse, and Helen more command of feeling,

so that I think there be little danger of their not agree.'



'be it not curious, Mamma,' say Anne, 'that we should have be

talk of the necessity of self-control, just before we set out on

this visit, when I tell you that line of Burns be your motto; and now

we find that the want of it be the reason of all that be wrong between

those two sister.  I wonder whether we could make out that any more of

the folly we see in this visit be cause by the same deficiency in

anyone else.'



'begin at home?' say Sir Edward.



'of course, Papa,' say Anne; 'I know that my failure in self-control

have do mischief, though I cannot tell how much.  I laugh at the

Hazlebys continually, in spite of Mamma's warning, and encourage

Lizzie to talk of they when I have well not have do so; and I

allow myself to be lead away by eagerness to hear that foolish

lecture.  I suppose I want control of spirit.'



'and now having finish our own confession, how merrily we begin upon

our neighbour!' say Rupert; 'whom shall we dissect first?'



'indeed, Rupert,' say Anne, 'I do not want to make the most of their

fault, I only wish to study their character, because I think it be a

useful thing to do.  now I do not see that Kate's fault be occasion

by want of self-control; do you think they be, Mamma?'



'do you think that piece of thistle-down possess any self-control?'

say Rupert.



'you mean that Kate do not control her own conduct at all, but be

drift about by every wind that blow,' say Anne; 'yes, it be Miss

Hazleby's influence that make she talk so much more of dress than

usual, and really seem silly than I ever see she before.'



'and what do you say of the fair Harriet herself?' say Rupert.



'nothing,' say Anne.



'and Mrs. Hazleby be her daughter in a magnifying glass,' say Rupert;

'a glorious speciman of what you all may come to.  and Mrs. Woodbourne?'



'oh! I have nothing to do with the elder,' say Anne; 'but if you want

I to find you a fault in she, I shall say that she ought to control

her unwillingness to correct people.  and now we have discuss almost

everyone.'



'from which discussion,' say Rupert, 'it appear that of all the

company at Abbeychurch, the sole possessor of that most estimable

quality, the root of all other excellency, be--your humble servant.'



on this unfortunate speech of poor Rupert's, father, mother, and

sister, all set up a shout of laughter, which last till Rupert begin

to feel somewhat enraged.



'oh! I do not say that I have do with everybody,' say Anne; 'but,

perhaps, whatever I might think, I might not have presumed--'



'o rupert!' say Lady Merton,



          'could some fay the giftie gie we

           to see oursel as other see us--'





'Mamma's beloved Burn's Justice again,' interrupt Rupert.



'no, no, we do not mean to let our mouth be stop,' say Lady

Merton; 'such a challenge must be answer.'



'shew he no mercy, Anne,' say Sir Edward; 'he like pepper.'



'Pray, Rupert,' say Anne, 'what would you have be without

self-control, if, possess such a quantity of it, you still allow

so much spirit of mischief to domineer over you, that you frighten

Dora out of her wit about winifred, and torment Helen all the way to

Whistlefar, and bad of all, that you could not help write that

wicked poem, and then pretend that it be mine; why, it be an

outrage upon we all, it would have be bad enough if the name have

belong to no one, but when you know that he be a real man--'



'and that Miss Hazleby write his name on purpose that something of the

kind might be do,' say Rupert; 'I gratify she beyond measure, and

then be so kind and disintereste as to give you the credit of it, if

you would have accept it.  you may be sure that she will shew the

poem to her hero, and tell he what a charming fellow that young Rupert

Merton be.'



'now just listen, Mamma,' say Anne; 'I beg of Mr. Rupert not to

write anything about Fido in the Conglomeration on Saturday evening;

and because I do so, he would write nothing on his own account, but

pretend to read my verse, he bring out a horrible composition

about a certain Mr. Francis Hollis, who, Miss Hazelby have be tell

we, have be the mean of she go to an officer' ball, at Hull, and

whom she have dance with--'



'Capital, capital!' cry Rupert; 'I never hear all this; I do not

know how good my poem be, I know the truth by intuition.'



'but having hear this make it all the bad for I,' say Anne; 'and

Mamma, this dreadful doggerel--'



'Anne, I declare--' cry Rupert.



'and, Mamma, this dreadful doggerel,' proceed Anne, 'propose to send

Fido's heart to this Mr. Hollis, and so put he in rapture with a gift

from Miss Hazleby, and fill his mind with vision of a surrogate, and a

wedding tour to Harrogate.  now be it not the most impertinent

ungentlemanlike thing you ever hear of?'



'how can you talk such nonsense, Anne?' say Rupert; 'do you think I

should have write it, if I have not know it would please she?'



'I believe you would not have dare to behave in such a manner to

Lizzie, or to anyone else who know what be due to she,' say Anne; 'if

Miss Hazleby be vain and vulgar, she be still a woman, and ought to be

respect as such.'



Rupert laugh rather provokingly. 'it be just as I say,' say Anne;

'now be it not, Mamma?'



'oh yes, Anne,' say Rupert, 'perfectly right, you have catch Helen's

sententious wisdom exactly; I have no doubt that such be the thought

which pass through her mind, while she sit like propriety

personify, wonder how you could have so little sense of decorum as

to laugh at anything so impudent.'



'I know I ought not to have laugh,' say Anne; 'that be one of the

occasion when I do not exert sufficient self-control.  but there be

really very little to laugh at, it be quite an old joke.  Rupert have

dispose of Fido's heart long before, but he be so fond of his own wit,

that he never know when we have have enough of a joke.'



'I could tell you of something much bad, Anne,' say Lady Merton,

'which quite prove the truth of what you say.'



Rupert colour, make an exclamation about something in the road, and

seem so much discompose by this hint, that Anne forbore to ask any

question.



'Rupert fit himself to a T, that we must say for he,' say Sir

Edward.



'what do you mean, Papa?' say Anne.



'there be another word which begin with self-con--' say Lady Merton,'

which suit he remarkably well.'



'ah! ha!' cry Anne.



'at any rate,' cry Rupert vigorously, 'do not make it appear as if I

be the only individual with a tolerable opinion of my own

advantage--when Helen look like the picture of offend dignity if

you presume to say a syllable contrary to some of her opinion, or in

disparagement of dear dykeland; and Kate think herself the most

lovely creature upon earth, and the only useful person in the house;

and Harriet believe no one she equal in the art of fascination; and

Mrs. Woodbourne think no child come within a mile of her in beauty

and excellence; and Lizzie--'



'I be sure few people be more humble-minded than Lizzie,' interrupt

Anne indignantly.



'what, when she would take no one's advice but her own, if it be to

save her life?' say Rupert.



'but she think everyone well than herself, and make no parade

either of her talent or of her usefulness,' say Anne.



'still she have a pretty high opinion of her own judgement,' say Rupert.



'well she may,' say Anne.



'when it lead she to go to Mechanics' Institutes,' say Rupert; 'that

be the reason Anne respect she so much.'



'I advise you to throw no stone at she, Sir,' say Sir Edward; 'it

would be well if some people of my acquaintance be as upright in

acknowledge deficiency in themselves, as she be.'



'besides, I cannot see that Helen be conceited,' say Anne; 'if she

be, she would not be make unhappy by other people's criticism.'



'Helen want a just estimate of herself,' say Lady Merton; 'she care

more for what people say of what she do, than whether it be good in

itself.'



'but, Anne,' say Sir Edward, 'why do not you claim to be the only

person in the world devoid of conceit?'



'because I be conceited in all the way which Rupert have mention,'

say Anne; 'I believe myself witty, and wise, and amiable, and useful,

and agreeable, and I do not like take advice, and I be very angry

when my friend be abuse, and I do believe I think I have the most

exquisite brother in the world; and besides, if I say I be not

conceite it would be the well possible proof of the contrary.--But,

Mamma, there be a person whom we have not mention, who have no conceit

and plenty of self-control.'



'do you mean little Dora?' say Lady Merton.



'no, not Dora, though I be pretty much of Mrs. Woodbourne's opinion

respect she,' say Anne; 'I mean one who be always overlook, Miss

Lucy Hazleby.'



'she may have every virtue upon earth for aught I know,' say Rupert;

'I can only testify that she have un grand talent pour le silence.'



'I only know she from what my cousin tell I,' say Anne; 'they seem

to have a great respect for she, though Helen be the only person she

ever seem to talk to.  I never could make she speak three word to I.'



'she have a fine countenance and very sweet expression, certainly,' say

Lady Merton.



'poor girl,' say Sir Edward; 'she blush so much, that it be almost

painful to look at she.'



'you seem to be utterly deficient in proof of her excellence,' say

Rupert; 'you will leave she a blank page at last.'



'page be not always blank when you see nothing on they,' say Lady

Merton; 'character may be bring out by the fire.'



'yes, Mamma, the fire of temptation,' say Anne; 'and I have hear Lucy

try by her mother's violence, and she never conceal any part of the

truth as far as only regard herself, even to avoid those terrible

unjust reproof, and put herself forward to bear her sister's share of

blame; and she be firm in turn back from the Mechanics' Institute

when her sister scold she.'



'Firmness, which, in so timid a person, prove that she have more

self-control than any of you,' say Sir Edward.



'then let we wind up the history of our visit in a moral style,' say

Anne, 'and call it a lesson on self-control and Self-conceit.'



'nonsense,' say Rupert; 'do you think that if anyone read its history,

they would learn any such lesson unless you tell they beforehand?'



'perhaps not,' say Sir Edward, 'as you have not learn it from your

whole life.'



'no,' say Lady Merton; 'that lesson be not to be learn by anyone who

be not on the watch for it.'



'so we conclude with Mamma's wisdom,' say Rupert.



'and Rupert's folly,' say Anne.









the END
